THE RED PAGE
A SONG OF PIPES AND TREES.
I know not if the trees of Aready
Had broader leaves,
Or if old suns wove finer ’broidery
Than our sun weaves
On the warm quiet of the dimpled ground.
In Tempo’s vale was laughter, and glad
sound
Of waters free;
I know not if the brown gods laughed as well
On Ossa’s knee
As the brown maids who hear the summer
tell
Tales ’ne'ath this tree.
They say Apollo had a reedy pipe
In Thessaly—
The world was all with wonder music ripe
On land and sea—
He blew his silver breath into the air,
Enchanted naiads with their dripping hair
Hose from the streams . . .
Perchance he would have changed his pipe
for mine
Whose incense seems
To make my couch beneath these boughs
divine
With olden dreams.
My pipe can call through rings of pearly
smoke
The old blue day,
All that Apollo saw beneath his oak
In ages grey,
All that the past upon the present spills
In joy of wonder through these shadows fills
My pipe with sound,
And all things seek at its enchanted call
This quiet ground—
I hold the flute Athene’s hand let fall
And Marsyas found.
DAVID MCKEE WEIGHT.
FRANCIS GRIERSON
To those who are interested in the liter-
ary expression of the reaction towards mys-
ticism the name of Francis Grierson is
known as a writer of original thought and
considerable charm of style. Maeterlinck,
chief of his clan, has praised his essays on
Modem Mysticism and The Celtic Tempera-
ment. The latter certainly deserves to stand
with the lectures of Renan and Arnold, and
the essay of Havelock Ellis as the most il-
luminating treatments of the subject.
Grierson is an American, and has told the
story of his life before the Civil War, with
a large historical background, in The Valley
of Shadoibs, which is in the form of a
superior kind of fiction. He went to Paris
in ISG9, and saw the end of the Second Em-
pire. Before the break-up, lie met many lit-
erary celebrities, and at this distance of
time sets down his memories of. them in
Parisian Portraits-i; Stephen Swift, 35.).
If you turned off the dusty thoroughfare
into a quiet, shady street, and found an un-
suspected gallery of good portraits in a little
house with a mellow light, you would have
something like the character of Grierson’s
verbal pictures. Much has been written,
and doubtless much will be written, about
Dumas pfere, Verlaine, Mallarme, Lamartine,
the Princess Racowitza. and others in his
collection—all remarkable figures in a
period of picturesque characters. They were
big enough to give many varied impressions,
and those of Grierson are fresh and original.
He was taken to the residence of Dumas
on the Boulevard Malesherbes.
As one thinks of a lion with his shaggy
mane full of the jungle-burs of adventure,
so I can see the author of Monte Cristo as
he appeared on that memorable evening.
Standing about were women friends—ac-
tresses, writers, poets, attracted by a world
of romance, symbolised in the figure seated
in the middle of the salon. I was instantly
impressed by two things : the figures on the
walls and the attitude of the poet. He sat
like a silent oracle surrounded by a crowd
of female admirers, the whole company set
off by panels representing life-sized figures
from Goethe’s great drama: Faust, Mephis-
topheles, Marguerite. There were no other
pictures in the room... Half indifferent he
sat, as some handsome young woman would
stroke his head, while another would place
her hand on his shoulder, as they might
have done with an old lion long tamed and
without teeth. There was nothing to dis-
tract the mind from the harmony of idea'
and personality; the company of women
might have been part of the frescoes, and
Dumas the creator of Faust instead of
Monte Cristo.
A romantic Bacchus, who had written
Monte Cristo and compiled a dictionary of
culinary art, what were his views of men
and things? The form of a face corresponds
to the spirit beneath, and had I been old
enough to judge. I might have guessed that
this wonderful man was above all things a
lover of sumptuous living, rare wines and
rich dinners, romantic suppers after dram-
atic triumphs, the table decorated with
human flowers from theatre, circus and
opera. I might have guessed that this im-
mense frame was never made for fasts and
vigils, and that, like Handel, he might have
ordered a dinner' for three and cleared it off
himself. And what musft have been his
cowers of digestion! Think of the barrels of
dynamic force consumed by such a human
generator in 50 years. . . the quantity and
quality of the viands that went to produce
books like Monte Cristo and Les Trois Moits-
qvetaires!
Grierson met Verlaine at a cafe where Jean
Moreas occupied a corner.
Once in a while he (Moreas) would fix
his eyes on his friend, and say with cnadish
glee: “J'ai du talent! Moi, j’ai du talent.”
M. Anatole France had just written a nat-
tering notice of the poet’s first success Le
I'Her in Itassionne. There was Verlaine sit-
ting before us, like a sleepy lion; others here
and there playing dominoes ; the general calm
broken now and then by the cry of exuberant
naivete : “Moi, j’ai du talent!”
His account of Verlaine does not add any-
thing to that which we have, had in Lepelle-
tier’s biography, but it is sympathetic, and
he says of his poetry: “There is more hu-
man feeling in two lines from Verlaine :
11 pleut sur 10 toit
Et.il plenr dans mon cceitr
than there is in the whole of Milton’s 1 Para-
dise Lost. To Grierson heart is the core of
Art, and the exquisite takes precedence of
the powerful.
Mallarme appealed to him more than any
of the other poets when he visited Paris in
ISS9, and the Quakerish meetings at Mal-
larme's house were in strange contrast with
the brilliant salons of the romantic Empire.
Locke’s New Novel
W. J. Locke is a novelist whose simple
purpose is to entertaim His stories move
in a world of their own, which has only a
superficial resemblance to the world we
know ; but the characters in it act consist-
ently, and seldom fail to hold the reader's in-
terest. If you have a well-made plot,
sprightly and often, times epigrammatic dia-
logue, it does not matter if the characters
are as unreal as those of the Arabian Nights
Entertainment.
Locke might, however, vary his formula.
The last few novels are built on the same
plan. Marcus Ordeyne, Septimus, Simon the
Jester, and Dr.' Quixtus are chips from the
same ecceptric block. And . the central idea
of his latest story, The Glory of Clementina
Wing (Dymock’s Book Arcade), is that of
Simon the Jester turned upside down.
It iiiay be remembered that Simon started
out on a career of active benevolence, which
led him into curious adventures. He quix-
otically scattered a fortune, and reaped a
magnificent crop of spiritual energy. In the
new yarn, Dr. Quixtus, who shares the
honors with Clementina, is crushed like Job
by a series of misfortunes, humiliated and
embittered, and then turns deliberately to do
evil to humanity. The perversion of his
simple nature is just as reasonable as that
of Othello’s ; but the methods adopted for
carrying out his fell design are rather ab-
surd. They serve to tangle up the charac-
ters, bring Quixtus into close relationship
with the famous artist Clementina Wing,
and lead to a settlement which is entirely
satisfactory.
Clementina is described in a sentence-
When her figure, which, as women ac-
quaintances would whisper to each other, but
never (not daring) to Clementina, had, after
all, its possibilities, was hidden by a straight,
shapeless, color-smeared, painting smock, and
all of Clementina as Cod made her that was
visible, save her capable hands, was the
swarthy face with its harsh contours, its
high cheek bones, its unlovely, premature fur-
rows, surmounted by the bedraggled hair
that would have "disgraced a wigwam,
Tommy Burgrave may be pardoned for re-
garding her less as a woman than a painter
of genius who somehow did not happen to be
a man.
When a figure is wrapped up in such in-
volved clauses and stabbed with such a pro-
fusion of commas, anyone would be par-
doned for regarding Locke as a careless
writer. That sentence,, however, is excep-
tional ; he is elsewhere clear and straight-
forward, and his account of Clementina’s
motor trip through France has many hashes
of fine description.
Clementina’s most interesting talk is with
Tommy Burgrave, the non-eccentric relief
character. Tommy has just been telling her
that she is a genius:—
“A woman has only one mission, to marry
and get babies... . Geqius, I can’t be a genius
for the simple reason that I’m a woman.
Did you ever hear of a man mother? No.
It’s a contradiction in terms. So there
can’t be a woman-genius.”
“Surely there was Bosa Bonheur, and—
and in your own line, Madame Vigee Le
Brun.”
“Very pretty,” said Clementina, “but stick
them beside Paul Potter and Gainsborough,
and what do they look like? Could a woman
have painted Paul Potter’s bull?”. ...
“What is your definition of genius?”
“Genius,” said Clementina, screwing up
her face, and looking at the tip of a discol-
ored thumb, “is the quality the creative
spirit assumes as soon as it can liberate it-
self from the bonds of flesh.”
“Good!” said Tommy. “Did you make up
that all at once? It knocks Carlyle’s defini-
tion silly. But I don’t see why it doesn’t
apuH equally to men and women.”
“Woman.” said Clementina, “has always
her sex hanging round the neck of her
spirit.”
There are some dramatic scenes in the book,
and the last, in which the essential woman
in Clementina drives her to do battle with a
professional seductress for the body and soul
of Dr. Quixtus. is a ripping one. The fairy
tale comes to a fitting end in that revelation
pf the hidden glory of Clementina Wing.
The contrast with Stanley P. Hyatt’s Off
the Main Track (Werner Laurie ) is a strik-
ing' one. Ilyatt has spent some years in
the wilds of S’Africa, and this book, like
Searcy’s, is the second skimming of his im-
pressions. They are for the most part un-
pleasant. The country and the people
offended him. The only really interesting
men he met were the scallywags, and he
writes about them coldly. They were much
the same as their kind in Australia—the
broken-down swell, the man who bathed in
champagne, the picturesque ne’er-do-well, etc.
One was a magistrate who cleared a bar in
Bulawayo with a chair once, and, appearing
next day on the Bench with a black eye,
fined all his opponents ss. each. He caine
to grief when he rode into the bar of the
Grand Hotel, and insisted on being served on
horseback.
*** • *
The author of Madge's Trip to Europe
and Hack (George Robertson and Co., Is.
6d.) had no eye for character and no origin-
ality of expression which would make her
letters interesting beyond her family circle.
They are the lively remarks of a girl who
saw _what the guide-book told her, and to
her innocence it seemed funny that Berlin
should be on the Spree. The photograph of
the statuary erected at Bern in commemora-
tion of the International Postal Union is
welcome, as the group is a bold and imagin-
ative piece of sculpture.
* * * *
. The hungry book-maker who tries to polish
off Australians in an imitation of Crosland’s
manner, is bound to have a scoff at the Aus-
tralian pronunciation of English. You can
hear almost every variety of English in the
motley crowds of the cities; but the Aus-
tralian-born is credited by competent
critics with a more correct enunciation than
is to be found, proportionately, in Britain.
In a volume of Essays and’Studies edited by
A. C. Bradley, and published by the Claren-
don Press, last year, Robert Bridges lam-
ented the decay of English pronunciation as
shown by Jones’s Phonetic Transcriptions of
English Prose (1907), a book intended for
the benefit of foreigners. Jones gives the
current pronunciation in this way
erv=of.
ernd-and.
ers=as.
frerm-from.
ert-at.
ter=to.
sergest=suggest.
ideei-idea.
prerduce=produce.
Bridges points out that English spelling
will inevitably develop in the phonetic direc-
tion. and the bad pronunciation will there-
fore be passed on. The only hope of arrest-
ing the decay of English is to invent an alpha-
bet which will represent every sound ; then
the correct pronunciation will be retained in
print, whatever may happen to the living
voice. He suggests an alphabet of 58 sym-
bols, and gives specimen passages in the new
style. He might as well suggest new courses
for the stars. ■ .
A new edition of A. W. Jose’s History of
Australasia has been issued bv Angus and
Robertson, Ltd., at Bs. 6d. net. The author
has added chapters on “The Story of Land
Settlement” and “The Growth of Indus-
tries,” and the printers have used a paper
which makes the photographs appear to
better advantage. The chapter on Aus-
tralian Literature has been illustrated with
portraits of the most prominent writers.

From a publisher’s advertisement of the
books of an Australian novelist: “Who can
forget Adam Lindsay Gordon’s The Sick
Stock Rider, John Farrell’s Sundowner,
‘Banjoe’ Patterson’s The Man from Money
River f” The writer bungled better than he
knew. “Banjo” turned the Snowy into a
money river for someone, as the book sells
steadily at 55., and nearly 50,000 copies
have been printed.

to extract the real man from the tangles of

“Jean Sibi”: The value of Italian Fan-
tasies, the latest work by Israel Zangwill, is
its appeal to almost, everybody, from the
artist to the washerwoman. This clever
Jew, who sees in Italy all the wonder hf a
pawnshop, and all the curious charm of a
lady with a past, “baedekers” all sorts of
“finds,” from the size of Tintoretto’s Para-
dise to the number of chemises in the trous-
seau of Lucrezia Borgia. Here Zangwill
talks lengthily on various topics, from soups
to saints; devotes much space to Machia-
velli, the Mantuan Palace of the Gonzagas,
the Picta theme in Renaissance art ; and
spends whole chapters on such subjects as
of Garibaldi, and the comic re-
public of San Marino. Israel’s most attrac-
tive chapter, however, is on St. Francis of
Assisi, in whom he appears to recognise a
kindred spirit. And certainly it can be
said that he shows a delightful .resemblance
to the holy man, for, much in the manner of
the saint, lie lias made the Wind his brother
and betrays the most conspicuous character-
istic of liis relation.
Still, apart from his volubility, he can
utter a few neat things:—
Not even Lot’s wife supplies sufficient
salt to swallow Genesis with.
Russia was naptised a thousand vears
ago, but she seems to have a duck’s back for
holy water. , *
What Nietzsche’s Superman was to be
Nietzsche did not precisely know, though we
may well suspect that the direction in which
he strained his vision was not the horizon,
but the looking-glass.
Those, however, are only rare flashes in
a prose that bores the- lover of clarified
writing. Israel’s system—if he has a sys-
tem —is to think in the grandiose, and write
in fanfares. Frequently he gives one
phrases like “gargantuan grotesquerie!” and
“megaphonious magnificence !”—which is
merely using language to make loud sounds.
“Hurtle” : In The Claw, Cynthia Stockley
allows (p. 254) her South African prejudice
to make her describe quite needlessly the
only Australian character in the book tliusly:
“Mr. Courtfield was an Australian, born and
bred. Judy told me this —perhaps to ac-
count for the extraordinary accent and bac
manners of the man—so obviously not a
gentleman. Cynthia does not, evidently,
know much about Australia, when she makes
Maurice Stair ask (p. 346) : “Would I
leave Africa with him for Australia, where
an old friend of his father’s had a large ranch
near Melbourne.”

“J.F.” : A few Bulletins back Dyson un-
burthened himself (as a fellow craftsman)
on the art of the dead O. Henry. The analy-
sis was_ excellently critical. The summing
up was impartial, and yet, after reading “An
Ornament of the Bar,” I fanev tbe gentle
Edward is drifting Henry-ward. “The
young lady succumbed to repeated attacks
on a m * n , er a ~<>z. quartz lode and a
-0-quarts’ load,” and “Somewhere between
Ipswich, Queensland, and the Eight Hours’
Monument” have a familiar ring. The “Cas-
tertniv “Chudleigh” “Chalmers” business
is, if memory serves, very like an over-
worked character of Rex Beach’s—“ Finge-
rless Fraser,” I think.
* * * *
Some Thoughts on Hamlet, the lecture
delivered at Sydney University by H. B. Irv-
ing, M.A. (Oxon.), has been published in
pamphlet form by Angus and Robertson. The
chief point he makes is that the “business”
added by actors, probably from the time of
Burbage, is essential to an understanding
ot the play.
* * * *
Louis Esson’s slandhr volume of verse,
‘Bells and Bees.” is favorably reviewed by
London Times. The writer observes:—
Mr. Esson bites in strongly and sharply
lus little etchings of Australian life, being a
rhymer who sees pictures rather than
visions, and he does so with a spirited lilt.
But as in a delightful “Cradle Song,” he has
a tender note.
Amongst the cheap reprints are IT. G.
M ells’ The Time Machine and Oscar Wilde's
Be Profundis (Is. net).
Alfred Stearcy’s adventurous career in
North Australia gave him plenty of material
for a second book. By Ftoo<l and Field (Geo
Robertson and Co.; 3s. 6d.), which is in
some respects a better book than the first.
In Australian Tropics was a collection of
episodes, set down as' remembered; but thjs
is a continuous narrative of his experiences
from the time when, as a lad. he was blown
ashore in a fishing boat, and had to live for
months with the blacks. The story of
his adventures with them, and of incidents
in his life as customs' officer and policeman
is one with plenty of excitement and no
little humor. He covered an extensive tract
of country from the Roper River, l-ouud the
North Coast to the Daly River, near which
is Alt. Searcy, to keep him in remembrance
if his book’s miss-fire. He became closely
acquainted with alligators, buitaloes, white
ants, Alalays, Chinkies and disreputable
Europeans, and talks about them brightly
and graphically.
In spite of the hardships and perils of his
life there, Searcy has an abounding enthusi-
asm for the Northern Territory. He urges
the , young man of courage and industry to
go *nd get busy with its undeveloped wealth.
There is not a note of regret or complaint
in his story.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Home-sickness.
Late along the mountain slopes the warm sun lingers,
Counting ev’ry pine and gum on loving golden
fingers;
But I know a yellow bay and a stretch of brown
lagoon
Where all the day amongst the reeds the salt winds
croon.
O blue waves, and gold sand, and brown reeds
a-sway,
My soul is sick with hunger for the sea to-day!
O’er the muddy meadow streams the willow arches;
Stiffly up the mountain roads the swarthy pine-tree
But 1 miss the twisted trees that, all silent, wait the
tide
And the peaceful guarded reaches where whip-birds
hide.
O brown trees so silent and still waters grey,
My soul is full of longing for your peace to-day!
Far above the gully’s slopes I hear winds calling,
And, far down, the music of the little waters falling.
But 'I have seen a hundred times the white sails dip
and sway,
And watched the white foam follow to the sea mists
grey.
White foam, and white sails, and. grey mist on the
blue,
With my whole heart I’m longing for a sight of you!
N.S.W.
R. B. WALLENDBEEN.
Winter.
Antarctic cyclones, on the wing,
Burst, howling o’er the Tasman Sea;
While Yarra’s floods, with swirl and swing,
Their “flotsam” o’er my garden fling,
With wrecks of cot and tree.
*Tis now I bless the cheery brand
That blazes, glorious, on my hearth;
I’d sooner far, than Sousa’s band,
Or any other in the land,
Fire-songs of warmth and worth.
No martial strains of tube or string,
Nor crash of drums, nor sigh of flute,
Could drag me forth, or comfort bring,
With southern cyclones on the wing.
Rude rain and sleet, to boot!
I’d rather, yes! before the Spring,
With Yarra whirling past at flood,
Than Melba, hear the kettle sing,
While Polar winds upon the wing
First still, then freeze, the blood.
For warmth is Life, and Love and more,
Our hearts’ desire, our nostrils’ breath;
While all our mountains, bare beneath
Are hearsed in snow; and every shore
The Sun smiled on in days before
Lies c01d... and cold is Death !
VICTORIA.
G. G. McG.
At the Judgment Seat.
“And so, Lord, it was all true, after all!”
Tints the astounded soul of Brown made moan,
Standing responsive to its Maker’s call,
Full in the glory of the Great White Throne.
Vast herald angels floated on the wing;
Well-fed they looked, and very clean and mild ;
Each wore a quoit-like halo ; ev’rything
Was as Brown’s Church had taught him as a child.
The soul of Brown peered round in frank amaze ;
It scanned the porphyry streets, and stairs of gold,
And all the rest, whereof, in youthful days,
Its owner had repeatedly been told.
And then the startled spook or wraith looked down.
It saw the fire that dieth not: a waste
Of white-hot flame where damned souls strove to
drown —-
A mercy they might never, never taste.
Stark terror made Brown’s ghost articulate,
And, turning from that marrow-freezing glow,
It prayed to be delivered from the fate
Of them that howled and gnashed their teeth
below.
“I never knew, dear Lord!” it wailed. “In life
My lot was cast among a Righteous horde
Whose days and nights were filled with hate and
strife,
People who grace and charity abhorred.
“ ’Tis true they snuffled of a Better Land,
And bellowed warnings of Hell’s agonies,
Yet bitter venom raged on ev’ry hand ;
They cursed their neighbors on their very knees.
“Their faith was hot abuse of other creeds ,
Wild Slander stood for Virtue in their eyes;
Their sermons dealt with other folks’ misdeeds ,
And all, or nearly all, their tales were lies.
“Am I to blame —who rarely heard the truth,
And saw but weird distortions of Thy Light •
In that I sinned, with little shame or ruth,
Convinced that nothing I was taught was right (
Brown’s spirit’s strange apology died dovvn,
It stood there speechless, limp and panic-grey.
And lo! all suddenly, Jehovah’s frown
Passed like a sudden, summer cloud away.
As in a gracious dream the ghost of Brown
Glimpsed near its ear the feathers of a wing ,
Upon its head there came a golden crown ;
It suddenly conceived a wish to sing.
No soul is more revered than Brown's to-day
Throughout the endless realms of time and space.
“He sinned and scoffed, no doubt,” the angels say,
“But who would have been different in his place.
K.S.W.
J. B. D.
Somebody’s.
There’s nothing to hope for and little to get
For a fellow of fifty in days like these;
But I somehow delight, as my last suns set.
In a bundle of letters of Somebody s.
They are not very brilliant or subtle or smart,
They do not amuse or especially tease,
And yet.. .how they tug at the roots of my heart,
These crumpled old letters of Somebody s!
And when at the end I lie still and behave-
Better far than I ever did yet, I wis
If you’d keep my old dust very warm in the grave
On my heart put those letters of.. .Somebody s.
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 14, 1911.
Prick fin.
EVICTED,
The attempt to crowd all the N.S.W. population Into Sydney is producing curious results. The municipality wants to pull down
sundry ancient slums, but the tenants decline to leave on the ground that it is impossible to get accommodation elsewhere. However,
the summer is coming in, and the open air life is pleasant.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the editor. but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-

dential.

cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 14,1911.
“THE BULLETIN’S” SPRING POEM.
These are the gladsome days of spring,
when the earth rejuvenates itself like a
girl with a new garment; and the surfing
person, whose ambition it is to be the very
brownest bather on the beach, hunts up the
envelope in which he hid away his costume
last drear autumn time ; and the jackass is
joyful in the tree, where he disturbs the
slumbers of the furry ’possum; and the
spring poet sometimes drinks too much
spring beer, and sometimes finds his credit
stopped at the spring pub around the cor-
ner. All these are indubitable sign* that
the period of the earth’s reawakening has
come round again—-for the thousand billionth
time or thereabouts, it may be. But the citi ■■
»en of New South Wales has a sign of his
own by which he knows the spring when it
arrives. It is a voice not wholly akin to
that of the arboreal jackass—a voice which
clamors in the office of the Chief Railway
Commissioner, telling him that the crop this
year will be heavy, if Providence is good to
the man on the land; that last year the
bogged and staggered railways broke down
beneath tne strain, to the farmer’s great
loss and discomfiture; and that this year
they will probably be bogged worse and
even more severely staggered. And the
gentle utterance of spring asks the Chief
Kailway Commissioner, as it has done so
often before, what he proposes to do about
the matter. And he sidesteps the question,
as he has so often done before, and tries to
change the subject to the weather. Then
the raging deputation goes out, and falls
blindly over the bones of a thousand other
deputations that came on the same errand,
and received the same abundant lack of
satisfaction. This is the great spring func-
tion of New South Wales. It is part farce
and part tragedy.
This year, it is intimated in a regretful
sort of way that though the very small cul-
tivated area of N.S.W. has increased a little,
it hasn’t increased much —not nearly so much
as it should have done considering the vigor-
ous growth of population and the heroic fuss
about land settlement. In fact, the move-
ment is just a little better than impercept-
ible. Last year, thanks to the policy which
insists that all commerce must be dragged
to Sydney, regardless of time and space and
distance and cost and common-sense, there
were on occasions 4,000,000 bags of wheat
lying at wayside stations awaiting removal.
This huge stock overflowed the goods sheds
and lay on the platforms or even in stacks
by the roadside, where it was rotted by the
sun and rain, and exposed to thieves and
birds and casual animals. There is only one
port to which produce is allowed to go for
export, for it is the policy of the unspeak-
ably parish-minded State Governments of
N.S.W. to hold that not only is all Australia
that isn’t N.S.W. an accursed place, but that
all N.S.W. which isn’t Sydney is almost
equally accursed. There are splendid sea
outlets in the State besides Sydney, but
every possible effort is made, by refusing
them railway connection, and by the lavish
absence of wharfage and other accessories,
to prevent them being used. And the efforts
have been so successful that the foreign
trade of every coastal town outside Sydney
(with the possible exception of Newcastle,
which has a local coal industry that can’t be
suppressed) is either declining or has van-
ished altogether. This concentration policy
is so disastrous to the farmer that the cul-
tivated area of the State actually began to
diminish after 1906, when the policy of drag-
ging everything to Sydney made its first
heroic breakdown. There was a drop in
1907, and a worse one in 1908. After that
there began a small revival, and the increase
of production in 1909 and 1910 almost drove
many farmers to madness because it was
again found impossible for the railways to
get everything to Sydney in reasonable time,
and many unsold crops decayed and perished
in odd corners. The wheat area in N.S.W.
sank from 1,939,447 acres to 1,394,056
acres between 1906 and 1909, despite the
fact that the steady rise in the markets
made the industry more and more profitable
—on paper. The price of wheat might
ascend continuously in London, but if the
goods were stranded at the railway siding of
Godforgotten, one price was much the same
as any other.
Among the countries which supply Britain
with the material for its imported Foreign-
trade loaf, Australia has now taken the place
once occupied by the United States. But
among the wheat-exporting States of Aus-
tralia, N.S.W., with all its natural advan-
tages, occupies a minor place. The Corn-
stalk farmer labors under disadvantages
which make his position precarious. If he
lives in the far north or south he has to pay
for the carriage of his produce over scores
or even hundreds of miles of needless dis-
tance, and for the dragging of it past adja-
cent ports which would serve his purpose
perfectly well at a fraction of the present
cost. And even when he is prepared to pay,
he often can’t get his material off his hands,
except after delays which mean serious de-
preciation and a long lingering wait for the
money which is, in many cases, badly
needed. The single lines that converge on
Sydney are blocked by the effort to shove
on to them the entire trade of the State.
The wretchedly-hampered freight train
which is dragging wheat 400 miles to Port
Jackson when it might have found an outlet
at another port with only 100 miles of travel,
can only make half or quarter, or even less,
speed by reason of the congestion. It takes
three weeks to get back for a fresh load
when it should, if allowed to run by a
straight line to the nearest good harbor, be
back at the original spot in about three
days. If. after much tribulation, it
reaches the goal of its ambition, it often
happens that the trucks have to stand for a
while with their loads because the struggle
to send away all the exports of the State
from the wharves of one city has created
another deadlock. At these wharves, be-
cause practically all the oversea passenger
and cargo trade of a vast State is crowded
there, only six grain ships can load at a
time. Of course, many more could load at
Jervis Bay, Twofold Bay, Port Stephens,
Goff’s Harbor, etc., if they were allowed,
but then they are not allowed. So with the
block on the ships, the stoppage on the
wharves behind the ships, and the deadlock
on the railways which lie behind the
wharves, and the wickedness or wooden-
headedness of the legislators who lie behind
it all, there is no resource for the farmer
when his patience is exhausted save to go
to some extent out of business. And the
reduction of the area under wheat by over
25 per cent, in three years suggests that, to
some extent, he went out. Later, he began
to come in again, and already he says he is
sorry. Although the rush of 1911 has hardly
commenced, there has already been an occa-
sion when country station-masters were
ordered to refuse all consignments of pro-
duce because the wharves of Sydney were
suffocated and everything was at a sort of
dead finish.
* * * *
The farmer in the west of N.S.W., whose
natural outlet is Sydney, suffers from all
these drawbacks save the needless and costly
and wildly superfluous haulage. He can’t
get quick despatch because his produce is
blocked on the railways and the wharves by
other produce which should go elsewhere.
(These remarks, of courge, only apply to the"
farmer of the near west. The cheap and
natural outlet for the man far out is either
through South Australia or via Portland in
western Victoria. This remote person can’t
get. railway communication with the port
which he wants because it is in another
State, and he can’t get railway communica-
tion with the port that he doesn’t want
because it wouldn’t pay, so he has no rail-
way communication. And, being unable to
get on without it, he lias almost ceased to
exist, consequently he no longer wants any-
thing.) Quaint and interesting are the
stories that are told of this policy of Need-
less Haulage. It often takes four or five
times as much as it need do in wages of
drivers, guards and firemen to drag a freight
train to its destination, because through
much congestion it is four or five times as
long on the road as it need be if the lines
were built on business princioles. It takes,
in many cases, four or five times as
much rolling stock as should be necessary
to carry the freight because a truck or loco-
motive only manages to do one journey
when, with a system which didn’t sacrifice
everything to the making of a huge dead-
lock at one bottle-necked city, it might easily
make four or five journeys. And partly by
reason of this absurd waste of money, Sydney
never seems able to get its North Shore
bridge and tunnel, or its city railway. At
the same time the policy of dragging all the
trade to one city and shoving, as far as pos-
sible, the whole increase of population into
one city, is producing its natural result in a
stupendous increase of rents. The working
man complains bitterly, but it is difficult to
see what else he could expect so long as he
votes for incurable parochialists of the Hol-
man type.
* * * *
The other day Sydney couldn’t even find a
berth, except in a sort of marine back slum
miles from the business centre, for the mail
steamer Ophir. Yet in the desire to crowd
everything into one already almost bursted
spot, the Congestion Party clamors to have
the whole Australian fleet put on to this
same spot. And after years of toil, the party
has managed to get the proposed port at
Jervis Bay limited to a perfectly dry frag-
ment which stops at high-water mark, so
that instead of N.S.W. having another sea-
outlet to relieve the pressure, the trade of
the Australian capital must also come to the
place which is almost off its head with won-
derment as to how to deal with the trade it
already possesses. Great and strenuous
things are now proposed as remedies for the
trouble. The report of the Decentralisation
Commission doesn’t seem to be the basis for
any of the present crop of theories, whereof
the most notable is a scheme for the dupli-
cation, at a cost of perhaps £20,000,000 or
£30,000,000, of the existing lines. For in
the frenzy of Congestion it is considered
better to build, perhaps. 2000 miles of new
line than to make the 80 miles or so of rail-
way which would connect the southern lines
with Melbourne in one direction and with
Twofold Bay in another, and give the north-
ern lines an alternative outlet at Port Ste-
phens. There is nothing in the actual volume
of the traffic to justify all this work as yet,
and the duplication, with all its enormous
cost, won’t reduce by a single mile the
superfluous haulage which is now breaking
the heart of the outlying farmer. Nor will
it in the least diminish the necessity to have,
in many places, four locomotives and 100
trucks to do the work that could easily be
done by one locomotive and 25 trucks. It
will also make the crush at Sydney worse
instead of better. And, at the best, this
wholesale duplication is a very long busi-
ness. Its completion is very far ahead, and
many big loans _ must be floated in the in-
terim. Meanwhile, the immediate trouble is
so great that it lias been seriously proposed
in the press to give stock trains precedence
over everything, even the mails and the ex-
press traffic. The reasons assigned are that
Sydney must have its chop or steak for break-
fast—which, under present conditions, is be-
coming a matter of difficulty ; and that the
stunned and wretched cow, after being bat-
tered for six days along a railway journey
which should be accomplished in 30 hours,
ceases to be an edible animal in any proper
sense of the term.
* * * *
So far as it is possible to judge, the only
people who profit by the present policy are
the landlords of Sydney—a class to which
the Government appears to have sold itself,
body and soul—and it is questionable if even
their gain is quite all that it appears to be.
The rest of the community reaps nothing but
loss. _ It pays the exorbitant rents caused by
the insane Congestion policy. It pays for
the Needless Haulage system, by which the
proper cost of transit is often multiplied by
three or four. It pays for the depreciation
of its produce when a million bags of wheat
are lying round in the wind and weather
waiting for the deadlock to clear up. It
pays the interest on the bank overdraft
while it is waiting for the long-postponed
chance to turn its crop into coin of the
realm. It is the loss of the community when
the cultivated area of N.S.W. drops by
270,000 acres, as it did between 1906 and
1908. And now, on top of all this, the pros-
pect of a still larger loss gibbers at the com-
munity. It is evident that something sudden
must be done, and the State is up against
two possibilities. It may, at huge expense,
furnish more facilities for concentrating the
State’s business at Sydney; it may do vast
duplications and resume some of the highest-
priced land in the State for wharfage pur-
poses ; it may thus force the already exorbi-
tant rents of the metropolis even higher;
and, after achieving all these strenuous deeds,
and running the public debt up to perhaps
£I50,000,000 ; it may leave some of the worst
evils, including the tendency of the remote
parts of the State to become depopulated, un-
touched. Or it may resume, for a compara-
tively small price, great areas of land at
some of the good but wholly-neglected har-
bors like Port Stephens and Twofold Bay;
build the few miles of railway over cheap
and almost untenanted country which will
connect them with the existing lines; and
construct wharves where land costs almost
nothing instead of in places where the pur-
chase money of the soil, if put into sove-
reigns and spread over the surface, would
almost shut out the view. And then it may
allow two or three new cities to grow up,
with the State as sole ground landlord, so
that the ground rents will, in time, render
State taxation almost superfluous. Whether
the Government at Sydney refuses to avail
itself of this splendid chance out of sheer
dull-wittedness, out of a deep-rooted hatred
of the principle of public land-ownership, out
of that strange reasonless loathing which it
seems to have for all Australia which isn’t
Sydney, out of its love for the rich Sydnev
landlord, or out of its fear of the retrograde
Sydney morning press, no one can say. But
in this matter, as in that of the Australian
capital, it toils as manfully in the interests
of the Tory, the capitalist and the ancient
retrogressionist as ever did Gregory Wade
or the most virulent of his associates.
THE COMMONWEALTH BANK AND
SOME SUNDRIES.
Among the Fisher Government’s schemes
and devices for the new Federal session are
included the establishment of a Common-
wealth Bank —a really national Bank of
Australia—and the passing of a Banking
Act which will put tne linancial institutions
of the country on a common basis. Exactly
what these two measures will be like re-
mains, at time of writing, a matter for con-
jecture ; but, meanwhile, the muddled Federal
note issue casts a long shadow over the fin-
ancial reputation of uie Ministry. Anyone
who has walked round the Federal note
arrangement, and viewed it from all points,
is justified in indulging gloomy anticipations
as to the future, it is not that the Fisher
Government is suspected of lack of intelli-
gence. Its trouble is that it has inherited
Alfred Deakin’s affability; that it is too
much afraid of offending its worst enemies;
that it hates to make a large, strenuous, new
departure except in matters lying within the
four corners of the Labor programme, which
programme is supplied to the Government
ready made. When it is obeying its printed
instructions, it is fairly . energetic. When
it strikes some big question on which it has
no definite nrinted instructions, it is a polite
and hesitating and rather futile affair, w r hich
carries its hat in its hand, and is capable of
apologising to a cow if it happens to collide
with the animal.
It was announced, the other day, that the
Federal note issue had reached £9,275,969.
That is to say, the Government had issued
that amount in paper and received gold in
exchange. It held £4,527,033 of coin as a
reserve against its notes —a sum which would
be absurdly large if the Government had
taken any sensible and adequate steps to
keep its paper in circulation, but which is
probably none too extensive, seeing that the
Commonwealth depends on the forbearance
of the banks to prevent its paper being sud-
denly presented for payment in one exciting
barrow-load. If it nad adopted the simple
ana effective device of requiring the banks to
hold a certain proportion of their cash re-
serves (in Canada the proportion is 40 per
cent.) in Federal paper money, and to pay
their customers in Federal £1 notes on de-
mand, it would have had a very much larger
circulation. Also, the circulation would have
been much more steady and reliable, and a
smaller gold reserve would have furnished
ample security. In other words, the Aus-
tralian Government would have been able to
make use of a very much larger quantity of
gold. However,.considering the use it made
of such gold as it was able to dispose of, it
is perhaps just as well that the amount was
no larger.
The Government had a great opportunity,
and did little good with it. A while ago
it obtained, by referendum, a direct mandate
from the people to take over the whole of
tne State debts, and when it takes them over
it is almost bound, according to its avowed
principles, to establish a sinking fund for
their repayment. The taking over of the
debts being a difficult and complicated pro-
blem, and also one in which the Labor Con-
terence takes little interest, it has done abso-
lutely nothing. The mandate of the people,
for all the results that have yet appeared,
might just as well never have been given.
Still the opportunity to do something, and
to do it simply and easily, and without a
single penny of cost, was, and still is, visi-
bly present. The Government has had about
£4,750,000 of gold to dispose of. It might,
had it been less timid, have had £10,000,000
or £32,000,000 available for investment with
absolute safety. And if, with this amount,
it had proceeded to buy up the cheapest
State securities in the market —-some of
them are quoted as low as £B2 for £lOO
worth of stock —and had set the interest
aside as a permanent fund for the repur-
chase of more State securities, the process to
go on till all the State debts were redeemed,
it would have produced certain notable re-
sults. (1) It would have started in real
earnest to fulfil its mandate to take over the
State debts. (2) It would have lived up to
its professions as the party of solvency and
honesty by commencing to wipe off the said
debts. (3) It would have founded its Fed-
eral note issue upon a rock by putting some-
thing like 20s. 6d. of cash and saleable secur-
ities behind each £1 note at the very begin-
ning of things, and the security would have
grown larger every year until there would
have been £5 or £lO or £l5 or £2O of cash and
saleable, or pawnable, securities behind each
£1 note. And it could have done all this —
even the ultimate complete extinction of the
State debts —without the process costing the
country a single copper.
Unfortunately, the Government had no
very large ambitions, and when it had to
deal with a question on which there were no
specific instructions provided in. the party
programme, it lacked the courage to attempt
the Bigger Things. When once it reached
a place wffiere its sailing orders were not
clearly laid down, it was as affable and as
hesitating as Alfred Deakin himself. It
established its note issue on lines which
made the circulation small, which made the
existence of even that small circulation de-
pendent on the forbearance of the private
banks, which gave the Government about as
little money as possible, and which com-
pelled it to hold a very large proportion of
that little money as a reserve for the sort of
emergency that the private banks could
bring about at any moment. Yet with all
these disadvantages, it had some loose cash
—about 4J millions —to play with, and in
the use to which it put this sum it made a
remarkable display of its capacity for throw-
ing away large opportunities. Instead of
applying the fund to the purchase of the
cheapest State securities in the market, and
thus getting perhaps £llO worth of stock for
£lOO, it offered its few millions Jto the States
in the shape of new loans, and thus got
only £lOO worth of stock ' for each
£lOO. In place of teaching economy it in-
cited the States to fresh and extra
extravagance by crying out that it had
cash to lend, and was simply craving to find
borrowers. The States never require much
incentive to borrow, and they rushed the op-
portunity with such energy that N.S.W. last
year added over £4,000,000 to its debt, and
is now getting on a worse jag than ever, and
borrowing at the glorious rate of £5,000,000
per annum, or a little more. By inciting
the States to increase their liabilities the
Federal authority has made the job of tak-
ing over their debts—the job for which the
neglected popular mandate was given quite
a long time ago—so much the more difficult.


The Commonwealth, having lent some
£4,000,000 to the States, now holds and re-
ceives the interest upon a certain quantity
of State securities. But instead of that in-
terest being set aside as a fund, accumulating
at compound interest, for the purchase of
more and more and still more State securi-
ties, it is treated as part of the ordinary
Federal revenue. So the taking over of the
State debts makes no progress. The extinc-
tion of the State debts makes no progress.
The Federal note issue doesn't'expand much,
nor does it become any more secure, because
there is no increase in the reserve behind it.
The Federal note issue still exists by the suf-
ferance or forbearance of the private banks.
Finally, the Australian Government has de-
liberately taught the States a new lesson in
extravagance, by hawking money about as if
it was so much fruit on a barrow, and calling
on thriftless State Premiers to come and
get six bananas in a bag for threepence.
The present Australian Government has
certainly been honest in a plodding, unimag-
inative sort of fashion. But it has put up
such a dismal record as a waster of oppor-
tunities that it is difficult to enthuse in the
old fashion about the proposal to found _ a
Commonwealth Bank. The institution will
almost certainly be a solvent bank. It will
doubtless be an honest bank. It will be full
of good intentions. But, if the Fisiier Gov-
ernment can find a chance, by carefully fol-
lowing the advice of its enemies, the Tory
press, to make it a bank that will only
earn 2d. where it might easily earn
Is., the Fisiier Government, on its past
record, seems safe to seize that chance with
both hands.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
How an Assembly Was Not Utterl.
Degraded.
On the authority of “Common Law Side ’:
It is plain that the official “Liberal’’ lie for the
impending general election in N. S. Wales will be
that the “dignity and honor of Parliament ’ have
been “degraded,” “dragged in the mire,” and so
forth, in connection witli the Speakership bus.ness.
In view of the Hood of mendacity that must soon
start whirling round this matter, it is well to
present the facts of the case right away. Let it be
understood, in the first place, that, before the
Speaker is elected, under the existing system, the
House is to all intents and purposes its own master.
The Clerk of Parliament, who is nominally in
charge, hasn’t even the powers of the chairman of a
public meeting. He may point to this or that
member, as a sign that the person indicated may
speak, but there nis authority ends. Those in favor
of obstruction may babble on indefinitely about any-
thing or nothing, and those in favor of riot are at
liberty to struggle on the floor of the Assembly, and
gnaw each other’s ears; and none may say them nay.
In the N. S. Wales House, the other day, there was
a deliberate plot to take advantage of the situation
for the purpose of holding up the business of the
country. The idea was that the Opposition should
keep the debate going to such an interminable ex-
tent, that the Lieutenant-Governor would be driven
in despair to dissolve Parliament. When Speaker
Willis' took the Chair the scheme had been in opera-
tion for 20 hours ; one “Liberal” had filled in time
by reading an entire speech that had been delivered
by another man in another Parliament months be-
fore; and there was every indication of the insane
business continuing for weeks. The ‘ Liberal
papers were to assist in the event of the Lieutenant-
Governor showing a stiff lip, with editorials of
gradually-increasing violence, urging that an imme-
diate dissolution was the only cure for an intoler-
able situation. The placing of Henry AVillis m the
Chair by the Labor party frustrated this pleasing
arrangement; and it is hard to understand how any
citizen claiming to aspire after decency, law and
order and the rest, can cavil at the circumstance.
A closely analogous situation arose in the House
of Commons over a quarter of a century ago. The
Irish members, by virtue of the primitive rules of
procedure of the day, had reduced obstruction to a
fine art. The business of the British Parliament
threatened to be held up indefinitely. One night,
after a debate had been strung out for days, ana
while Joseph Biggar was steadily reading a Blue
Book from cover to cover —his supply of oratory
having temporarily run short —Speaker Brand broke
in with the words “Discussion on this question has
proceeded far enough.” And the debate ended. The
uproar was terrific. It was conceded on all sides
that the Speaker’s action was illegal—a point
which is by no means conceded in regard to the
recent action of Henry Willis and his supporters.
But it contained the elements of equity and horse-
sense, and that was good enough for the country.
Within a few months the Amended Rules of Pro-
cedure were passed, making wilful and fractious
obstruction an impossibility. . _ . ,
The action of Henry Willis in taking the Speaker s
Chair has not “degraded Parliament,” nor has the
action of the Labor party in putting him there
“trailed the Assembly in the mire.” In fact, an
exactly opposite effect has been produced;—as will
be admitted by anyone who reflects on the hideous
possibilities of a House wherein political hooligans
of the Wood-Onslow-Fitzpa trick breed were
suffered to rage indefinitely in a masterless state.
Also, the AVillis affair has been of value, inasmuch
as it has called attention to the need of new rules
regarding the appointment of the Speaker. As
things are, it is possible for an adroit and unscrupu-
lous Opposition to deny the Government of the day
a Speaker for practically as long as it pleases. With-
out a doubt, had the WADE-ites been skillfully gen-
eralled, they would still be debating the Speakership
matter. This contingency requires to be wiped out.
The Clerk of Parliament should be relieved of the
brief and exiguous authority he holds while the
Speaker is being chosen. A more rational plan
would be to put some Parliamentarian of weight
(preferably an ex-Speaker) in the Chair, and invest
him, temporarily, with all the powers to quell dis-
order or deliberate prolixity that the real Speaker
holds. Thus would such unpleasant spectacles as
those which threatened, last month, to debase the
N.S.AV. Assembly for an indefinite period, be obviated
for all time.
The Ghost of Yassberra.
A man who got a rather cold reception on
the opening day of the Australian Parlia-
ment was Palmer. M.H.R., who propounded
the theory that no more money should be
spent on the proposed Australian capital at
Yassberra until the people had had a chance
to declare, by a straight out vote at a refer-
endum, whether they wanted any blessed
Yassberra capital at all. It was annoying
of Palmer to rake up the subject, for the
very name of that old controversy arouses
unpleasant memories. It reminds a certain
crowd of cowardly or unscrupulous Labor-
ites (and a certain crowd of equally cow*
ardly or unscrupulous “Liberals”) how they
sold the interests of Australia, and how they
turned dog on the allegedly sacred princi-
ple of the referendum and declared the
people unfit to manage their own affairs,
partly because the only real principle that
some of them owned was the principle of
dragging all trade to Sydney, and partly
because some more of them were afraid of
the hollow but infuriated WadE and the
Tory press of the N.S.W. metropolis. Pal-
mes’s words recalled mean shifts, petty lies,
shabby dodges, low-down treacheries, dirty
expedients, cheap shufflings and threadbare
betrayals, all of which the Yassberra push
Simply hates to have dragged again into the
light of day. In order to gratify the crowd
which wants Australia to have a mean, un-
worthy capital, lest one that is lit to be the
centre of a whole continent should over-
shadow the adjacent big State cities, many
strange and smellful things were done. Ihe
report of the eminent N.S.W. Lands official
who was specially appointed to advise on
sites, and who gave his unhesitating vote tor
Monaro, was set aside. The records of the
N S W. Government Meteorologist, published
by the N.S.W. State Government, and show-
ing the district to be almost the driest in
eastern N.S.W., were treated as a series ot
lies. The report of the Federal Commis-
sioners declaring the place to be unsuitable,
and the water supply inadequate, was
shunted out of sight. The evidences ot com-
parative sterility shown in the agricultural
reports of the N.S.W. State Statistician,
were ignored just as if they had never ex-
isted. ‘ The proviso that the Federal site
must be at least 100 miles from Sydney was
strained to the extent that part ot the Aus-
tralian capital territory is only 87 miles trom
Sydney. In order to make certain that the
metropolis of Australia shall be a mere
suburb or annexe of Sydney, existing for the
greater profit and glory of the Sydney land-
lords for whom Hughes and Watson and
McGowen and Holman seem to care so ten-
derly, the scrap of land called the Federal
port was made to end at high-water mark, so
that not even a skiff can sail, except on the
sufferance of the State authorities, into the
so-called seaport. All these and a hundred
other petty meannesses had to be done be-
fore the Sydney Congestion Party came out
on top, and those who have the dirt of them
on their clothes and their consciences natur-
ally dislike any avoidable stirring up of
the ancient mess. Yet the misery of. the
business is that they can’t avoid at times
stirring up the old mess themselves. For
instance, some months ago, when it was pro-
posed that the world should be invited to
send in plans for the new city, it was neces-
sary to give the world some kind of descrip-
tion of the place so that it would know
what it was doing. And this was part of the
account of the balmy Land of Promise as
set forth by the people who Avere responsible
for it: —
The greater part of the city area lies on the Mo-
longlo River, and during periods of prolonged dry
weather the river ceases to run. It is readily fordable
at short intervals during the greater part of the year,
though subject to sudden alterations in volume. In
1891 the highest recorded flood took place, when
the waters overflowed the selected site for the
Federal capital. The maximum recorded summer
temperature is 104°.. .During winter the tempera-
ture frequently falls below freezing point.
That was the best face the Yassberra-ites
could put on their alleged Paradise when
they had to furnish something like a recog-
nisable description of it. It isn’t at all like
the descriptions they used .to give in the
glad old days when they toiled so manfully
in the cause of Congestion. Australia is to
have, it seems, the only white man’s capital
..in the world that is neither on a harbor, a
lake or a permanent-river. It isn’t a cheer-
ing prospect for those who think more of
Australia than of the policy .of dragging
everything to Sydney. And it is no v/onder
that the persons who constructed this insult
to their country looked coldly on. Palmer,
M.H.R., when he dug up the remains of the
great job and proposed that the people
should at last be allowed to express a direct
opinion upon it. Whenever this question re-
appears on the surface the astonishment of
Attorney-General Hughes and his clique
that anyone should propose to consult the
people, or that the people should want any
voice in the matter, is like unto the stag-
gered aspect of the old-time aristocrat when
he heard for the first time that the common
folk had souls. Yet the odds will pro-
bably be against Hughes and his parochial
gang in the end, and the day will come
when Yassberra will be a deserted capital,
and a decayed monument to the madness ot
its builders.

The Case of Dry Brown.
“Herman Hogg” writes
I don’t think that Dry Brown, State Attorney-
General of Victoria, is the fool some people imagine,
with his claim that Victoria should possess the
Riverina. Rather I think he is on a very good
wicket —almost an infallible wicket if Brown is a
decent sort of batsman. In fact, his case seems so
good that it is difficult to imagine why it_ wasn’t
carried to the Courts long ago. According to
British Act of Parliament, the boundary between
Victoria and N.S.W. is “a straight line drawn from
Cape Howe to the nearest source of the River
Murray, and thence by the course of that river,” etc.
Now, certain facts stand out just here like fishing-
rods: (1) The topmost waters of the Murrumbidgee
are among the sources of the Murray. It is the con-
fluence of the Murrumbidgee and the old Hume
River which make the Murray! it was only in com-
paratively recent years that the name Murray has
been regularly applied to that part of the river
which lies above the junction of the Murrumbidgee.
The Murrumbidgee is a much more important source
of the Murray than the Indi, to which the boundary
line is drawn; and unless the N.S.W. Government
gets a spade and diverts the head waters of the Mur-
rumbidgee in quite a new direction, nothing can pre-
vent it being a source of the Murray. (2) Every
map shows that the head waters of the Murrumbidgee
(which afterwards helps to make the Murray) fire
much -nearer to Cape Howe than the head waters of
the Indi (which also helps to make the Murray).
(3) The most visible reason why the line was drawn
from Cape Howe, not to the head waters of the
Murrumbidgee, which are the nearest sources of the
Murray, but to the head waters of the Indi, which
are many miles more remote, thus cutting away the
Riverina from Victoria and adding it to N.S.W., is
that the Sydney Government had most of the job of
laying out the boundary. (4) And by way <>f col-
lateral evidence, the old District of Port Phillip,
which later became Victoria, extended from the
Murrumbidgee southward to the sea. Therefore,
Dry Brown of Victoria has a case —it almost looks
like an overwhelming case. Anyhow, if he carries
his point and annexes Riverina, it should be a
mio-hty good thing for Riverina. For then the half-
dozen Victorian railways which now stop dead at
the present border-line will be able to cross the
riveri and give Riverina short, straight, cheap lines of
communication with a much more adjacent port
than Sydney. In this matter the Yass-Canberra
push scattered away an opportunity. If the Aus-
tralian capital (which is to be fixed within the
boundaries of N.S.W.) had been located on Monaro
inside the Debatable Land, that would have amounted
to a recognition bv the Australian Parliament that
the Debatable Land is in N.S.W and not in Vic-
toria And if it had been so fixed without any pro-
test from the State Government of Victoria—tlm theii
Vic Government showed no desire. to protest that
would have amounted to a recognition by Victoria
that the Debatable Land is in N.S.W. and not m
Victoria. In fact, it was in the power of N.S.W. to
mike Victoria give away its case. The chance was
neglected, and now Dry Brown, of Melbourne, has a
very good show if he has the energy to push the
matter to a finish. The most uncertain item in the
case is the perseverance and tenacity of Dry Brown.
Does anybody know anything of Brown? In fact,
does Brown exist?

An Officer and a (Prospective) Pauper.
Reference was made in the Australian Par-
liament, last week, to the meagre pay ot Area
Officers, and to the sweating that prevails m
the military service generally. _ The indict-
ment didn’t go far enough. It is a scandal,
for example, that there should be no pen-
sions for army officers. In every other army
on the civilised earth the regular fighting-
man is provided with a pension proportion-
ate to the rank he holds on leaving the ser-
vice In Australia tne soldier drifts into
private life without a farthing. The anom-
aly is obvious when it is remembered that
every civil servant, from the highly-salaried
front-rankers down to postmasters and
policemen, have certain pension rights. Other
countries are particularly considerate of
those who give their services to the State.
Only last July a resolution was carried in
the British Parliament increasing the then
maximum pension of £lOOO per annum for
Dominion Governors to £l3OO per annum.
And a similar tendency is observable else-
where. There is particular need that Aus-
tralia should make its military service worth
while, in a financial manner of speaking. Our
army carries no social or other prestige. In
fact, as one legacy of the muddling volunteer
system which prevailed in the States before
Federation, it is commonly regarded as
something of a jest, not to say a byword,
and there is no sort of a rush to break into
it. The only way the country can wipe out
that highly undesirable prejudice is by at-
tracting to the service men of character and
intelligence; and lots of them. And the sole
means by which that is going to be achieved
is by offering brainy men reasonable induce-
ments to make soldiering their life profes-
sion. As things are, a captain has to retire
at 40, a major at 45, and a lieutenant-
colonel at 48. It may be due to no fault on
the officer’s part that he did not get his
step, and is among those who are thrown
out on a co'd world at the age of two
score; it may be merely a matter of his
superiors being a long-lived lot. Yet he must
go, and —if he is an Australian —set about
the forlorn hope of finding a new job in his
middle age. Obvously such a system is no
good to anyone but a soldier of independent
means. For various reasons, besides the
powerful one above-mentioned, the army
offers scant allurement, as a calling, to the
moneyed Australian. And, in any case, it.
is the capable Australian —who sometimes is
born into a moneyed clan, but far more often
is not —whom it is desirable to attract. As
in every other walk of life, this class of citi-
zen will carefully eschew the profession of
soldiering unless he can discern a prospect of
reasonable comfort at the beginning, middle
and end of the job. Hutton fought hard for
a military pension system when he was in
Australia ; Kitchener has recommended the
reform in his official report, and likewise
verbally. If the Australian Government has
any arguments in rebuttal, it should . put
them forward; otherwise it has no justifica-
tion for delaying a measure that appears as
wise as it is equitable.

Men of Peace.
Ramsay Macdonald, one of the lamentable
British Labor members who have at various
times afflicted Australia, has been writing
silly advice to this country in the British
Friend, and Sydney Telegraph, character-
istically enough, gives him a heap of space.
Says Ramsay :
I disagree with Australia when it assumes that its
domestic politics require the backing of force. The
one influence above all others that will justify the
policy of a White Australia to the world, and keep
it safe in the long run for Australia itself, is that it
is not backed up by force, but by the elementary
justice of the case. ...
From the military point of view the citizen army
is unnecessary, because it presupposes invasion, a
thing which practically every military and naval
authority deems impossible. . . . The Australian
Government is said to have been advised to the Same
effect, and when one takes into account, not merely
the geographical difficulties, but those of a financial
and political nature, in which Japan will be involved
for years, one can appreciate a remark 1 heard from
a high military authority, that a plan for the in-
vasion of Australia by Japan would be worth the
while of the Editor of Punch to look at, but would
not be worth space in the archives of the Admiralty.
Moreover, for the time being, our treaty with
Japan should give Australia an added sense of
security, and, in the present moment of transition
between a military organisation and a judicial
system for the settlement of disputes, it is a great
pity that Australia should begin a policy ot mili-
tarism. This will hamper the fulfilment of an object
which the rest of the world is beginning to set its
heart upon with more and more determination, as
the result of experience of armies and navies
the establishment of an International Court which
will end militarism and establish peace.
They are wonderful talkers, these Peace
Society folk, but they somehow fail to carry
conviction. The idea that the White Aus-
tralia policy, without even a gun behind it,
should depend on the “elementary justice
of its case is very beautiful as coming from
a party which finds that the elementary jus-
tice of its own case doesn’t impress the local
landlord, monopolist, sweater, criminal or
House of Lords worth a cent. And it would
be interesting to know the name of even one
of these alleged or bogus military, and .naval
authorities who declare that the invasion ot
Australia is “impossible.” But the rare jest
of the whole story is that, picture ot an m-
ternational court which will settle all inter-
national differences without resort to force
the court which won’t have a single regi-
ment to enforce its decrees, yet will be im-
plicitly obeyed—on the good days of the mil-
lennium when the vegetarian lion slumbers
with its ear resting on the lamb.
Even the House of Commons has its
police and its chucker-out; and a
plain little English police court has
one or two vast bobbies on hand to pre-
vent the accused throwing his boots at the
magistrate. No court which hadn’t a police-
man handv ever amounted to much in this
world. Arbitration tribunals are very handy
when neither party wants to fight, and each
is looking for a chance to back out with dig-
nity • but they are of no service at any other
time.’ When poor little Korea carried its
case against Japan to the Hague tribunal
it haupened that Japan reallv wanted to
fight, and the blessed tribunal, knowing that
in such circumstances if didn’t amount to a
row of pins, saved its face bv disqualifying
the gentlemen from Korea, and chasing them
off the premises. Korea with an effective
army might have been listened to, but an
unarmed Korea was anybody’s stray dog.

has finally dismissed the matter of Hop Poon
Gooey, his Chinese wife and newborn Chink-
lin°' from the sphere of sentimental agita-

parson friends w T ere blown into space by the
cold breath of Batchelor. Gooey, of Gee-
long, before going to Hongkong to marry a
Chinawoman, got permission to bring her
with him for a six months’ honeymoon when
he returned to Australia. He delayed his
return so that his wife might produce a baby
within the six months’ limit, and the period
was extended to nine montns to allow of the
tinted matron being fit for deportation to
Hongkong. Meanwhile, Gooey and the par-
sons assailed the Australian Government
with arguments touching social morality, the
sanctity of the home, and precedental cases
of Chows who had been specially permitted
to establish Chow wives, in this country.
Gooey had evidently designed to lure the
Law into a further indiscretion by the grace
of Batchelor, but Batchelor is rigidly urn
gracious. Therefore Mrs. Gooey will return
to Hongkong in fulfilment of her husband’s-
contract, tAing her baby with her. It
rested with the Government to maintain the
law of local man, and if Mr. Hop Gooey

which the parsons speak about, he will not
part himself from wife and offspring. Goff
When the Shoddy Idols Go.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Truer faith this nation needeth —-we shall
build the Commonweal
When the larger Creed succeedeth and the
ancient idols reel!
Man who marches through the ages leaves
behind him changing creeds;
From his day’s swift-turning pages hner
truth and wisdom reads !
For the Past with galling shackle binds the
prisoned soul of man,
And its priests with cunning tackle, strangle
Progress for a span !
But new hymns of adoration in our hearts
are breathing low ; .
And this Land shall be a Nation when the
Shoddy Idols go !
We have bowed to idols olden, mumbled
creeds of dumb despair;
Swinging censers rich and golden we have
climbed the altar stair!
But the Truth that will not slumber, woke
our hearts with sudden pain,
And our father’s joss is lumber that we pass
in new disdain !
Man who drags his old gods slowly through
the everlasting years,
Spurns at, last each image holy, and his
prayers are changed to jeers !
So our fathers dragged Tradition over seas,
and bended low —
Fails the last, lone superstition when their
Shoddy Idols go!
Thought and Will —these make the Nation,
for the courage of the soul ,
Stirs the flesh with jubilation when its faith
is strong and whole!
Wherefore we shall doubt no longer—doubt
is handmaid unto Death ;
Find ye creeds of Truth and stronger, thus
the law eternal saith !
And this land is Truthward turning in these
new, aspiring days—
When the hearts of men are burning, clear
the path, unbar the ways !
And they see the newer dawning—signs are
these that plainly show-
That the sepulchre is yawning where the
Shoddy Idols go.
Stronger faith this land requireth—with the
soul as well as steel,
With the Truth that man desireth we shall
build the Commonweal!
Man who marches through the ages leaves
dead creeds and faiths behind ;
And To-morrow’s priests and sages will not
crucify the Mind !
For the Past with fetters galling strangled
Thought upon her bier,
But the sound of idols falling echoes loudly
in mine ear!
Hail! New hymns of jubilation in our
hearts now swing and glow;
And this land shall be a Nation when the
Shoddy Idols go!
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.

S. M. Herald, after referring acidly
(l/9/’ll) to “the dovecotes and dog ken-
nels in place of human habitations” that
the city landlord has been in the habit of
letting to the poor, proceeded as follows:—
The municipalities within reasonable distance of
the city are at the present juncture afforded an op-
portunity to do for the working classes what their
British compeers have done with excellent results.
If it pays Private Enterprise to put up cottages
suitable to the demands of the house-hunters, it is
reasonable to assume that municipal enterprise
would be similarly successful, and it is equally
reasonable to assume that a better class of dwelling
could be erected, and still let at a rental that would
pay the authorities without exposing the tenant to
the harsh conditions he has at present to face.
This clarion call for State landlordism
reads curiously in a sheet which, a year or
so ago, was representing Socialism to be all
the calamitous things George lleid said it
was. Furthermore, if it is desirable that
the ordinary common or garden council
should build and let houses as a means of
rescuing the indigent from the extortionate
treatment of the Private Enterprise land-
lord, it is obviously desirable, also, that the
State should sell gas, ferry trips, etc. (these
monopoly-controlled necessaries being notor-
iously extortionate in price and poor in
quality at present) ; and when that is ad-
mitted, it is hard to see what distinction
there is between the Herald’s point of
view and that of a mere Socialistic organ.

What the “Scotch Australians” of Mel-
bourne said to the Commonwealth’s Minister
of Defence last week, whilst petitioning for
a reprieve of the condemned Vic. Scottish
Regiment in Kilts, was almost as weak-
minded as the Minister’s reply. Having
stated that the question was a “racial one,”
involving glorious traditions of Scottish regi-
mental prowess in Britain’s wars, they
drifted into health reasons, and declared that
“the British Medical Association has given
the palm to the kilt as a soldier’s dress.”
Now, considering that the V.S.R. warriors
have rarely worn their legs uncovered in the
past, and would not have to remove their
pants much oftener if the Commonwealth
sanctioned their racial distinctiveness, it is
obvious that their kilts, under the circs., are
more conducive to rheumatism than helpful
to health. The racial argument is pernicious,
and the sanitary argument doesn’t wash.
Minister Pea.rce, in reply, laid down the
good Australian law of policy as regards
clans, sects and the symbols thereof—the
law that a general uniformity of Australian
army uniform makes for uniformity of Aus-
tralian sentiment. The army cannot have a
national outfit if part of it wears kilts, or
other ancient forms of foreign clothing. Or
the kilted section cannot be called national.
Having declared the policy, the Minister
proceeded to tamper with his principles. He
said he would consider the matter of allow-
ing the Vic. Scottish Regiment to enroll re-
cruits on its own,” and would not object
it regiments desired to retain, at their own
expense, special uniforms for special occa
sions. In short, the Minister, although
convinced of the necessity for an Australian
national sentiment in army clothing, would
be prepared to let foreign racial sentiment
clothe itself in un-Australian finery on special
national occasions.
The following is from Dalgety’s Review :
A notable shipment of merino rams and ewes
was placed aboard the Georgic last week for South
African ports. The sheep came from a number of
the noted studs and flocks in N. S. Wales... Our
stud- and flock-masters welcome the South African
buyers, and there is no embargo or high export duty
placed on sheep by Commonwealth laws. This
stand is in marked contrast to the prohibitive duty
placed on ostriches and Angora goats by the S
African Government. The South Africans come here
for the best types of Australian sheep for wool-
growing purposes, and every opportunity is afforded
them for securing the very best rams and ewes at
the same prices as are charged to Australian flock-
masters. There is no attempt to monopolise merino
wool-growing in Australia as there is in regard to
mohair and ostrich feather-growing in S. Africa.
Whether the freedom of Australians should be re-
ciprocated by the South Africans, or whether the
restrictions of the South Africans should be followed
by the Australians, it is not for us to say; it is
sufficient to point out the anomalous state of affairs,
for wool-growing to Australian pastoralists is fully
as important as Angora- and ostrich-breeding is to
South African farmers.
Of course it is, but “our flockmasters wel-
come the S. African buyers,” and the above
wise remarks appear in a paper that none
but the flockmaster reads. In the interests
of the nation’s ereat industry, the “anomal-
ous state of affairs” should be published in
newspapers that the other Australian reads.
Generations of Australian sheepowners have
evolved a sheep that is superior to all others,
and unpatriotic individuals are allowed to
sell to foreign countries this hard-won
national asset. The sheepman owns the
sheep, but the type belongs to the nation. An
export duty is not sufficient. Only total pro-
hibition will answer.

“Samson and Delilah,’’ which is being-
done occasionally at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney), has one supreme merit that is almost
unique in grand opera productions. The
stars not only sing, but look and act their
parts. Eleonora de Cisneros is a born Deli-
—a gorgeous sample of feminine grace
and vitality and vast barbaric beauty. Zeni
is the only operatic tenor the writer has
heard or seen who possesses the physical at-
tributes of a Samson. They make an epic
spectacle as they tower above the small,
whining Israelites and the latter’s degen-
erate-looking oppressors. Saint-Saens’s work
shows the tempting and undoing of Samson
by Delilah; she being impelled thereto, not
only by racial sentiment, but by reason of
the fact that Samson had spurned her on a
previous occasion. It ends with the des-
truction by Samson of the Temple of Dagon
and its Philistine worshippers. A dramatic
and effective picture.
* * * *
The music is familiar to many Sydneyites
(Georgina Delmar, Andrew Black and
Walter Whyte rendered it in the Town Hall
less than a year ago), but the present inter-
pretation amounts to a revelation. The
mezzo-soprano star is an actress as well as
a consummate vocal artist. Zeni has a ring-
ing tenor of mighty power and volume.
Kaufmann, a bass who plays Abimelech, and
Scandiani (the High Priest) are excellent;
and the stage settings are superb. It is con-
ceivable that this opera might be as per-
fectly produced in London or Paris, but it
is impossible that it should be more skilfully
cast or that the chorus work should be liner
anywhere.

Puccini gets a bold grip of the story that
stamps through “La Bolieme.” The jovial
quartet of Bohemians who warm their blue
fingers—in an ecstatic burst of song—by the
flames from Rodolfo’s Great Tragedy on the
hearth, are still skipping off to" the pawn-
shop in Act IV. Melba, as Mimi, fails to re-
peat the histrionic sensation of her first ap-
pearance as Violetta in “Traviata.” On the
dramatic side she is less convincing than
Bassich —who created the part of Mimi in
Sydney in 1901 —but Bassich, as a singer,
was a mile or two below Melba. The flaw-
less vocal art of Australia’s greatest song-
bird cast a new glamor over Puccini’s opera
on Saturday night, and there was great joy
when tenor McCormack, as Rodolfo, poured
his love-song into the moonlight with Mimi-
Melba, who couldn’t go home just then, be-
cause she’d lost her door-key, and wanted to
scratch for it on Rodolfo’s attic floor. All
the beauty of the Skylark’s perfect voice is
hung out at the sign of “La Bolibme.”
McCormack gets greater opportunities as an
actor here than in “Traviata”; and the
histrionic craft of Scandiani, harnessed to
his delightful voice, made Marcello one of
the prize snatchers in Puccini’s melodious
opera. Dammacco and Cristiani deserve a
bouquet for their determination to sing in
the act of packing up an overcoat for the
pawnshop. The tiny Axarine breaks into
“La Boh&me” in the Latin Quarter as
Musetta —Act 11. A slip of a girl, Axarine
has most of her troubles before her, if she
means to toil up the prickly path or operatic
fame. She is a graceful little creature with
a few bird-like notes in her soprano pipe,
but there seems to be only one good reason
for calling her the “Russian Patti’ - —all
the other Pattis must be doing time in
Siberia. Musical-director Angelini, and
deputy-conductor Sacerdote were hauled into
the public eye by Melba-Mimi, who left her
death-bed at the end of the fourth act to take
several tons of appiause.
