•*- *
The new piece at the Adelphi, “The Angel
of His Dreams,” is different from most
bellowdramas. The hero usually winds up
by marrying the heroine, but in this case he
marries the villainess, and doesn’t lock her
out when she comes home drunk. There is a
moral, of course—most melodramas have
morals—and it seems to be “The wages of
being good is lead” ; for a good young man in
the second act is shot by his wife, because he
didn’t steal some money. There is also a
change from the usual weapons of warfare;
and kitchen chairs and brandy bottles make
a refreshing variation upon noisy revolvers.
The story is of a woman—bad, naturally—
who came between a parson and his sweet-
heart, and, as aforesaid, shot her husband,
married the parson, and took to drink. The
parson is arrested for the murder. To shield
himself the murdered man’s father kills the
parson’s wife; but she gets in first with a
confession, and tells that it was the dead
man’s father who stole the money, and the
villain is arrested. There is the usual
sprinkling of funny people. Cecil Manner-
ing makes a good parson as the Rev. Chris
Esdaile, but puts up with too much from his
wife. Major Ferrars, the villain (C. R.
Stanford) ; R. W. Riley, as Roy, his son,
who was shot; and Sam Tippett (J. R.
Howard) are the chief male characters. Ada
Guildford (Hilda, the parson’s wife), Adele
Kelly (Milly, who wanted to marry the par-
son ). and Lilian Wiseman (Bessie, whose
job in life is to wear an apron and give
back-answers) are prominent among the
ladies.

“My dear man, don’t you know that King-
Henry the Eighth has been dead for hun-
dreds and hundreds of years ?” “Good Lord !
Has he? How the time does fly! I didn't
even know he. was ill.” The programme
asserts that eight calendars have become
waste paper since Clark and Hamilton were
last at Sydney Tivoli. No turn quite so re-
freshing has turned up in the interval, and
Memory, after a hurried glance over the
shelves of the Past, says: “No, we have only
received one sample of those goods.” Mr.
Clark (or Hamilton) fools naturally with-
out effort, and the decorative Miss Hamilton
(or Clark) acts up to him in a winsomely
demure way. A lot of the evergreen humor
of the original turn has been retained. The
male of the pair still stops in the middle of
a patter song to wearily damn his dropping
eyeglass, and plays both sides of a slice of
bread carefully on the piano. It is all non-
sense served up gaily. Plarry Allister, the
other newcomer, is a serious character im-
personator whose portraits are just limelight
waxworks. He works rapidly, and is most
successful with the celebrities we know only
by portraits. His. George Reid is recognis-
able; but his Deakin w-ould be cut by a heap
ot Australians. McLallen and Carson, on
skates, Dapper Louie Tracy, comedian Fred
Bluett,, and Clarke, and Mostol. the patter
pair with the electric feet, make up the rest
of the bill.
* * * *
Mill Sullivan, a dialect comedian from
Lancashire,, constitutes the week’s new goods
at the - National Amphitheatre. Lancashire
appears to be the region where jokes die
last; anyhow, every humorist from that
county, in the writer’s recollection, has been
a species of human chestnut tree. William
is no exception to the rule. However, he
unloads his ancient humor in a sprightly
and taking fashion. Eva Mudge, the quick-
change marvel, will flit shortly; but Jordan
and Harvey (Israelites) and Garrison (the
owner of the Roman Maids) are still going
strong.
** * *
The Bulletin came away from the
Y.M.C.A. Hall in Sydney, the other night,
profoundly discouraged. It had been listen-
ing to one of C. Wragge’s interesting lec-
tures concerning the flight of a sole, or per-
haps it was soul, and all went well until the
announcement was made that there is no
death, and that after we finish up on this
plane we are merely transferred to another
to get work again. So if the Inclement is
right, there is no escape through all Eter-
nity from Calamity Wade, Bro. Worrall,
Jokook, Fitzparrot, Jack Wren, Phidson, P.
Moses, and similar people whose existence
up till now had made The Bulletin’s
meteorological editor ready to pass in his
alley without protest. But the Inclement’s
remarks put a different complexion on
things, and unless this paper can learn from
him that the above-mentioned are to be
marooned on a plane of their own, it refuses
to have anything to do with the new arrange-
ment.

For another week H. B. Irving, at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), will present the Melan-
choly Dane as a more amiable egotist than
was expected, and the question as to whether
Gertrude's long-winded son was madder than
he thought himself, or less balmy than his
friends thought him, will receive undue at-
tention in letters to the daily press. The
management hungers and thirsts for intel-
lectual discussion on the point. Probably
the shade of England’s classic dramatist is
wondering what somebody will say next. A
revival of “Hamlet” seems bound to agitate
all Shakspearian students save the irrever-
ent few who reckon that the Bard dashed off
his immortal creations in commercial haste,
regardless of posterity and its admiration
societies. The present commentator, who —
Himself when wmng did eagerly frequent
Doctor and Crank, and heard great argu-
ment
About it and about; but evermore
Came out by the same door where in lie
went
inclines personally to the theory that Shak.
ought to have intended Hamlet to be mad,
seeing that the wmrling wordster speaks of
the Great Beyond as a country whence no
traveller returns, although his deceased par-
ent has just given him an object lesson to
the contrary No matter. On the 23rd “The
Lyons Mail” ’mil be staged.

“The Chocolate Soldier” is still adding to
its reputation at Melbourne Royal, where

*■ * *
“The Speckled Band” goes on playing at
the Princess’s. Unhappily, the Band’s reper-
toire is. limited to one tune, Gaston Mer-
vale_ being the Conductor. Disguised as a
medical scientist of the deepest dye, Gaston
waves his arms mriously, whilst a certain
strain of melodrama is repeated again and
again. His shapely stepdaughter (Miss Dix)
in the clingsome gown, persists in trembling
in his hated presence for two long years, al-
though she has a private income, and might
go into board and residence elsewhere. The
incredible Doctor’s housekeeper (Miss
Vaugnan), who steadfastly loves him for his
murderous habits and horrible grimaces,
hangs on to the hope of marrying him when
his surviving stepdaughter has followed her
sister and mother to Eternity. The village
grocer patiently nurses his suspicions, and
Dr. Watson keeps on believing that some-
thing is wrong. The preposterous mystery
remains in a state of suspense waiting for
Sherlock Holmes to happen. And the whole
burden of the performance—the sole cause of
his employing an educated adder to wriggle
down a bedroom bell-rope, and bite ladies as
they lie asleep—is the raving doctor’s sordid
desire to inherit his victims’ private means.
All things considered, this degenerate Sousa
deserves to be slain by his own Speckled
Band. The reptile, chivvied from its fatal
bell-rope. by the providential Sherlock, bites
the waving hand that fed it, and leaves the
housekeeper without a hope of wedded bliss.

Next Saturday week Australian drama
will get a look in at the Princess’s. George
marlow, Ltd., has undertaken to present
“The Lady of the Pluckup” to a world that
wants to meet her. “The Lady” is Randolph
Bedford’s literary offspring, and her appear-
ance on the posters indicates an artistic tem-
perament.
** * *
Melbourne’s Repertory Theatre reopens
for its second season on Monday, 25th. Gre-
gan McMahon has got three plays in rehear-
sal —“Candida.” "The Fountain” and “The
Voysey Inheritance,” and two performances
of each will be given.
** * *
At the King’s there are only two more
nights of “The French Spy” and the present
co. On Saturday comes a one-time new and
original drama of domestic interest entitled
“Struck Oil.” In this, Maggie Moore will
make her latest reappearance as Miss Stofel,
supported by H. R. Roberts and a number of
more or less important comedians.
** * *
The Binns Vagabonds, who are musical,
and Alcide Capitaine, whose gymnastics are
sweet displays of female form divine, came
to strengthen the bill at the Opera House
last Saturday. Also, Sydney Bach returned
after a short absence. Several other people
are proceeding as before, but the Spissell-
macks, or McSpissells, are closing their
panto, to-night (Thursday).
Dolores, having added a Saturday matinee
to her farewell appearances at Melbourne
Town Hall, has departed for an uncertain
period. The lady of the dimples is not off
to Europe immediately. She has to sing in
some of the Victorian towns, and re-arouse
the drowsy enthusiasm of Tasmania ere she
quits these latitudes But Saturday’s, was her
good-bye recital in Melbourne, and when she
had sung “Home, Sweet Home” with just
enough of special reference-to-the-occasion
in her voice, the multitude sighed like a fur-
nace. Dolores is Queen of Arts in descrip-
tive warbling. She never loses charm for her
audience, as another fine singer would do
under equally exacting conditions. Her un-
affected winsomeness holds good all the time
because it never descends to bewitchery.

In Adelaide, the King’s is making a vig-
orous snow with variety turns new to Aus-
tralia. One is Haydee’s marionettes, an old-
fashioned performance revived. The imita-
tion of Chung Ling Soo conjuring is remark-
able. Other items include Maude Mortimer,
with splendid costumes, a sense of humor
and a grand-opera voice; and Smite and
Smote, French acrobats, whose burlesque
wrestling match is a killing turn. The Tivoli
scores heavily with the cannon-ball play of
Francis and Alfred, Lamberti’s mixed music
and acting as various players and composers
of note, and Alexander Prince’s concertina
work. “The Whip,” at the Royal, is start-
ing that second week which in Adelaide is
the sure mark of success. It is the regular
Drury Lane type, obvious melodrama, but
of the best class, aim built round and de-
pending on several big stage effects. There
is an absence of higb-falutin’, the villain in
particular being almost real. The heroine’s
stagey proposal to act as jockey in a big race
is promptly frowned upon, and the comic
relief in the Chamber of Horrors’ scene
merges with great skill into the serious in-
terest of the" play. Wondergraph and the
Empire have both been starring “Enoch
Arden” (How do these things happen?) ;
West’s have made a feature of “The Circus
Girl”; Paris pictures at the old rink have
got well into their stride; and the Yitoscope
has got uack to the Victoria Hall after being-
elbowed out for a week or two, The subur-
ban halls are mostly full of films, too.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”: You have it from “Stargazer” that news-
papers “here, there and everywhere” have credited
“The Chocolate Soldier” with “retaining the wit
and vivacity of Shaw.” Surely not. The plot of
“Arms and the Man” is roughly caricatured or tra-
vestied, and the opera is sufficiently bright to be
amusing. Nobody that I know of has credited it
with Shaw’s wit and vivacity. “Arms and the
Man” is for the educated minority, not for the
public that wants comic opera. Nor is “The Choco-
late Soldier,” being only an English translation of a
broad German travesty of Shaw’s witty satire, quite
meaningless enough for the average comic-opera
patron. It ought to be rather more simply ridicu-
lous, seeing that it couldn’t possibly have been made
so simply and gayly cynical and funny as a Gilbert-
Sullivan opera. Shaw didn’t intend his comedy to
be travestied for the musical stage. “The Chocolate
Soldier” couldn’t have prospered on a fidelity to
Shaw’s wit and vivacity, which have very little com-
mercial value on the stage. Why, in the name of
business principles, should the opera be expected to
breathe the literary, elfin spirit of “Arms and the
Man” ?
Lamberti, the good musician and better impersona-
tor, who has been showing at the Sydney Tivoli, is
an Englishman (proper name Lambert), who has
had what the classics call “a chequered career.” A
member of a big shipping family, he helped to
manage the first line of steamers that ran up the
Manchester Ship Canal, but as there wasn’t more
salary than he could spend, he resigned. Migrating
to Canada on spec., he got right up against tough
luck. “There was a time in Winnipeg,” he said the
other night, “when I was working about 15 hours
out of the 24—8 a.m. till 5 p.m., selling tickets for
the Canadian-Pacific Railway Co. ; from 6 p.m. to
8 p.m. acting as a head waiter; from 8.15 p.m. till
11 p.m., conductor of the Opera House orchestra —and
even then I couldn’t scratch together enough to get
clear of the town! That was before the boom. I
eventually raised 500dols. by composing a comic
opera named “Chon,” and producing it with ama-
teurs. I tried my hand at most things in Canada,
from gold mining to cattle punching. With my
500dols. I got to New York, and there things
brightened. I got a start as musical director of two
or three cos., and gradually worked on to the stage.
Sixteen years after leaving England I returned as
the ‘star’ at the Hippodrome. Then, being satisfied
that I had proved I could do better than collect a
few pounds a week in the shipping house, I wrote to
my relations. Coming back to Australia? Sure!”
A Melbourne father, appealing to H. B. Irving,
said: “Another reason why 1 think my son’s natural
inclination is for acting—he detests getting up
early.”
This paper referred to Mrs. Korolewicz-Wayda,
the delightful soprano of the Grand Opera Company,
as a Russian. It has been informed, more in
sorrow than in anger, that she is a Pole. She
scorns the insanitary and melodramatic race of
Nicholas 11., and those who accuse her of belonging
to it in future will do so at their own risk.
Thomas Kingston, whose death was reported last
week, was a son of the house of Shipwright, the
famous Piccadilly Circus (London) barber folk.
He was educated at a Benedictine monastery in
Kent, and at one time intended to become a priest.
The habit of religion stuck to him to the last.
When he was in Sydney the other day, playing in
“The Girl from the Golden West,” etc., he regularly
turned up at early mass at the Cathedral —-an un-
usual whim in a mime. His wife is Adeline de
Lara, the pianist.

past preparing for the production, at Her Majesty’s
(Sydnev) of “Ben Hur,” on. a scale of magnificence
unequalled by the great settings of the piece which

There is money in producing popular plays on
the cinematograph screen —over £5OOO was cleared
by C. Spencer out of “The Fatal Wedding”—and
the scramble for the transient lucre shows no sign
of slackening lately. At the present time Wests’,
L>td., are photographing a play; Roy Redgrave and
“Jimmy” McMahon are posing “The Christian” in
front of the machine ; and George Musgrove is bring-
ing “Sweet Nell, of Old Drury” to Sydney with a
similar object.

“Theorbo”: The Armes Beaumont testimonial is
making progress, but will be the better for another
fillip. It has not been stated here that Beaumont
made his first operatic appearance as Thaddeus,
in “The Bohemian Girl,” in Sydney. liis voice
first came under notice when he was a choir
boy at St. Peter’s, Melbourne. He worked
at that time as a clerk at Throckmorton’s,
on the wharf. (Mrs. Throckmorton was the leader
of Society, preceding Mrs. Malleson.) Beaumont
had reached some degree of public notice when the
Musical Union, a rival to the Philharmonic, gave
“The Messiah” at Christmas. Squires was to have
sung the tenor, but had a cold or something. The
manager came forward with: “Ladies and Gentle-
mell) —We have with us Mr. Edward Armes Beau-
mont, the young Australian Sims Reeves. He has
kindly consented to sing the tenor.” Beaumont de-
clares that this was the first notification he got.
Nevertheless, he complied. Next day he was famous.
He accepted an engagement from R. S. Smythe, to
travel with Poussard, the violinist; Douay, the
’cellist; and Miss Amelia Bailey (Mrs. Smythe), the
soprano. After returning from Maoriland, he was
laid up with a sprained knee. Fred Lyster came to
him with the music of Thaddeus, and said: “Here s
the thing, my boy. Study this while you are on
your back.” And he did ; and that is how he came
to appear in Sydney.
“Groper”: “Martin Luther” (B. 20/7/’ll) either
knows little about the Fisk Jubilee Singers, or else
he holds back what he does know. They started
out to make up (say) £5OOO, thinking they had a
gigantic job in front, but before they had got
through the English provinces they had raised that
£5OOO and another lot on top of it, and still had
nearly half the tour to do. Having raised for the
’Varsity all that was needed and more, they an-
nounced that they would return and do a bit for
themselves. There was no running under false pre-
tences with them, and their work was so fine that
they deserved every shilling they scooped.

nuated at the Shaftesbury Theatre, London. It had
reached the ripe old age of 2| years.
“Harrison O.”: I made lately a few well-chosen
remarks regarding the horrible habit of wedging
unnecessary acts into plays, and now a further lllus-
tration is supplied by “The Witness for the De-
fence.” In the first act, a wife who is bullied and
beaten, injects a small but adequate quantity of
lead into her husband’s carcase. In the interval we
go outside and see about the usual dog, _ and wonder
what will happen in the next act. Nothing happens,
except that a young man who is in love with the
lady tells his uncle as many of the circumstances
of the ease as he is acquainted with. We carry out
some further negotiations in regard to the quadru-
ped, and return for the third act. In this the lady
herself supplies a complete history of the whole
affair to the lawyer, whose evidence secured her
acquittal on the capital charge. The Gent. One
announces that she must unfold the _ narrative to her
voung man, and to the doing of this the fourth act
is devoted. All through the play the characters
keep on telling one another things they al-
ready know, or things the audience already knows.
There is some dramatic interest in the last act, since
the question arises: Will the lady be forgiven by her
adorer? But the two middle acts are absolutely
unnecessary, and consequently extremely dreary.

“C Ockney” : Still they come, the actor-dramatists.
(Come to think of it, we should be badly off for.plays
without them.) The latest is A. E. W. Mason, with his
“Witness for the Defence.” As a mummer the future
novelist, playwright and M.P. studied stagecraft
with F R. Benson’s Sliakspearian troupe, and for a
while with Edward Compton’s Old Comedy Co. (Ben-
son and Compton, by the way, are still travelling
the English provinces with the same goods—Compton
has been at it for 30 years!) Mason turned his
early experience to good account; Shakspeare and old
comedy should not be a bad diet for _ the embryo
playwright. His first success as a novelist was, nat-
urally enough, a “costume” drama in book form -
“The Courtship of Morrice Buckler.” Since then he
has published over a dozen novels, good, bad and
indifferent, in all of which, however, the plot, the
construction and the dramatic grip betray the hand
of the writer for the stage. It is something of a coin-
cidence that the two Irvings (Ethel and “H. 8.”)
were playing in Mason plays at the same time re-
cently in London, Ethel in her present Australian
production, and “H. 8.” in “Princess Clementina.”
Robert Grieg, an Australian, who did some sound
character-acting with the dismantled Hugh Ward
Co has joined Allan Hamilton’s “Beauty and the
Barge” crowd. As one result of the theatrical com-
bine the Australian will seemingly have to depend
for a shop almost entirely on either Hamilton, Plim-
mer and Denniston, or Bilanderson. The shows
controlled by these people have lapped up a dozen
capable artists of local extraction during the past
month.
“0.K.” : Astonishing to find Melbourne Argus say-
ing of Melba in “Traviata” that “there was curi-
osity as to her powers as an actress, as was to be
expected She portrayed the character with a
poignancy that few were prepared for.” Now, curi-
osity followed by great surprise implies a previous
ignorance of what was to be expected; yet Melba
appeared as Camille at Melbourne Princess’s nine
years ago. Three acts of “Traviata” were played
three times, Mrs. Slapoffski, Lempriere Pringle,
Louis Arens, Bensua(le and Charles Rose being the
other principals. During the same short season
Melba sang twice in two acts of “Faust” (in
French), one act of “Rigoletto,” and the Mad Scene
from "Lucia.” So her powers as an actress are no
new revelation to the Commonwealth ; and the mana-
gerial credit for the first revelation is due to George
Musgrove.
“Harrison 0” : Most quaint has been the evolution
of Sherlock Holmes. Conan Doyle, an Englishman,
depicted him as a calm, frozen person—a type much
admired by Bull, who is himself one of the most
jumpy of blokes. When a play was constructed
around S. Holmes, the reasoning machine, all brain
and no heart, slithered into love, and developed into
a sentimental humbug. Now, in “The Speckled
Band,” we meet a cove whom we feel convinced
would introduce himself as Sherlock S. Holmes, of
N’Yark City, U.S.A. —a good-natured Yankee humor-
ist, who appears more anxious to score cheap verbal
victories over his opponents than to have those
opponents quodded.
“8.J.H.0.”: Gregan McMahon will make his second
appeal to the lovers of the literary drama towards
the end of this month. On the 25th, and again
three nights later, Shaw’s “Candida” will tell her
reverend husband what every woman thinks she
knows; on October 2 the Repertory people will do
their best with Calderon’s sociological comedy, “The
Fountain” ; and for two nights during the following
week performances of Granville Barker’s “The Voy-
sey Inheritance” will be given. McMahon’s last
season was well worth while, though it is doubtful
whether among his band of mummeresses was one
capable of doing justice to Candida. Still, we may
hope for the best, and rest assured that the worst
won’t be awful.
“C. Ockney”: If “0.K.” (B. 24/8/’ll) thinks
that “Snell,” by simply making a statement and
inviting me to contradict it, “scores a point,” he is
welcome to his opinion. For his otherwise rational
contribution to the mimicry discussion, I, personally,
am grateful—he, evidently speaking with knowledge,
bears out everything I have had to say on the sub-
ject. “0.K.” has but to turn to my first “Poverty
Point” par. (B. 22/6/’ll), and he will find these
words: “This ‘profitable industry’ has, of course,
been going on for more years than I care to count.
...Every ‘fat’ part that has scored a big hit in
London has to be copied in every particular by the
mummer who is selected to play it in Australia.
The managements insist upon it.” Now, “0.K.”
substantiates all this by actually citing the case of
a prominent actor-manager (Frank Thornton), who,
when he played Dick Phenyl in “Sweet Lavender,”
gave “a close imitation of the voice and mannerr
isms” of Edward Terry, the original creator (Thorn-
ton, by the way, is not the only manager who has
been caught at it!). If, then, managers insist upon
it, and, when they act, practise slavish imitation,
how in the name of common-sense can the unfortu-
nate mummer (wljo, unless he be quite mad, looks
after his bread and butter first thing) be blamed?
Yet “Snell” (so he says) will continue to throw
bricks at him. Thornton copied Terry because he
evidently thought it the best thing to do if he
wanted to make good ; and, at the risk of once
again being accused of “defending the game,” I
submit he did right. Pinero, who knew Terry’s
mannerisms and idiosyncrasies as an actor (which
were most marked) to a nicety (having indeed pre-
viously exploited them in two comedies, “The
Rocket” and “In Chancerv”), built Dick Phenyl to
fit Edward Terry and nobody else. Terry scored an
enormous hit in the part and Thornton could not
help but copy him if he wanted to reproduce his
success. Ought he (Thornton) to have had one of
“Snell’s” bricks when he brought out the play to
Australia? Another question. When mummers imi-
tate, why should they thereupon be charged
with inability to do original work? “0.K.”
blames and belittles Leslie Holland for copy-
ing George Grossmitli, jun., in a musical
nnmedv part, yet, a“ a nressman, “0.K.”
had probablv seen Holland only a few months
before playing a straight light-comedy role
in “The Brass Bottle.” Is the mummer not entitled
to anv commendation for the different characterisa-
tions?' Verily, the actor-man might exclaim with
Dick Phenyl: “Blame, blame, blame! But praise
—oh, dear no!” And, in this connection, let it not
be forgotten that Grossmith, in addition to being
one of the leading comedians of the Gaiety, is in
manv plays there his own author. Grossmith, the
author, might well be pardoned if he thought that
Grossmith’s performance, of what has come to be
described as a “Grossmith part,” should be played in
the Grossmith manner, and in no other way.
The, movement for building a “Marshall-Hall
Conservatorium of Music” in Melbourne commands
sympathy on Marshall-Hall’s account only. He
started his conservatorium in a hired building, what
time he was the paid Professor of Music at the
University, and for love, not money, he advanced
the cause of Art, and put himself with a full
orchestra at its head. When he had to retire from
the University chair, and leave the salary to an-
other and a milder Professor, Hall fell practically
into the hands of a nretentious syndicate of patrons,
who “ran” him as the elders of a small religious '
sect run their parson. These appalling financiers of
the Cause seem to have alienated the working
musicians who formerly stood by Marshall-Hall in
his orchestral concert enterprise. The workers have
now established a Musicians’ Union and a Vic. Pro-
fessional Orchestra whose concerts get no support
from the pretentious patrons of Art. In fact, the
gilded push seems bent on “queering the pitch of
the V.P.O. As the matter now stands, the gilded
ones are the nobles who maintain the monarchy,
whilst the members of the Musical Union are rebels
ag’in the Government. Marshall-Hall himself may
have all the personal qualities to make a good in-
stitutional monarch, but he is ruled by a bumptious
bureaucracy which might dethrone him at any
time, and replace him with somebody who would
have no claim to sympathy whatever. If Melbourne
is to have a permanent conservatorium of music,
with plentv of room for expansion, it should surely
be provided by the local Government, and its man-
agement should be in perfect harmony with the
local musical trades union.
A LUST FOR FACTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sydney Protestant Defence Association and its Ir-
relevant H. Gainford strongly object to the with-
drawal of the biograph pictures of Sextus the Fifth.
According to the morning paper, “The Council fur-
ther expresses both its surprise and indignation at the
West’s Picture Company yielding to Papal clerical
pressure by withdrawing the film entitled Sextus \ ~
thus depriving the Australian public of seeing that
historical picture. This Council still further pro-
tests against the fact that the overwhelming majority
—over three-fourths of the people—are denied a pic-
ture because of the Papal ecclesiastical threat.” The
Irrelevant Gainford later said that “the fact that the
Catholics resent the picture is one of the greatest
evidences that it is historically true.”
“We hate the Papacy,
We scorn the Vatican;
The blessed Jasper Sea
’S for no It-al-ian !
We’d give the Cartherlicks the rope—
The awful scoundrels stole our Pope ! !
“Our Pope is truly bad —
Borgia and Deeming mixed—
That’s why we feel so mad !
A cleanly Pius Sixt
Is naught to us but opium dope.
The Pope we want’s an awful Pope!
“That it’s historical
’S a virtue in our eyes,
The Papists show their mal-
A-fides by their cries.
We know him well from heel to cope,
His character’s made him our Pope!
“No Francis of the birds,
No Camaldoli saint,
No priest of quiet words,
Nor free from worldly taint
We want —our sure and certain hope
Is on a really wicked Pope! ”
VICTORIA.
MARTIN LUTHER.
THE GALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
H. B. Irving, talking, by special request, to the
boys of Melbourne Church of England Grammar
School, earnestly advised the lads not to think of
entering the theatrical profession, unless they had
“a clear and distinct call.”
So, little boy, do not aspire
To set the River Thames a-fire
As Hamlet, that funereal Dane,
Unless your call’s so clear and plain
You’ll not confound
It with the milkman’s, on his round.
Don’t lie awake for fear you’ll lose
The whispered summons of the Muse.
Her voice is stirring as a bell.
Some actors here have heard it well,
Though many swear
No sound disturbed the drowsy air.
The call is clear. Alas! of late
The hearer can’t discriminate.
He’s called upon the stage he knows,
And forth to play Othello goes,
When it but means
He’s called to meekly move the scenes,
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
“Mere Bloke” : One of Australia’s staunch-
est barriers against the subversive tenden-
cies of mob rule is, undoubtedly, the M.L.C.
The M.L.C. stands for all that this God-for-
saken country has of cultivated intelligence.
But the note of intellectual superiority may
be over-stressed, and G. J. Crooke, M.L.C.,
of Gippsland (Vic.), erred in this fashion
last week. His purpose was to express sym-
pathy with the Sale borough council in its
agitation for an improved railway service to
Rosedale. He wrote :
My experience is that whilst the present manage-
ment —or rather mismanagement—of the railway
continues, it is quite useless to ask for or anticioate
any improvement in our antiquated system. I have
already informed the Department of my opinion,
which is not confined to Gippsland, and which I
hope will soon grow strong enough to get a shift on.
Then we will get that to which we are entitled.
Under the existing Act, members have no right to
make any requests.
How stingingly the illiterate toiler is
taunted in his futile gropings after the
meaning of the above! He cannot parse it;
nor, indeed, can anyone else. Being the
literature of an Upper House, it is neces-
sarily Greek to the commoner and caviare
to the general.
From an Adelaide critic:—
Blamire Young has opened an exhibition of his
pictures at the Art Society’s new rooms, lent to
him for the purpose. Adelaide has hitherto known
him only by two paintings in the Art Gallery—
Chaucer and an apprentice, and a fat, broken-down
music-master; both are heavy, powerful, a kind of
glorified scene-painting. The surprise at finding
Blamire’s talent to be so various is correspondingly
great. This is hardly a “one-man” show at all.
One would say four men had been at work. He can
paint washerwomen in the old familiar style—one of
these was the first to acquire the Red Label of
honor ; or misty seashores, with graceful nudes half-
hidden in the mist; or fantastic figures (often in
crinolines) that somehow seem to match a G.K.
Chesterton fantasy; or clever brown studies, as in
the mining town of Walhalla, seen nestling in its
gully by night—one of the best things in the show.
He can give water and wharves in the manner of
Will Ashton, or he can dash in his scenery in the
right impressionist style, with heavy storm-clouds,
such as may rarely be seen in Nature. (Those
storm-clouds seem to be becoming a habit with him.)
His coloring is delightful, and his show is already
popular. The financial aspect of it is a different
question. Most of the money in S.A., these times,
belongs to the farmers, and their buying runs rather
to pianos, phonographs and motor-cars than to
paintings.
Two sober facts that have no earthly con-
nection with each other have been dragged
into close relationship by some insignificant
but ultra-“loyal” members of Melbourne’s
Board of Shirks. Fact 1 is that that cum-
bersome body was appointed to carry out and
maintain the city’s sewerage system, and to
keep it adequately supplied with water. It
does neither of these things well, but that is
by the way. Fact 2 is that the Coronation
of George, May’s husband, was accomplished
months ago, and is now almost forgotten.
The connecting link between the two is that
the Board has just waited on Governor-
General Denman and handed him an address
congratulating the aforesaid George on the
above-mentioned Coronation, which address
he, Denman, is desired to transmit. It will
probably be handed to George’s head flunkey
to be shied under the copper, but that is also
by the way. The address, which took some
months of one of the employees’ time, is in-
scribed (addresses are never written) on
ivory white vellum, and bears, besides some
grovellous remarks, the coats of arms of (1)
George, previously referred to; (2)the State
of Premier John Weary; (3) the Board or
Plank itself: and (4 to 25 inclusive) those
of the 22 municipalities composing the Plank.
It is a sweet memento of the dead past, and
must be soothing to those who pay the heavy
sewerage rates; but citizens who have to
drink muddy water and precious little of
that, and who occasionally get a worm out
of the tap. begin to understand how the
worms breed so freely.

“Harrison O.” : It is some time since as
much blither, bosh and bunkum has been
talked as in connection with the case of Hop
Boon Gooey, the Chow who brought his wife
out here on the distinct understanding that
she was to return in six months’ time, who
had the period extended, and who is now
dropping tears over the cauliflowers because
she is not to be allowed to remain here per-
manently in defiance of the law of the White
Man’s Land. A deputation of parsons and
other barrackers for a Piebald Australia
talked a lot of maudlin rot about separating
husband and wife, and “putting apart those
whom God has joined together,” and drew
touching word pictures of H. P. Gooey’s
grief at being separated from his spouse.
However, their appeal was robbed of a good
deal of its pathos when it was announced
that Gooey had been given permission 10
years ago to bring out a missus (either the
present one or another), and had kept on
thinking about the matter for over nine
years before taking any action. Under the
circumstances, the touching picture of the
Chinese Romeo almost crazy with grief at
being parted from his Juliet does not look
as convincing as it might be. He seems a
calm, unimpassioned sort of Romeo.
* * * *
“C.I1.”: The fact is reported that some
clergymen turned out and did hard labor
among the strike-breakers at Manchester
(England). Well, let us consider their case
more in sorrow than in anger. The poor
devil of a sky-pilot must keep solid with the
affluent classes, who have “livings” to dis-
pose of, though some of these should be
called “starvings.” In England and Wales
there are over 500 full-blown Anglican clerics
receiving less than £75 annually per sin-
shifter. These are not curates —the down-
trodden curate may get anything down to
ss. per week, and a dubious egg for break-
fast —but the genuine article. To-day the
Congregational Union in Britain is scraping
together a fund of £250,000, in the hope of
being able to fix a minimum wage of £l2O
per year for its preachers. More than one-
tenth of the Baptist ministers never climb
the giddy financial Alp of £75 a year, and
nearly half don’t attain to £l5O. The Welsh
Baptist preacher seldom reaches £lOO, and
often he languishes in the abyss of £5O or
even less. So the superabundant cleric
hangs humbly on to the tail of the rich,
which is often a suppressed tail and very
difficult to hang on to- —not because he has
much hope of getting more, but because he is
in great dread lest he should get even less.

Brutal frankness of a N. S. Wales factory
inspector as set out in the department’s
annual report recently issued: —
Another case was that of a Chinese fruiterer who
employed two young Chinamen, who informed me
they were paid 7s. 6d. per week and their keep ;
also that they did not get any half-holiday. When
the proprietor came into the shop he told me they
were his partners. He could not produce any regis-
tration of his firm, or any deed of partnership.
Action was taken, the case was defended, and
although the defendant could not produce any docu-
ments to prove his statement, the magistrate dis-
missed the case.
Whaffor?
“The Undertaker” : It seems impossible for
public talkers —and a good many public
writers, too —to say that anybody is Dead.
In his House of Rep.’s maiden speech,
Frank Brennan referred to the “demise” of
Harry Beard, his predecessor in Batman,
and also remarked that G. B. Edwards, of
North Sydney, had “passed away.” Then this
deponent strayed into the Vic. Legislative
Assembly, where Norman Bayles, M.L.A.,
fought with the problem of how to indicate
that a man was dead, without saying so. The
dead man had “passed into the Unknown.”
and had “gone into another sphere” (he said
“better sphere” at first, but hastily cor-
rected himself), and had “departed from
among us.” What a pity that so few people
say that Dead is Dead, for there is no other
word. Two things—Life, Death; Death,
Life —as in Victor Daley’s poem. Why
boggle about it? But even a medical man
at an inquest will say flatulently, “Life was
extinct.” When I’m buried (or cremated, if
Randolph Bedford is Dictator by then), I’m
not going to be Deceased, or Departed or
Life-Extinguished. I’m going to be adver-
tised as DEAD I
RIOT.

Vic. Caledonians, arguing eloquently for
the retention of the kilt as the uniform of
their particular regiment, assured Senator
Pearce that, on the score of cheapness, there
is much to be said, as the Scotch petticoat
will last 10 years. But, really, the chief vir-
tue of the kilt is that it doesn’t bag at the
knees. Also, it never seems to clamor for
patches at the back.

During the visit of the King and Queen
to Ireland, most of the local papers, even the
Nationalist ones, always started George s
pronoun with a capital “II,” and did the
same for Mary. “The people,” said one elo-
quent publication, “notwithstanding politicB
misunderstandings, love Himself and Her-
self.” The habit of putting a tower in front
of the pronoun used to apply only to the
Deity, but times or printers have changed.
THE CRY THAT REACHED HIS VEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Friday.—George Reid, Commonwealth
High Commissioner, is inaugurating a scheme for
actively advertising Australian products in France,
Austria, Switzerland, and Belgium, in view of the
dear food” outcry.
He is arranging also for the insertion of many
illustrated articles in French newspapers, emphasis-
ing that boats with Australian meat shipments call
regularly at Marseilles.— Cable.
MEAT!
Gorge has flung aside his napkin, and is scrambling
' to his feet!
Now the waiters fly before him, dropping bottles,
corks and dishes,
And the floor is simply littered with the entrees,
joints and fishes ;
There’s a wrangle in the kitchen —there’s disorder in
the hall,
Where the porter with his whistle sounds the urgent
motor call;
For the banquet is deserted and the chef is quite
insane—
He is peppering a turkey with a new insectibane!
And a parched and spectral tripe-hound on the table
has a feed,
While he wonders what disaster has disturbed his
owner Greid;
To a bone of utter sweetness his sad countenance is
glued,
When his master flies to answer an appeal for cheaper
food.
FOOD!I
How could any man resist it who with banquets is
imbued ?
Now the sympathetic waistcoat with its job of mercy
hurries,
While the dry and mournful tripe-hound on the
table snarls and worries;
And no carrier of dishes rushes in on tender feet.
With his limp and grimy serviette to thrash him
from the treat;
They are crowded in the hall way—-they are jumping
on the pave,
As with crumbs upon his waistcoat, Gorge is start-
ing off to save
Ev’ry empty vest in Europe with a simple ad. and
neat;
That each liner’s freezing-chamber is chock-full of
frozen meat;
Over other urgent matters Gorge will banquet,
sleep and brood ;
But he tramoles on the waiters at the howl for
cheaper food.
GREID ! ! I
Pie Commissioner in London and a man all cooks
must heed !
Art and Science, Commerce, Letters on his splendid
doorstep lurking,
Sniff the heavy fumes of dinner and observe Sur-
gorge is working.
See the butler, like a high-priest in a well-estab-
lished shrine,
In a stately manner passing with his offerings of
wine,
None may stop the dinner-service when he’s in a
working mood,
Save, by yelling at the window, “Hey, Surgorge!
We’re short of food!”
Comes a croaking cry of horror and a rush of flat-
tened feet,
And Surgorge bursts through the waiters, and,
crumb-powdered, gains the street,
From the turkey, wme and truffles he comes charg-
ing down the hall,
When above old London’s champing he hears fellow-
waistcoats call.

ROSE, BLUE AND GOLD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
Rose and blue and gold
Go to make my sweet!
Tender words her lips unfold,
Tender thoughts her eyes have told,
Oh, her lips are sweet!
Golden is her hair —
Golden like a crown —
And the glow of Youth is fair
On her cheek as roses rare.
Oh ! her little feet!
Rose and blue and gold
Go to make my sweet.

Thus “Conscript’s Mother” in S. M.
Herald last week, in protest against her
“15-year-old boy being taught to kill his
fellow-men” ‘. —
I have watched over him and tended him, and
now he has to turn out to this. To learn to fight
for what? We have nothing to fight for in this
country. What working man has?
Yet the poorest and most unpatriotic work-
ing-man has something to fight for; for in-
stance, the honor of his women folks. If
“Conscript’s Mother” really holds that in-
vasion’s only horror consists in the destruc-
tion of property, there is. something gravely
wrong either with her intelligence or her
morals. And the frequent statement that
the working man in Australia has nothing to
fight for is sheer folly, anyhow. The people
who make it haven’t the beginning of an
idea what immeasurable depths lie below
them, and how poor they can become before
they have nothing to fight for. “Conscript’s
Mother” may think she has struck bottom,
but it would probably take her years of
travel and invest'cation to even faintly
realise what bottom is.

Surf-bathing as a counter attraction to church-
going was alluded to yesterday by the Rev. T.
Morgan, of the McNeil Memorial Church, Waverley.
...“I am sorry to say,” observed the reverend
gentleman, “that not more than 10 out of every 100
young people growing up to manhood and woman-
hood —I am speaking now of the Protestant popula-
tion—go into the churches. There are more people
on Bondi Beach on a Sunday morning, I think, than
in all the churches in the eastern suburbs. It is a
common thing to see men and women going out on
Sunday morning for pleasure, and taking all their
children with them. They are bringing their
children up in ungodliness.”— Sydney daily paper ,
Parson (popping up in the surf): “Dearly
beloved breth'ren, the Scripture moveih us
in sundry places,” etc., etc.

A Passing Bloke comments: —
Dr. Billy Maloney’s young daughter burst into the
limelight the other day as an advocate of the Tele-
phone Girl. She opened the new Exchange in Mel-
bourne by reading a little speech of protest against
the overworking of switchboard operators. Scores
of her suffering sistern were at work around the Ex-
change hall at the time, and Miss Maloney suggested
that their employment was “injurious to the ner-
vous system.” With ingenuous emphasis she spoke
of herself as “being only a girl,” the tacit sugges-
tion being that girls are specially subject to switch-
board nervousness. Then why are any of them em-
ployed on a job which seemingly would be less in-
jurious, or not at all injurious, to young men?
Common-sense (which is not always arrogant stu-
pidity) has grown weary of the woes of women
workers in the P.M.G.’s department. If their sex
demands particular consideration for them, why are
they there at all? Either they are well fitted for
their job, or unfitted, and if they are fitted for it,
then the sex question is quite out of place in their
periodical Book of Lamentations. Anyhow, the com-
munity at large doesn’t ask for “weak women” to
be doing any sort of Government work which could
be performed by strong men with less injury to the
public health. In fact, the community at large is
apt to wonder whv so many women are having their
nerves racked in the P.M.G.’s department, the com-
munity being not fully aware of the fact that the
tendency of every modern Government is to provide
as much employment as possible for the womankind
of its old friend, Political Influence. #
Billjim, down for the Show, is mostly re-
garded as the natural diet of the city spielers,
but sometimes the boot adorns the other leg.
During Melbourne’s annual Cadge week one
of him, having spent half a day at Fleming-
ton, and bitten Weary Watt’s ear for a new
road or bridge or something, bethought him of
the familiar backblocks. He had lost or
hypothecated his return ticket, but that
didn’t worry him. Going into a bike shop
he expressed a desire to buy a reliable motor-
bike, but insisted on a trial. The shop-
keeper was agreeable, and handed him a
machine; also, he personally superintended
the trial on a faster jigger. They went out
along the Sydney-road, and were half-way
home again, when William reckoned his
machine was hardly swift enough. The
trusting bike-man exchanged vehicles, so
that he was on the slow contraption, and
Jimbill on the fast one. Then William-
james went away at such a pace that the
other lost sight of him —and has kept on
doing so ever since. But Billiam didu t
want to steal the machine; he only wanted
to get home. So a few days later the bike-
seller . got a note from a Mallee township,
telling him to call at Spencer-street station
for his weapon, which Bill had sent back
per passenger train at “collect” rates. It
cost Bs. 6d., and the tyres were wrecks, the
frame joints and spokes loose, and the engine
almost worn out, but the handles were all
right. # #
The most mysterious circumstance about
Melbourne’s latest murder case or disappear-
ance mystery, whichever it is, is the failure
of detective Macmanamanammy to rush to
the front. Day by day the public was re-
galed with accounts of the doings of those
wondrous sleuths, Murphy, O’Dynamite,
Mac Moses, Mac Patrick, Columbus Johnson
and the rest. But for some inscrutable rea-
son, the greatest and most unspellable gepius
of them all failed to heave up on the horizon.
“Locust” : Referring to a par. about “the
strenuous ‘Jimmie Pannikin’ ” (long may he
stren.!) having school-grounds planted with
fruit trees, it is interesting to know that
miles and miles of German country roads
are improved by rows of fruit trees. In
that country, however, there is a very highly
developed civic sentiment; and that fruit re-
mains unplucked until ripe. Remembering
my own schoolboy days, I should say that —
unless the Australboy has changed—those
trees of “Jimmie Pannikin’s” could be relied
upon to produce aches in wide concentric
circles. Still there is no reason why we
shouldn’t cultivate civic sentiment along
with fruit trees. I remember, indeed, the
time when it would have been thought ab-
surd to grow flowers unprotected in un-
fenced Australian parks.
Some of the sickest sailormen afloat are
probably the crew of the lost Yankee ship
Puritan. Three days after they took to the
boats they sighted an island, but the mate
remembered some hair-raising cannibal yarns
he had read as a boy, and assured the crew
that a landing there would mean a big feast,
with themselves figuring on the bill of fare.
They pulled their belts a hole or two tighter
and sneaked past with fearful scannings of
the horizon for the cannibal chief’s canoes,
and kept their course for another 10 days of
misery. Finally, when they were beginning
to feel some sympathy for the feelings of
even cannibals, they sighted another island
in ,the same group, and decided that, even
if they were eaten there, they’d probably be
fed up on a few square meals first, and that
was worth it, so they ran the boat ashore.
When they were told that they couldn’t find
a cannibal with a fine tooth comb in all the
Eastern Pacifie, and that if they’d landed at
the first island they would have been met by
the Lord Mayor with an address of welcome
and the Municipal Band playing “Hail
Columbia,” and the Corporation cook
hastily putting on the turtle soup to warm
up for them, they made one or two remarks
to that mate. The mate had to admit sadly
that, when he thought of the 700 or 800
miles they had frantically fled away from
fried fish and turtle steak, they couldn’t say
anything that hurt as much as his own feel-
ingS ’ * * * . *
“Malbon” : Observe that a Vic. M.P. has
a Daylight Saving Bill on the stocks. The
Bulletin says there “is something to re-
commend” Outtrim’s idea. But why make
all this elaborate fuss, putting clocks on in this
month, and putting them back again some
other month 7 As a case in point, the Queens-
land Railway Department closes its goods
sheds at 5 p.m. in winter and at 5.30 p.m. in
summer, or thereabouts, and isn’t so puerile
as to pretend that the time is 5 o’clock,
when it is half an hour nearer the schnapps
and bitters that come before dinner.
* * * *
Some people go on strange pilgrimages.
As a case in point, an old man was recently
found at Tatura (Vic.) —a very, very old
and dirty man and nearly blind. He had
collapsed from exhaustion in a pool of water.
This ancient hoof-traveller was worth about
£1560, yet he was plugging in rags over a
stretch of 200 miles or more with Bendigo
for his destination. And he wasn’t looking
for his long-lost son or the holy grail, nor
was he on the track of the man who had
stolen his wife : the deplorable old person’s
destination was just the shop of a common-
place Chinese herbalist.
An unusual prosecution was undertaken
at Sydney in the Central Criminal Court a
few days ago, when the Newcastle and Hun-
ter River Shipping (jo. was asked to be up-
standing and either remove a dark suspicion
that the steamer Hunter had been sent to
sea in an unseaworthy state or take the
consequences. The allegations, put briefly,
were that the vessel ran or jumped upon a
natural monument in the Hunter river last
October. It climbed off, and, without being-
docked, was loaded up with 700 excursionists
and despatched to the Hawkesbury. But,
after it had finished the job, the officers be-
gan to have doubts and other portable pro-
perty. And these doubts grew into a cer-
tainty that was calculated to make the jaw
drop when they docked the Hunter and
found a big hole through which David Jones
was doubtless trying to crawl for all he was
worth. Happily, the hole was too small to
fully admit Jones, or Jones, much fattened
by the eating of wrecks, was too big for the
hole; still the hole was a disquieting cir-
cumstance, especially combined with the
knowledge that Jones was outside. Various
experts declared that the orifice didn’t, in
itself, make the ship unseaworthy, but they
all seemed to imply that they didn’t con-
sider it an advantage. It appeared, how-
ever, that the co., regarded as a co., didn’t
know that its ship was plugging up and
down the coast while afflicted with submar-
ine ventilation, so the hole blew over, so to
speak. It was a fairly exciting gap while it
lasted, but the incident, as diplomatists say,
is now closed. So is the hole.
Colonel Barney, and the story of what he
noted and what he didn’t note at the time
of his trip to Gladstone (Q.) in the very
long ago, are given a prominent place in the
re-awakened newspaper controversy re the
Spanish ship that is supposed to have per-
ished there in 1596. Barney’s testimony
either way is absolutely worthless. He was
careless of everything but himself, his family
anu belongings. The Lord Auckland, in
which he sailed from Sydney to take charge
of the new colony of North Australia (to be
peopled by prisoners from Pentonville)
grounded on a shoal while abreast of Facing
Island. Then Barney rose in haste, had his
portable goods put into a boat, got in him-
selr, hauled in his wife, and fled to the shore.
There he lay down and went to sleep, like
George Reid. He took no part in directing
or helping those on board, but when all was
announced safe, he and his family returned
to the ship. Some days later he issued a
manuscript Government Gazette proclaim-
ing the establishment of the new colony of
North Australia, and then he lay down again
exhausted with the effort. He was a most
horizontal colonel. Finding no control over
them, the military got drunk. The S. M.
Herald of that day denounced Barney, and
Robert Lowe said things about him that
even a dog would resent. Finally the flat
warrior threw up the sponge, and the enter-
prise was abandoned. As an authority upon
the alleged Spanish occupation of Gladstone
he is utterly valueless, and he doesn’t seem
worth much on any subject.

to exercise on behalf of the general welfare. —S.A.

THE BENDING RACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Saturday, 1.30 p.m.—Bending Race.”— Vic.
Show’s programme.
I watched the horsemen twist around,
Within, and past each steady post;
Whichever pony there was found
The twistiest, had the winner’s boast
And going thence to have a drink
I grew reflective o’er tmy beer,
And truly couldn’t help but think
Of Alfred Deakin’s bright career.
Men—facts —were like those posts to him :
Let one resist, or two deride,
Or three start howling with a vim—
Then Alfred nimbly skipped aside.
He skipped to left, he skipped to right.
To lofty hopes, to bargains base,
He shied at darkness and at light,
And ran a perfect Bending Race.
Too deft in sliding out of shock,
Too swift to mix it up in strife,
He would not take a lusty knock
In that hard steeplechase called Life.
With broken necks too many lie,
So fierce and fast the gallop goes!
And Alfred learned to swerve and shy
From foes to friends, from friends to foes.
There’s some will ride a hurdle down
Where others jump it high and clean,
And though it’s not the best renown,
Good winners with cut legs I’ve seen.
Take Old Bill Lyne—-he used to race
Much like a camel with a hump;
All scarred and bruised he got his place—
But Alfred always'dodged his jump.
Grand Nationals will yet be run
With big, brave horses in the lead,
And stakes be paid, and bets be won —
But Alfred sits and takes no heed.
For in a' little niche of fame
He finds himself a little place,
Wherein will stand inscribed his name —
The winner of the Bending Race.
VICTORIA.
C.

“Settled”: “Phoenix” (B. 10/8/’ll) may
know a thing or two about the N.S.W. Northern
Rivers stinging-tree, but he certainly knows nothing
about the one that grows in Queensland. In no way
do their leaves resemble those of a mulberry. For one
thing, they are quite heart-shaped. The sting is so
terrible that nothing alive in Queensland will pur-
posely rub up against it, and no sane cow would
dream of eating the foliage. I have seen working
bullocks go almost mad after coming in contact with
it, and I have been kicked yards while trying to
extract the juice from the working bullock’s sister
after she had met with the same sort of accident.
The effect is so severe on the goddess that the teats
go blind after a few stings; and many good cows
have found their way to the butcher’s in consequence.
The only living thing I have seen on the vegetable
is the big soldier ant; and I think this chap wor-
ships it on account of its ability to bite, as though
it were an army of “soldiers” rolled into one.
“Dossett”: All I can tell “Phoenix” (B.
10/8/’ll) about the stinging-tree is the most im-
portant thing to know. If he ever comes in contact
with it, liquid ammonia, freely applied, will enable
him to lower a pint of beer without turning somer-
saults. I’ve proved it.

“9-3-11": Re pars. (B. 17/8/’ll) on the
death adder. One Abo-fellow asserts that the viper
is only found in rocky country. Another says he
loves to play in the dust on a floury road. Both
wrong. The death adder is found mostly in poor
forest country and on the grassy headlands along the
coast; whether there are rocks or not is only a
matter of detail, for the adder caretli not. The sand
is the place where he loves to play. I have killed
death adders measuring 3ft. in length in the Tweed
River District (N.S.W.). “Hassan” asks isn’t 7yds.
a little over the odds for the jump of an adder?
No; the large ones can jump that distance without
much trouble—l have seen them do a little more.
The reason he is quite still until pushed is that he
is deaf and very dull of eyesight. As soon as he
is touched, he strikes the nearest object he sees in
the direction from which the touch came; thus if
you stand on the south side and touch him on the
north side with a crooked stick, he springs at any-
thing he sees on the north, or, if nothing else offers,
at the air, and, consequently, right away from the
person manipulating the stick.
“Montalex” escapes the death that other
men have died: —
“Pride goeth before destruction.” I knew all
about the venomous properties of the little red-
backed spider, known in Maori land as the “katipo ;
and when a friend pointed out one the other day,
and asked if it were the real Mackay, I said, “You
bet! Fetch a little phial and a cork, and see me
get him.” But I didn’t. He got me! There’s a
knack of smartly nipping the little demon up be-
tween finger and thumb, and popping him into limbo
before he knows what’s happening. I had done the
trick often, but this time I must have been the tenth
of a second too slow. A pang shot from thumb to
shoulder. I “swore a prayer or two” and danced a
weird fandango until the pain let-up on me, which
it did in about half a minute. But, then, just as 1
was beginning to hope that the circus was happily
over, the agony hit me suddenly harder than ever,
and for the next three hours—minutes, I mean —X
executed corantos, lavoltas, sarabands, boleros, eachu-
cas, and all the rest of ’em. After that the intervals
became longer and the pain less poignant, but next
morning my arm was puffed and purple from wrist
to shoulder, and there was a big swelling in the
armpit that drove me to a doctor, and kept me at
him for a fortnight. He talked about septicaemia,
and said I was lucky. Dunno about the luck, but I
do know that, though I have been stung by most
kinds of bees, wasps and ants—including that holy
terror, the sumpit-api of Malaysia—-I give easily the
first prize for agony-production to little Monsieur
Rouge-et-Noir.
“Vagabond”: I’ve seen a-plenty nigger
mothers who went one better than the Solomon Island-
ers mentioned by “Arlo” (B. 10/8/’ll). He says that
a few days after the baby is found among the cab-
bages or the poetical island equivalent, the .mother
girds on her spade and hoe; and hies forth to dig
the spuds, etc. The African nig. beats that. It is a
common thing there for the mother to go on work-
ing in the fields till within an hour or so of the
trouble, to retire gracefully, and within another hour
or two get Back to the Land and bog in to work
again, leaving the new arrival under a scrap of
shade. The buck nigger in those parts doesn’t en-
courage spells-off on any excuse whatever. He buys
the ladies for so many cows down, and the market
value of the candidate is mainly decided on two
things—her probable horse-power in the fields and
the likelihood of her producing children, the latter
figured out on appearance and family records. Looks
don’t cut much ice, though, Of course, a good-looker
might tip the scale down another calf on a close
bargain. But if the proposed bride was a perfect
Venus, and had, say, only one hand, the groom would
as soon think of buying a working bullock with
three legs.
“Yalgun” : Before fowl-roosts are off, ex-
cuse me while I inform “Jeroi” that I have seen
fowls using a mulberry-tree and peach-tree in the
•same yard as boudoirs, with the result that the peach
died. If you will excuse “Jeroi,” he might explain
the reason.
“JDKZ”: Referring to the appeal of
“Wynde Wiffe” against the decision of “Mr. Justice
Ockey,” that venomous snakes do not climb (B.
24/8/’ll), I have looked into the Genesis law repoit
of the case referred to, viz., “Landlord v. Adam and
Another,” and find that there is no ieferer.ce to the
height of the apple-tree or the length of the snake,
There is ample evidence that the orchard had been
but recently planted, and it is, therefore, reasonable
to assume that the tree could not have been more
t j ian or sft. high. The snake was probably a
go-footer (as Nick would be sure to do the thing
,■, +Ol -i iL would
well )’ so that ’ by standmg on his tail he would
have reached over the top of the tree and down me
other side. Further, there is no evidence that it was
a venomous snake. And, lastly, even admitting that
the tree was a 50-footer, and the snake a little
josser and venomous, it would have been an easy
matter for him to have procured an apple for his
test match without climbing, as the law of gravita-
tion had- already been invented. Appeal, therefore,
dismissed, with costs on highest scale.

“Mallarapoonie” ; Westralia provides some
unusual aboriginal place-names. Here are a few
which I have hit up against lately, with detrimental
results to my jaw: Ivoorarawalyee, Gwambygine,
Moonyoonooka, Yundamindra, Dumbleyung, Widgie-
mooltha, Toodyay. Yowergabbie, Waggrakine, Nab-
bawa, Jennacubbine, Yatheroo and Mooliabeenee.
Then there are the “Ups,” a big family in Groper-
land—Dandalup, Kojonup, Wagerup,' Gwonangcrup,
Barrabupp, Cookernup, Mummbellup, Peringilup,

Pootenup, Dwalgarup, and so on. Next come the
“ings”—Wandering, Benjabbenng, lillimmnmg,
Ivarping, Bulla Bulling, Gilgering,' Korrelocking,
Noggogering, Popanvinning, Ivatanning and Bowelling.
Personally, I thing'Bowelling ought to be abolished
as an indictable offence, and as being outside 1 olite
Society. Following these are a few that add to the
gaiety of life in an otherwise flat and montonous

Moonta, etc., to be had. The native place-name
should always be adopted. Invariably it he
found to be the most suitable and appropriate, besides
being euphonic. In fact, you couldn’t find better if
you searched the country from Stockinbingal to
Doodlakine.

“Blackall”: At Boatman (Q.) artesian
bore, just completed, we fixed the pipe so that the
water ran into two 400-gallon tanks direct. The tanks
when put in place were empty and clean. When
they were full we turned the water away, and then
found hundreds of small and apparently blind fish
swimming inside the tanks, in no way incommoded
by the water being scalding hot. There was abso-
lutely no other way for the fish to get into the tanks
except through the bore pipe. We struck a splendid
flow in the bore, and the owners did the christening
with a cask of whisky. The townsfolk tracked the
Luggy for the 2J miles; and next morning the town
was^deserted—no shops open; ditto, pubs. Of course
it was the fish they were all after.
“Quidam” ; Curious things, names. There
is Jenolan, as applied to caves, for instance. The
word, somebody will tell you, means “Abode of the
Bounding Kangaroo” ; another learned liar translates
it, “Big pfeller hole, where Debbil-Debbil live.”
Perhaps it’s true; still, long ago, one J. Nolan carved
his name at the caves, and it’s quite as likely that
that’s how the tourist show-place got its present-
day label. Then there is the River Lett, at Hartley
(N.S.W.). I often wondered why it was called the
River Lett, when other streams in the locality were
Brown>fi Rive ,. ; Middle River , Fish
River> and so OTI gut at last the mystery was ex-
plained. A surveyor, exploring the.district in its
early days, camped by the stream, and in the official
S p e i]j n g h e wrote “riverlet,” and marked the “river-
jet” on his sketch map. When the map was being
elaborated in Sydney, the Department’s draughtsman,
tfWtfMs
kj. » presumably out of pure philanthropy. And to
this day the little mountain stream is rippling gaily
over the rocks with this cart-before-the-horse sort of
name, and the two people who, between them, gave
Australia an extra river never even got a rise in
screw. And it’s just as good a river as the Cotter,
too, and more permanently damp. *

“Dick K.” : I beg to name grey box as
being a harder timber than the black wattle, men-
tjoned by “Wang” (B. 27/7/T1). In a green state
gTey box is slightly softer than flint; when dead it
j s harder than the Devil’s corns, and the chopping of it
would break anything but the heart of an Australian
bushman. The fact of “Wang” having “gapped”
his axe through assaulting wattle does not prove
hardness in the other party. I’ve seen axes “gapped”
in an argument with soft, green stringy-bark. It all
depends on the axe. Now, I use mine for quarrying,
but it won’t cut grey box. I use dynamite when I
want to knock splinters off that animal.
DUST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Melbourne man arose to sav
That lying filled him with disgust,
But on St. Kilda-road one day
He’d seen the air so stiff with dust
That when they hosed the cloud a bit
The water just bored holes in it.
A stranger out of Maoriland
Last Summer saw the dust so thick
That you could plough it over, and
When at a dog he threw a brick
The miss le he essayed to chuck
Rose in the atmosphere, and stuck.
Then Truthful James from Broken Ilill
With weary mien remembered when
Thick yellow dust the space did fill
’Twixt Heaven and Earth, and business men
Who’d aught of consequence to do
Were forced to put a tunnel through!
VICTORIA.

“Wilkie”: Up in the Ilodgkinson coun-
try (Q.) there are two descriptions of saurians, one
the small variety, locally known as “crocodiles,” and
accounted harmless, and the other the large gentle-
man called “alligator.” Both names are probably
misapplied ; but that is a trifling detail. The larger
lizard, if his appetite is good, is capable of
hooking a horse, or even a man, from the bank of a
lagoon. Consequently, wary prospectors generally
avoid trouble by heaving a plug of gelignite before
getting too close to a suspicious-looking waterhole.
One day a,couple of prospectors arrived at a lagoon,
and before watering their horses made the usual
commotion. About a quarter of a mile along the
waterhole a tribe of binghis were out in the liquid
catching the fish that they had stupefied with the
weed that the northern nigger uses for the purpose,
and too intent on the business to notice the approach
of the conquering white man. The incident fur-
nished a beautiful illustration of the vibration set
up in • aqua (fairly) pur a by the action of a high
explosive, as, almost simultaneously with the con-
cussion, the aboriginal sports left the liquid refresh-
ment with one wild, unnatural yell, and, without
waiting for spears, boomerangs, dilly-bags, and other
impedimenta, started on a sprint championship for
the scrub-covered fastnesses of Mount Mulligan,
about 10 miles towards the horizon. Even the
smallest piccaninny considerably exceeded the motor-
car speed limit.
* * « *
“Tamumu” : Re “turkey,” as applied to a
bushman’s equipment. In S. E. White’s “Blazed
Trail,” a drunken lumber-jack has just been thrown
off a train in motion, after the playful manner
adopted by railway conductors towards hoboes and
other ride-stealers in the free and enlightened Re-
public. Then follows:
“Where’s that fellow’s turkey?” he inquired.
Several men looked towards Thorpe, who, not under-
standing this argot of the camps, was a little be-
wildered. Shearer reached over his head and took
from the rack a heavy canvas bag, which he handed
to the conductor.
“That’s the ‘turkey,’ ” he explained.
This would seem to indicate that “turkey” is simply
an old and esteemed friend, well known throughout
Australasia as Swag, Bluey, Matilda, Drum, Totalisa-
tor, Parcel, Curse o’ God, etc., to say nothing of
other titles quite unfit for publication.
“The Pariah”: Have twice seen rabbits
mesmerised by cats. In each case the charmer was
a lady and the charmed a buck, but this may have
been only coincidence. Bunny seemed to have no
power to do anything but squeal, which he did with
vigor. How puss started operations I don’t know, as
I was attracted by the squealing in each case. Both
arrests were made in orthodox fashion by the coat
collar, and both prisoners died owing to a punctured
jugular. I was called a liar the first time, but I
had one of my base calumniators as a witness to the
second performance.

“H.S.M.”: Took one of my tired immi-
grants out fishing to-day. He was new at the game;
so I fixed up the tackle, and we pulled out to the
middle of a deep waterhole in river, sat back to
back, and cast our lines. Presently he said: “What
do you call that little painted thing fixed on to the
line?” “That’s the float,” I replied. “Are they
expensive?” was his next. “That one of yours is
a pretty good one,” I volunteered. “Well,” he said,
“I’m sorry, sir, but it’s just sunk 1”
A LIVELY SENSATION.

“Eddyson”: The man with tram-cable
nerves is the only fortunate. Juff had rotten nerves.
He was a studious man, with a mania
for microscopes, which he made him-
self, grinding the lenses with the
patience of a demnition Chilian mill.
He lived in a small bush township
beyond Dimolly, and one night a
rooster belonging to Winton, whose
house was quite a quarter of a mile
off, got hold of his nerves, and drove
Juff to the verge of idiocy. This
rooster had a particularly piercing
crow, with a tinny finish, breaking
deplorably on the high note. He
started experimenting with his crow
on the night in question, having made
lip his mind to fetch that triumphant
top-note, or break the welkin in the
attempt, and he crowed and crowed,
hour after hour, and Juff lay on his
bed and writhed like a soul in the
Pit. After that Juff was simply the
rooster’s meat. The rooster was the
boss fiend of a New Gehenna, and Juff
was the one and only damned. Even-
tually Juff appeared to Winton,
white-faced, haggard, tremulous as a
wheat field, and stated his piteous
case against the crowing cock with a
shattered top note. A fortnight later
Winton met Juff. “Great Scott,
man!” gasped the former, “you’re
looking worse and worse.” “Yes,”
replied the man of nerves ; “I’ve been
lying awake of nights, waiting for
that infernal rooster of yours to be-
gin.” “Confound it!” snorted Win-
ton, “I killed him the day you com-
plained. Your housekeeper bought
him.” “Then I ate him?” Juff said.
That should have been satisfactory,
but two months from date Juff was
stowed away in a wild asylum, and
his pleasant delusion was a strident
rooster in his inside that kept him
awake by crowing all night long.
‘Tete” : I’ll back the Maori to eat any-
one on this earth. And he knows his power, too.
“King” Mahuta, he of the big talk and the bigger
dreams, wishing to sell a few hundreds of thou-
sands of acres of “surplus” lands, lately summoned
a little house-party to help him consider the matter.
The following trifles appeared on the bill-of-fare:
3822 loaves of bread, 215 cases of cabin bread,
21281 b. of butter, 142 bags of sugar, 1640 tins of
jam, 57 bullocks, 54 pigs, 356 sacks of potatoes,
274 sacks of kumeras, 4 bags of rice, 1200 pigeons,
474 sacks of pipis, 70 boxes of toheroas, 102 sacks
of mussels, 83 sacks of tuna (eels), 35 sacks of
hapuka (the smell of which is enough at any time
to put the ordinary pakeha off his food for days),
80 boxes of manga (barracouta), 8 cases of cocoa,
2001 b. of tea, 12 bottles of tomato sauce. Latest
advices are that Mahuta is asking the Government
for a subsidy. I suppose he wants to buy some
medicine.

“Pick” : When you’re talking of Australian
timbers that will stand the acid of age, I want to
introduce a line of red-gum posts that I helped to
extract from a swampy flat near Bermagui, on the
South Coast (N.S.W.). These posts stood with wet
feet for 34 years, holding up the rails of a boundary-
fence. The spotted-gum rails had given in to dry
rot and white ants, but the posts were still sound,
little weathered or sun-cracked, and the portions
underground had no appearance of footrot, or even
chilblains. I, therefore, vociferously proclaim the
red-gum as the daisy vegetable among long-standing
hardwoods, and I want to hear of its betters from
Abo-Washingtons only.

“Condamine” : If any Abo-quack will tell
me the cause and cure of “brigalow itch,” I will
drink his health. For myself, I only know for cer-
tain that it was a most unpardonable oversight to
neglect it on a certain occasion, some years back,
when plagues were all the rage in Egypt. To those
fortunate ones who are not acquainted with it, I
might explain that it is something like a poisonous
insect bite, and only seems to affect workers in
brigalow and similar scrubs. What causes it I have
never heard properly explained ; but the bark of the
brigalow tree is generally debited with it. My own
opinion is that it is due to the hairy remains of a
caterpillar, which adheres to the bark after the owner
performs the caterpillar equivalent of throwing
seven. Anyway, I know that should one of these in-
sects drop on to the back of the neck and be slapped
off, the result is something like getting a stroke
from Sam Johnford.
“Chink” ; Apropos the discussion on snake-
killing birds, I can supply a few figures that will
cause some Aboliars to adjust their views. Where I
was in Gippsland some years ago, snakes were as
numerous as “Liberal” politicians’ excuses for ham-
pering Australia’s progress. A careful tally was
kept of the operations of their enemies, as observed,
and in five months the killings were credited as fol-
lows: Laughing Jacks, 62; butcher-birds, 15; spar-
row hawks (kestrels), 3; eagles, 2; and owls, 2;
total, 84.
“Wattle”: Black Billy was general utility
on a station, and his earliest morning job was feed-
ing the fowls. The boss suggested to him that they
might be fed a bit earlier, to which Billy replied.
“By cripes, boss, mine tinkit you bin wantem this
pfeller lift ’em off plurry roost next time.”
AT THE BOGEY-HOLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sun rolls blazing dow.i the west, t'.ie knock-off
whistle blows,
The smithy-shack falls silent, and the buzz-saw
slackens, dumb ;
The shadows lengthen eastward, and the surface-
“talent” goes
To skylark in the singing creek where whitening
waters hum
And purr across the stones, and swirl green in the
shady pool,
Green in the weedy depths, ‘and yellow on the sand
—a dream
Of sparkling color, as the flashing runnels, crystal,
cool,
Rush rippling past the painted rocks that sprawl
across the stream.
Low boughs bend blossomingabove the placid pool,
and drip
A scant, slow flood of petals, pink and white, upon
the creek
That crawls beneath; and Bill and Co. come loung-
ing down, and slip
Lazy into the flood, leaving the coarse, confining
breek
And shirt and hobnailed blucher planted where the
towels wait
Upon the looming shoulder of the falls, in breezy
shade
Among the scrub; and, later, throned in cool,
primeval state,
Bill lolls upon his gravel-bank, in nature’s robe
arrayed.
A while, below the spouting falls, uproarious naked-
ness,
Cleaving the shadowed deep, goes shouting breath-
less to the bank,
While, up above, swift scarlet marks the wild,
peculiar dress
Of niggers nipping through the scrub, long-footed,
bony, lank ;
Where lubras, black and blushless, giggle, goggle-
eyed, and screech
Wild nigger-gibes and jokes profound about this
Bill or that—
Strange gibes that sound like German to the heedless
ears they reach ;
How Sammy Brown is spindle-shanked and William
Johnson fat!
The sun goes down, with crimson clouds about his
chariot-wheels,
And Bill swims slowly out and slings his clothes
upon his back ;
The niggers’ camp-curs yelp and snarl, the skittish
lubra squeals
And cackles as she picks her pet from out the
mongrel pack.
The crowd is booted, shirted, breeched, that lounged,
brown-shouldered, bare,
Along the shallows. Night comes down; where
blazing splendor shone
Fine brilliance decks the sky. Above the creek the
camp-fires flare,
And, while Bill skins the fatted steak, Sam puts
the billy on!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
THE ABSTAINER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sky was of brass ; there was no sign of rain ;
My mouth seemed as dry as the sun-baked plain,
When I saw, in the shade of a tall, hot tree,
A drover, and asked him to drink with me.
He was wrinkled and bearded and old and grey,
And he smiled at me in a kindly way;
Then he started to talk of the old-time sprees
And the spidery ends of those jamborees.
“Well, there’s no doubt about it, to-day is hot;
But, if you don’t mind, son, I’d rather not.”
Then he grinned in the shade of that tall, hot tree.
“I’m thirsty enough as I am,” said he.
N.S.W.
TOY WHEEL.
REUNITED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The wife of Reverend Abel Brown
Went to the Summer Sea
To hunt her missing husband down;
Long since he’d sailed from Sydney town
The savage soul to free.
She came to where a chieftain sat,
And told her tale of woe.
I hate to have to tell you that
A heathen most polite and fat
Could treat a lady so ;
But when that nigger went to rest,
A glutton sore misguided,
He had tattooed upon his chest,
By way of loathly heathen jest:
“In death they’re not divided!”
