Captain Lloyd, who died at Hobart a few
days ago, aged 93 years, came to old Van
Diemen’s Land in the same ship that
brought Sir John Franklin as Governor, in
3837. The deceased once had a professional
interview with Martin Cash, bushranger.
A letter re Judkins: —
Adelaide Social Reform Bureau has raised a £2OO
subscription to the Judkins Fund in Melbourne, which
is now well into the second thousand. The hopeless
sickness of the pugnacious little Purist seems to have
reminded many people that, like Othello, he did the
State some service. It was Judkins who practically
compelled the Victorian Government to put an end
to Wren’s lawless Tote and club, what time Premier
Bent was personally hobnobbing with the law-
breakers. The Wowserism that vents itself in carp-
ings at “Social evils” is only a casual blot on the
Judkinsism that had a grave political evil to de-
nounce. Jud. did something that every decent citizen
of Melbourne approved. He worried and bullied the
Government into cutting a cancerous growth out of
the body politic, and whether he worked as a Wowser
or a reformer the result was equally good. Now that
his own poor little body is afflicted by a cancerous
growth, which seemingly is beyond eradication, he
has the comfort of knowing that public sympathy
is mounting up to a substantial figure. Spook jour-
nalist Stead, the man who owns the spirit of Julia,
sent £lOO by cable.
HORRIBLE REFLECTION.
For the purposes of this Act a Chinaman shall be
deemed to be a girl under 18 years of age.
To prevent Chinese competition in Maoriland
laundries (which come under the Factories Act)
the above amendment in the interpretation
clause was sent to the Crown Law Office for
consideration.
Joe Mikulee, the American who is said to
he walking round the earth for a 30,000-dollar
bonus, told an Age reporter that, when he
started Avalking, he took size six in boots,
but now he takes nines. Well, there have
been other athletes who started with a 6f
hat, and in 12 months developed a head the
size of a kitchen copper. Apparently suc-
cess must get you at one end or the other.
Incidentally, Mikulee’s statement casts a
vivid light on the progress and development
of policemen’s feet. There’s a well-known
politician, too, who has developed the largest
pair of feet in Australian politics, running
away from his principles.
Mrs. G. 11. Cox, who died recently at
Burrundulla, Mudgee (N.S.W.), aged 78
years, was the widow of G. H. Cox, who, at
his death in 1901, was dad of the Legisla-
tive Council. Mrs. Cox and her husband
were cousins, he being the son of George
Cox, and she the daughter of Henry Cox, of
Mulgoa. The original William Cox, who
came to Australia in ISOI, was paymaster
in the old New South Wales Rum-selling
Corps. On leaving that honorable and
strong-breathed regiment, he contracted with
Governor Macquarie to build the road across
the Blue Mountains, and did the work in a
fair to middling fashion.
The N.S.W. politician who has the worst
luck of all just now is Wood, M.L.A. Almost
the only language he has been accustomed to
use in the House is language that Speaker
Willis objects to, and his fluency has been to
a great extent turned off at the meter. Wood
is a sort of shipwrecked mariner adrift on
an ocean of undrinkable vocabulary.
The S.A. Labor party has lost a strenuous
worker in A. F. Block, who died last week
at the early age of 33. He held several im-
portant offices in connection with the Trades
Hall, and was secretary of the State branch
of the Federated Agricultural Implement
Makers' Association. Also, he hustled like a
man and a brother in the Labor cause gener-
ally, and left a good name behind him.
The “unearned increment” tax of S.A.
Treasurer Vaughan, if it had been passed
by the Legislative Council, would have smit-
ten one gentlemen who is about to sell city
property in Adelaide. This is Ruthven
Smith, a wealthy absentee, whose uncle in
1836 bought three acres facing Bundle and
Grote streets for £1 apiece. The uncle be-
queathed this dirt to the present holder, and
the Federal land tax is inducing him to sell
the greater portion. It is a moderate esti-
mate to put down the value of the three
blocks at £150,000.
“Bathurstian”:—
•h H. Stewart, of Mount Pleasant, Bathurst
(N.S.W.), who was presented with an address by his
tenants, on his birthday last week, is a marvellous
old citizen. He is 86, yet he may be seen any day
cantonng about on a. lively-looking cob. A month
or so ago the animal fell with him and broke his
arm; it didn’t seem to affect his vitality. Every
year lie goes fly-fishing to M.L., sometimes with a
friend, but more often by himself. Local rumor
avers that he _ has never taken a sleeping compart-
ment in his life. He regards such luxuries as lit
onfy for weaklings and sybarites. His father was
the old British General who was Acting-Governor of
N. S. Wales for a dozen days, and between them they
jived in as many reigns, probably, as any pair in the
Commonwealth.
By the Oldest Inhabitant:—
All the Sydney dailies mentioned Fred Lassetter,
recently dead and buried in his 83rd year, as being
the son of Rev. Matt Lassetter, a Methodist minister.
As a matter of fact, Matt Lassetter was a local
preacher in South London before 1832, in which year
he left for Sydney with his wife and the boy Fred.
It was not until 1835 that he applied for admission
to the ministry, by which time he had a wife and
four children. He did not wait for a “call,” but
entered into business, said business, I think, being
that of a pastrycook and confectioner. Towards the
end of the ’SO’s Matt Lassetter removed to Van
Diemen’s Land with his family, and took up a
small farm at Longford. His wife died, and he
shifted to Launceston, where he opened a school.
Soon after that he moved on to America, leaving
Fred to paddle his own canoe, and Fred proved a
very good navigator.
Am°ng the recently dead is Richard Grice,
ot Melbourne, who was connected with the
Purves and Levy clans, and one or two
other notable social sets. He was an
ancient “sport,” and a strenuous follower of
hounds and race horses. His name will be
missed from the list of committeemen and
from V.R.C. programmes. Tradition says
that he nearly won a Melbourne Cup with
a horse named Hova. The late Mr. Grice
was born in the building now occupied by the
Deutscher Turn Verein in East Melbourne
He married a Miss Currie, after whom he
christened his Berwick estate Eirruc. At
the picturesque Berwick cemetery he was
buried, and several racing identities—Byron
Moore among them—went to the funeral.
P r ° f - Walter Thos. Mills, of Milwaukee,
U.S.A., the little old chap with the snowy
side-levers, recently lecturing in Maoriland,
was pen-pictured by Upton Sinclair in “The
Jungle,” the sensational yaller-press fiction
of the year. Sinclair made Jurgis, the saved
soul, meet, among other Socialist leaders, “a
man who. was known in the party as the
‘Little Giant.’ The Lord had used up so
much material in the making of his head
that there had not been enough to complete
his legs; but he got about on the platform,
and when he shook his raven whiskers, the
pillars of Capitalism rocked. He had writ-
ten a veritable encyclopaedia upon the sub-
.leot. a book that was nearly as big as him-
self.
Concerning a brick :
Manchester (Eng.) papers tell of the presentation
to John Redmond of a brick taken from the cottage
in which dwelt John Mitchel, during his period of
transportation in Van Diemen’s Land. Senator Long
made the presentation, and expressed a hope that
Redmond would find the article handy as a paper-
weight for use m the restored Irish Parliament.
Mitchel returned to Ireland after serving his term of
banishment, and in 1875 was elected unopposed as
member for Tipperary. He was declared ineligible,
but a month later the same constituency returned
him a second time, with a majority of over 2500 his
opponent polling only 746 votes. Again Mitchel
was disqualified, and the opponent with the micro-
scopic minority got the seat. Then the flames of
dissatisfaction went up like a burning oil store, but
within a few days Mitchel died and the trouble
unavoidably fizzled out.
“Vagabond” makes a few cuss remarks;—
The Maoriland Workek recently loaded every line
and page of a whole issue up to the muzzle with
anti-militarism.” It parades photos, of the two
louts who went to gaol rather than to drill, and re-
quests readers—in fat black type, over the picture
of one of them taken in an Ajax-defying-the-Income-
Tax attitude—to “Honor the Fight He Made.” I
hope the hero jumps the Worker for damages and
defamation of character for insinuating that he’d
fight anything . except his dinner. One writer fires
some deadly volleys of verse, exhorting M.L. to
“Let her people triumph, let her people be free.”
Very good. If she wants to triumph, let ’er
triumph. P’raps the poet will explain how she’s
going to carry out the programme if some objection-
able foreigners come along and poke brutal bavonets
in her carcase. She might tell ’em to g’away, that
she was busy triumphing and letting her people be
free, and if they took no notice she could treat them
with dignified contempt, though it is a bit difficult
to be dignified when the other chap’s chasing vou
round at the business-end of a gun. What a pity
the Worker can’t run a Jap edition, and persuade
Japan that she also ought to triumph and let ’er be
free, and that the way to do it was to refuse to
learn the goose-step and which end to poke the car-
tridge in. Then there might be a better outlook for
the Worker programme.

Minister of Industry, whose pertinent re-

William Perry was an Official Assignee
at Sydney in 1849, and was the first secre-
tary to the A.M.P. Society. With the aid
of Parson Walsh, Thomas Mort, Thomas
Holt and Charles Lowe, he founded the now
big Australian life office. It is understood
that Mr. Perry’s policy was the first issued
and among the first paid, as he died in
1854. He left a widow, a daughter of Rev.
W. W. Simpson, M.A., of Dapto, Illawarra,
who died on Tuesday, August 29, at her
residence, Roseneath, Albion, near Bris-
bane. She was aged 86 years.
Passed out at the age of 61, Thomas Lux-
ton, a Victorian personality of shaggy ex-
terior. Thomas arrived in Victoria in ’53,
and graduated in finance on the Stock Ex-
changes of Bendigo, Ballarat and Mel-
bourne. He acquired much cash in various
ventures, and was the controlling force in
the amalgamated businesses of J. McEwan
and McLean Bros, and Rigg. As a public man,
Luxton was bogged among a multiplicity of
interests. He was concerned in all sorts of
things, from school treats to jockey, clubs,
ana found time to spend 20 years in the
P’ran council, as well as seven or eight
years in the House of Fat. Politically, how-
ever, he was a person of little importance.
He sought the unhappy mean between Tory
inertia and “Liberal” inaction, and was
generally successful in his quest.
William Gilbert Marsh, of Adelaide, who
is about to retire from active sky-pilotage,
in the Anglican denomination, has it to his
debit that he was the first secretary of Mel-
bourne Y.M.C.A., and held the billet for 15
years. Then he took to the Church, and
toiled for another 15 years in the vineyard of
St. Luke’s, Adelaide. He is of United
States origin, and still gives out the hymn
through his nose to some extent—at least,
certain hypercritical folk so allege.
A “moving picture” sets “Screen” off on
the trail:—
The Australian biograph of “The Lady’’ Outlaw
interests me rather more than any other. It is sup-
posed to be founded on John Batman, the Victorian
pioneer. There is a tradition that Batman married
a woman outlaw, whom he captured in Tasmania. I
have been raking up, but cannot discover the slight-
est foundation for the story. The Batmans had eight
fine children; one daughter married Weire, after-
wards town clerk of Geelong. Can anybody explain
how the outlaw legend arose? Surely it must be
legend! According to the biograph, excellently
got up in Sydney by Rolfe, so long associated with
Dampier, the lady was of good family in England.
Her lover was transported, and went through some
Rufus Dawes kind of experiences in Tasmania. She
went there to seek him, and was informed that he
had died through the cruelty of Elton, his employer.
Thereupon she takes to bushranging, as leader of a
convict gang, and is just going to shoot Elton when
she finds that her lover is alive, and with a wife.
More agony! Here Batman comes in, under the
name of Dashwood, and, after a desperate chase, cap-
tures her. Of course, it winds up with the customary
cuddle of the biograph. But I don’t believe a flick
of it.
Three ex-Melbournians are giving Aus-
tralia a poor advt. in the City of Fog. They
are Mrs. Turner, clairvoyante, who could
not see clearly enough to discern detective
Macmanamny when she met him, with dis-
astrous results to her pocket; “Pastor” Ab-
bott, matrimonial agent; and Charles Bailey,
the notorious pseudo-medium. They are
showing on Sundays at Caxton Hall, on the
silver-coin admission racket, and they have
struck trouble in Wm. Marriott and Lad-
broke Black. Marriott tackled the trio, and
heaved at Bailey a challenge, which that
dealer in spirits has carefully side-stepped.
He offered £lOO if Bailey, being locked in a
cage in the middle of the Caxton Hall or anv
other public hall, could by occult mean's
transfer a marked coin or other small article
from a few feet outside the cage to its in-
terior. But though the spirits will turn
themselves into a parcels express when it’s a
matter of bringing jewels, etc., all the way
from Calcutta to Melbourne, Bailey isn’t
prepared to trust them with an insignificant
job like this.
Lord Denman has established one record
for Australia, anyhow. Last Friday he
rode over the big fences on Melbourne Show
Ground, being the first Governor-General to
perform the feat. The new potentate’s
nerve must be all right. The average Eng-
lish horseman, unaccustomed to tall and
unbreakable timber, either in the hunting
field or on steeplechase courses, shies
affrightedly at such obstacles as Denmau
surmounted.
Two ancient names appear among the

Mirams* too, is a very old man these days,
and isn’t quite the shifter he was.
C. F. Folland, just dead in Adelaide, was
an unushal veteran. He went on the land,
was a local preacher, and wrote copiously
to the papers. All that is commonplace in
S.A. But Folland, at the age of 60, turned
into a fighting Agnostic ; and after that he
treated the Biblical three-score-and-ten limit
with contempt, and lived gaily to the age of
over 97. He was one of the few remaining
people anywhere who were alive before
Waterloo; that disturbance occurred on the
first anniversary of his birth. He was thus
grown up when South Australia was founded.
He reached Adelaide when that village was
(wo years old, and used to hunt all kinds of
game with the natives, both game
and natives being then abundant within
a mile or two of the G.P.O. He
had a shot at law, printing, and painting, and
was ruined by the dishonoring of Governor
Gawler’s official drafts, before he started to
put in 68 years on a farm. And he leaves
97 descendants.
“Oxonian,” his Vindication :
The recently-appointed Melbourne Professor of Eng-
lish is described (B. 24/8/’ll) as a “gentleman from
Aberdeen,” the product of a “fifth-rate British Uni-
versity.” Now, Mr. Wallace was not chosen on the
strength of his Aberdeen degree alone. He is also a
graduate of Oxford, where he attended the lectures of
Professors Raleigh, Napier, Wright and De Selin-
court, and received private instruction from Ur.
Sweet. Also, he took a first-class in the Honor
School of English in 1907. All who know anything
of University work will realise that Mr. Wallace’s
wider experience is a factor that must have weighed
heavily with the selection committee. Mr. Murdoch
has presumably studied only under Professor Morris,
and the study of English has progressed greatly
since his time; Mr. Wallace is familiar with the
methods of some of the most eminent English
scholars now living, some of whom, as, for instance,
Sweet and Napier, have European reputations.
JUDGE DENNISTON, OF MAORILAND.
The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant writes :
The will of Mrs. Macarthur Onslow, of Camden
Park (N.S.W.), who died in England recently, has
been probated at £200,000. She was the daughter
of James Macarthur, the only one of John
Macarthur’s four sons who had issue, and she was
an only child. She married Captain Onslow, a re-
tired R.N. man, who died in 1882, leaving five sons
and two daughters. Mrs. Macarthur Onslow was in
her 70th year. In 1899 the Camden Park estate
was formed into a limited liability company (not
including the homestead), the authorised capital
being £150.000. The capital subscribed (in 1904)
was £104,007, in £1 shares; 100.000 of the shares
being fully paid-up. There were 10 shareholders.
ROYAL PROGRESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Perhaps,” said the High Commissioner, “his
Ma.iesty is the most highly and most wisely-trained
and most widely-experienced Sovereign who ever sat
on Britain’s throne.”— Cable.
So kings go out, and kings come in,
And still in grace and wisdom rise,
Mach greater, grander than his kin,
More learned, more wonderful and wise.
The king that died perfection seemed,
By word of mouth and praise of pen.
The newer king with splendor gleamed.
Such wit and virtue were undreamed
By simple, trusting subjects when
His father was the best of men.
So good begins, but better things
Are yet to come. ’Tis Heaven’s plan,
By so improving English kings,
To give us soon the Superman.
But here emotions traitorous
And melancholy feelings throng:
If kings go on perfecting thus,
’Tis quite ridiculous in us,
And manifestly very wrong,
To hope that one will linger long!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
All the States now open their financial
year on July 1 ; hence only two months have thus
far run. Under these circumstances the available
revenue figures don’t mean much ; but, so far as
they go, they are highly satisfactory. On the other
hand, the expenditure figures fill the atmosphere with
the drinky odor of Boom. In N. S. Wales the Labor
party was supposed to represent financial sanity;
but, having reached office, it has taken the
easy way of drift. The two months’ revenue
amounted to £2,185,021, or £56,332 more
than the receipts for the corresponding one-
sixth of the previous year; the expenditure was
£2,262,456, or £123,256 more than the figures at
this time last year. And that is not the worst
feature; the “other expenditure”—public works,
closer settlement, railway loan and general loan —
has run to £1,072,691, or at the appalling rate of
nearly £6,500,000 a year. The public works and
closer settlement funds are certainly provided to
some extent out of revenue and so-called revenue,
the so-called revenue being derived from the sale of
public lands; but, even apart from those items, the
party which was supposed to favor only public
works that would return interest and a moderate
sinking fund, is borrowing and spending at the rate
of just upon £5,000,000 a year—more, that is, than
any of the Governments whose extravagance the
party hitherto attacked. Such a thing as a sinking
fund is never mentioned nowadays. The whole busi-
ness amounts to a vast system of bribery: one party
is merely outbidding the other, and the price will
have to be paid by the fools who apparently think
they are getting it all for nothing.
Victoria, as usual, furnishes no progress figures as
to expenditure out of either revenue or loans. Its
revenue figures for the two months are £1,354,446,
as against £1,387,501. The shrinkage is wholly due
to smaller Commonwealth payments. State revenue
proper is £45,797 in excess of the previous year’s.
Railway revenue is soaring—another instance of the
dreadful effects of the Commonwealth’s gentle hint
to the land monopolist to move on. Queensland has
again taken the bit between its teeth, and is rushing
down the steep place leading to the old dry creek
that is strewn with the bones of the boom wrecks
of the ’9o’s. Its loan expenditure for two months
reached £436,443, or at rate of over £2,500,000 a
year for a mere handful of 604,000 people. N. S.
Wales had, according to the census, 1,648,210
people; so Queensland’s insanity is rather worse, on
figures, than Mother’s is. Its ordinary revenue for
two months was £898,334, as against £774,478 in
the corresponding fragment of 1909-10, and its ex-
penditure (apart from interest, not yet charged) was
£545,099, as against £442,085. Westralia started
the financial year with a so-called surplus of £13,302.
At the end of July the alleged surplus was turned
into a deficit of £46,096 ; and at the end of August
the disgrace had grown to £78,227. The branch of
the trouble in which Cohen is most interested isn’t
so much as mentioned. S. Australia preserves a pro-
found silence; and Tasmania merely issues a few
revenue figures that are so far of no significance at
all.
* * * *
Tas. Treasurer Lewis reports that he re-
ceived £970,092 for the financial year ended June 30,
and spent £1,016,963, thus overshooting the con-
stable by £46,871. As he started the year with a
floating deficiency of £51,271, his total leeway
amounts to £98,142. As it is expected that the
255. per capita return from the Commonwealth will
this year approximate £240,000 ( £lO,OOO increase
on last year’s receipts from the Commonwealth) ;
that the land tax will return £BO,OOO (increase
£15,000) ; income taxes £135,000 (increase of
£20,000); and interest on transferred properties
£30,000, the Treasurer reckons he can spend at least
£50,000 more than he spent last year, and still
wind up with no worse deficiency than his present
£98,142. The £50,000 is to be cut up thus: In-
creased interest payable, £21,000; increased railway
expenditure (mostly on repairs and wages), £15,000 ;
education, £4000; gaols and police, £2000; hospi-
tals and charities, £4000; and sundries, £4OOO.
As an election is looming up on the adjacent horizon,
it was not expected that the Tory administration
would promulgate any other sort of policy. The
burden of Treasurer Lewis’s song is: “We have
reached the limit of taxation; let there be peace,
blessed peace, and let the deficit stay on the
premises if it wants to!” So it is proposed to con-
tinue the old shiftless methods of the past: the de-
lightful policy of drift which leadeth to depopula-
tion. An illuminating passage in Treasurer Lewis’s
hopelessly drab statement is the announcement that
£5OO is to be voted for assisted immigration. As
Tassie is already failing to retain her natural in-
crease, the idea of assisting immigrants, even to the
funny little extent mentioned, is positively dazzling.

This very successful institution used to be officially
known as the Government Advances to Settlers
Office; but a couple of years ago it was made a
branch of a new concern —the State-Guaranteed Ad-
vances Office, which controls the financial props
offered to “settlers,” “workers,” and “local authori-
ties.” This Settlers Branch is, however, the senior
partner in the business, and finances the Workers
Branch. But behind the senior partner is the Public
Trustee, who represents the State which supplies
the money—borrowed money, of course, for we are
talking about Maoriland at present. It is a pretty
good indication of the rottenness of the Rickety
country’s financial methods, that to provide for the
repayment of the Public Trustee a so-called sinking
fund has been established, which is no more a sink-
ing fund than Joe Ward is a Dreadnought. This
bogus sinking fund consists “of a sum to be paid
in each financial year out of the Advances Account
...equal to 1 p.c. of the total moneys raised for
the purpose of the branch, and then outstanding.”
So, if the branch has borrowed £1,000,000 alto-
gether, it must pay £lO,OOO a year into this fraud;
but it doesn’t have to pay it in out of profits—it
simply has to pay it in out of the borrowed money.
If it hasn’t got that much borrowed money left in
the bag, then it may borrow some more; and if it
can’t borrow any more, then the precious “sinking
fund” can look after itself. Of course the financial
bosses of the office don’t show the “sinking fund”
among the reserves. On one side they just show so
much money borrowed, and on the other side the
“sinking fund” is among the assets into which the
loans have been put. It is such a ridiculous misuse
of a term that it ought to be dropped ; otherwise,
some day, when Maoriland may be honest enough to
have a real sinking fund, it may find it impossible
to get Bull Cohen to believe that the thing is the
genuine article.
The real reserves are shown in the table; and
they are growing so very nicely that there is no
need to offer any gilded ha’pennies. A solid ad-
dition of £60,441 to the reserve funds in 12
months is good enough to stand on its own merits.
Certainly, risks have increased considerably; and
the total to provide for contingencies is not a penny
more than it should be. This shows the risks in
force and some other details: —

The expenses are still very moderate; they include
the fees paid to the Valuation Department for its
services. The actual management exes, were 3s. p.c.
on capital employed, and that represents no advance
on the very slim figure of the nrevious year. It all
amounts to this. The Maoriland settler is getting ac-
commodation at cost price—that is, the price at which
the most solvent entity can borrow in large sums —
plus a shade over I p.c. for exes., and a shade under 1
p.c. for contingencies. Thus, the poor little settler,
out of whom the private Cohen used to bite 7J or
8 p.c. interest, now has to pay only lj p.c. more
for his small lot than the State itself can negotiate
a big loan for. It is a dreadful thing, this Socialism,
and frightfully prejudicial to various interests —
especially the interests that run from 7 to 20 p.c.
As mentioned above, though £60,441 was put by
last year, the reserves are no bigger than they
ought to be. There is £6,140,984 out on mortgage;
and the reserves total £240,767. Thus, if some-
thing bad happened to Maoriland —and it is a place
that something bad might easily happen to—and the
bank had a lot of its securities thrown on its hands,
and realised only £4 p.c. less than it has lent upon
them, the whole of the reserves would be exhausted,
and the bank would have to hold out its hat to the
State, just as the private Bank of N.Z. did a few
years ago. But while that wasn’t regarded as a
very dreadful thing for the B.N.Z. to do, and nobody
has quoted it as proof that Private Enterprise is a
failure, a State bank’s repetition of the performance
would be quoted for ever and ever as full proof that
Socialism is an incompetent blunderer. So Maori-
land had better not let its eye rest too long on that
£60,441 of profit; it had far better consider the
risks that run side by side with £6,140,984 of ad-
vances, and go on building its reserves very much
higher yet.

This is a branch of what is officially known as the
State-Guaranteed Advances Office. The original
authority was given by the Advances to Workers
Act of 1906; but various changes have since been
made, including a recent and valuable one, whereby
power is given to the Board to make advances for
a shorter term than the originally-prescribed 364
years. No doubt many men foolishly undertake to
pay off a debt with a rapidity that is beyond them;
but when a Board is told, “You must not lend
money for less than 36i years,” then it often has to
sit down and say, “Very well; in that case we can’t
lend at all.” A mining town, for instance, though
flourishing to-day, will very likely be a mere harbor
for gohannas and moas and wallabies and other
reptiles 25 years hence. And there are a good many
other towns which one wouldn’t like to swear would
be of much account 361 years hence. That this
home-providery was needed is best shown by the
swift growth of its advances. That growth is very
gratifying, of course, to everybody except the land-
lord who has been extracting 15s. a week for the
use of his wooden rabbit-hutch; but the swiftness
has its perils. When trouble strikes a country, as
it struck Victoria on the bursting of the land boom,
that mortgage co. or building society mostly fares
worst which has made the biggest recent advances,
The old advances —the ones in connection with, which
many payments of instalment money have been made
—don’t worry the liquidator. In two years this
office has added £695,523 to its advances; and that
means that it has made more than £700,000 of new
advances. Yet it has only increased its reserves by
£ 5884; and £ 5884 wouldn’t go very far in pro-
tecting £700,000 of advances if the securities were
hit by some whirlwind. So the management’s job
is to move ahead very cautiously, keep a firm hand
on the expenses, resist the inevitable squeeze for
more liberal terms, and Dile up a reserve as fast as
possible. Though Maoriland hasn’t lately been hit
by any whirlwind worth mentioning, in this office’s
Settlers Branch “19 freehold and 13 leasehold securi-
ties have for various reasons been realised by the
Department during its existence. This year, losses
to the extent of £53 have been made. There are,
moreover,'"on the Department’s hands two securities
which are not satisfactory. At present they are
leased at a nominal rental, awaiting a favorable op-
portunity for realisation.” That is a danger signal,
hoisted up ahead of both branches. The £1,177,959
of outstanding loans is represented by 4282 mort-
gages ; the past year’s average advance on freehold
was £323, and bn leasehold £194. Of course, in
many instances the borrowers have themselves
thrown in a pretty big slice of the cost; and the
bigger the slice the less, of course, the Department s
worry.

*Less something to be absorbed by an em-

That is as close as The Bulletin can get to it on the
data available. Things, fortunately, promise to be
made clearer in the future. The position hitherto has
been that the Finney estate has had a lot of money
in the business, and on account of that liability, the
co.’s articles have contained provisions which have
caused many investors to stand off the stock ; also,
no balance-sheet has been published. When Thomas
Finney died, the junior partners naturally wanted
to hang on to the business, but evidently, like most
junior partners, they hadn’t got the capital to buy
the big man’s interest. In such a case, if the big
man’s representatives have a reasonable amount of
discretion and common sense, they don’t force a
liquidation sale; and they didn’t here. They
arranged to leave their money in the business as a
loan provided (1) the other partners didn’t draw
their divs., but put them into capital, and (2) the
loan was gradually paid off. The new concern had
good commercial and financial control; it struck out
vigorously just when Queensland was ready to repay
enterprise handsomely ; and. on the figures available,
the co. has moved forward rapidly. Nevertheless, if
profits were computed in the ordinary way, there
wouldn’t be as much difference between the last
two years’ figures as the table shows. The 1910
figures have been diminished by some unspecified
amounts written off and by others that have been
applied to a bonus fund ; while the 1911 bonus has
yet to come off the total for that year. If the net
profit is thus and otherwise reduced to £19,500
and it will probably be about that —the result will
still be very excellent. The dividend has been com-
puted on the capital as at the end of the year (July
31). On the total amount, the net profit (say
£20,000) was at the rate of over 22} p.c. ; and as
the increased div. is only 8 p.c., a very fine ad-
dition is made to reserves. And those reserves ap-
pear to be the real thing, inasmuch as there is now
no goodwill or other paper entry on the other side of
the balance-sheet.

It was mentioned above that the amount shown as
profit in the first table might contain .a few pounds
of share premium. Twelve months ago the Board
decided to issue 25,000 shares at a premium of Is.,
and apparently if there was anything left over after
paying the expenses of that issue, it was passed to
Profit and Loss. Now, partly to get rid of the
Finney liability, it is proposed to issue 65,000
shares at a premium of 2s. The co. certainly needs
more capital; for its liabilities on July 31 were
£151,305, including £41,240 of debentures, £24,407
of Finney money, and £41,590 of property mort-
gages. No doubt the bulk of this is cheap money ;
but while : it exists shareholders have only a second
mortgage on assets and profits, and second mort-
gages are cold things when misfortune looks in.
As security for the new issue, there is not only the
highly profitable business and the stock and book
debts, but property valued at about £37,000, over
and above the mortgage. The issue is, of course, not
preferential, so the other capital shares in the securi-
ties mentioned. The new issue is large; but it has
to be remembered that the original share capital was
ridiculously small, due to the special circumstances
under which the co. was launched. Also, since it
was launched, the co. has spent over £75,000 on
premises, the co.’s freehold replacing a short-term
leasehold—always an unsatisfactory and dangerous
tenure for a co. with an attractive name and a valu-
able connection. Since it started seven years ago,
the co. has wiped out goodwill items of £16,235,
besides flotation expenses, and has built up a reserve
of £34,126. Of its total profits it has divided less
than 32 p.c. ; and a co. which starts off with a re-
cord like that deserves to have bouquets thrown at
it. There are, of course, circumstances which can’t
be tested ; for instance, the stock valuation and the
book debts have to be taken on trust. Also, the co.
can’t reasonably expect to show the sarne rate of
profit on its new and proposed capital as it could on
its old capital, because much of the new money is
represented by city property, and the rest of it pays
off debt which wasn’t costing more than perhaps
4-j p.c. But, given all that in, the position has be-
come much sounder, and the new issue will make it
sounder still. <\

There have been Pitts and Badgerys almost ever
since there have been sheep and cows in N. S. Wales,
so that when this co. was formed, in 1888, it took
over a couple of handy names and a profitable con-
nection. But that didn’t keep it out of difficulties;
in fact, the bigger one’s pastoral connection in the
early ’9o’s, the deeper one was likely to get into the
soup. Once this co. realised where it was, it didn’t,
however, try to persuade itself that it was anywhere
else; instead, it set to work steadily to get out, and
after that to empty the tureen and label it dangerous.
Part of the trouble, as usual, was that it had made
advances on securities which had dried up and blown
away; the other part was that much of the money
thus advanced belonged to other people—unreason-
able people who wanted their money back, no matter
what had happened. There was only one way to
honorably get out of the mess, and the co. took it:
it toiled on, and devoted the profits to the removal
of the old dead horse instead of scattering them
among the shareholders. Later on the prosperity of
the country gave the deserving co. a hand ; and by
1907 the directors reckoned that, in writing off a
final £17,500, they had got all their assets down to
bedrock. The last of the goodwill paper asset disap-
peared at the same time. Being thus out of the
soup, the co. proceeded to put itself as far as possible
out of the temptation to drop into the seductive
crockery again. This shows what happened (inci-
dentally it shows a few other things) :
1900. 1909. 1910. 1911.
Overdrafts ..£55,741 £43,896 £17,311 £29,272
Other liabilities .. 3,009 7,139 20,421 23,205
Advances, fore-
closures, etc. .. 87,087 34,744 35,303 41,459
Freeholds, etc. ... 10,718 94,218 104,465 123,813
Goodwill .. .. 25,000 Nil Nil Nil
Thus the capital and other funds which the co:
formerly put into advances it has sunk firmly in free-
holds; the latter item sorang from £10,718 in 1900
to £94,218 in 1909, and £123,813 in 1911. The
bulk of this is represented by city property—mainly
office premises for its own and tenants’ use, with
some recent additions in the shape of wool and pro-
duce stores and sale yards. Thus the co.’s capital
and reserves are wholly invested in freeholds, by far
the greater part of which may be regarded as a gilt-
edged security ; and the comparatively small amount
required to finance clients (“sundry debtors”) is
obtained by overdraft and otherwise. Whether un-
der adverse conditions the co. will be able to hold
its business without offering harassed clients the old
accommodation remains to be seen; if it can, it has
left four-fifths of its risks and troubles behind it.
Two years ago, having had a very good time, the
Board decided to nail down some of the profit by
transferring it to capital; so it paid a bonus of Is.
per share, and made a call at the same time. Next
vear, the same was done to the extent of 35.; this
year 2s. This last transaction will make the shares
17s. paid, leaving only 3s. to go. There are 100,000
of them.
Amalgamated Zinc (De Bavay’s), Ltd.:—
Written
Profits. Dividends. Off. Deficit.
£3,378
Reserves.
Mar., 1910 .. £32,194 Nil Nil 28 81ft
Sept., 1910 .. 44,253 Nil Nil 73069
June, 1911 .. 100,106 £75,000 Nil 98)575
De Bavay is a Melbourne brewery chemist, who took
a hand in solving the problem of the Broken Hill
zinc tailings. For about 18 years the B. H. cos.
mined sulphide ore, which contains up to 22 p.c.)
and occasionally more, zinc, and the object of their
concentration process was to eliminate that zinc
along with the other mullock. Certainly, zinc was
rather precious mullock, being worth anything up
to £3O per ton; but it wasn’t worth anything at
all so long as it was mixed with quartz, rhodonite,
etc., and it was worth considerably less than nothing
if it was mixed with the silver and lead, because the
silver and lead smelters imposed a fine if it was
present. Consequently huge Alps of the mullock,
that ought to have been precious but wasn’t, grew
up along the line of lode, and all sorts of scientists
poked their noses in and consumed much gas in
trying to solve the riddle. Exactly who solved it
hasn’t been decided yet, though various lawyers have
grown several inches fatter round the waist after
swallowing the fees that have been paid them to argue
things before Patents and other Courts. Three or
four of the scientists adopted the oil and acid pro-
cess, each with his own modifications; and De
Bavay was among the number. In this process acid
and oil (they mostly use eucalyptus oil nowadays)
are mixed with the sandy Alps, and the oil and
acid between them lift the zinc to the surface and
float it off —not pure zinc, of course, but from 42
to 48 p.c. metal.
The B. H. cos. had got rather tired of their
Alps, and they mostly sold them right out for very
little. For one thing they were not sure of the zinc
market. In the baby years of this century, when
zinc was first seriously talked about, the average
price for one six months got down below £l6 18s. ;
and, though it moved up again, in 1908, when De
Bavay’s people began to look around, it was down be-
low £l9 10s. To-daythe metal is worth £27 12s. 6d.
Under the co.’s most important contract the mining
co. shares in the extra profits with this co. ; but,
though it doesn’t thus get the whole hog, this co.’s
share of the animal is very fat, as the table shows.
In nine months the co. has made a profit of
£100,106 on its capital of £500,000 (and there is
a lot of water in that £500,000). And unless
something sudden happens to the zinc market, there
will be a big “surplus” to be added to next half-
year’s profit. The usual practice is to pay finally
for the zinc concentrates delivered in one half-year
at the price averaged in the following half-year.
For book-keeping purposes the metal delivered be-
tween January 1 and June 30 was valued at £22;
but, as remarked just now, the present price is
£27 12s. 6d. (June average was £24 9s. 7d. ;
July, £24 13s. lOd.; August, £26 11s. 2d.) If
the average for this half-year works out at £25 per
ton, then the profit for the past nine months, in-
stead of being £100,106, will be just upon £35,000
more than that. Unfortunately, the market that can
run up swiftly from £22 to £27 can run down just
as swiftly. The outlook is certainly alleged to be
excellent; and so, by the way, was the outlook for
copper when that metal soared up to £llO in 1907.
(In these days it refuses to go up to £58.) The
zinc or spelter market very largely depends upon a
“convention” or honorable understanding among
European smelters ; and though there is no sign of
a crack at present, such foundations are not the
best conceivable. It isn’t possible to arrive at the
price at which it would no longer pay the co. to
produce spelter, because it has got some Alps on
better terms than others; but the figures already
printed show, anyhow, the tremendous importance
of the rackety spelter market to this co.

A bonus of £253 to the staff is deducted from the
half-year’s profits. The co. doesn’t give much infor-
mation about itself; but wherever you dig you get
good results. It has no debts whatever. On the
other side, its assets are a good lot. The capital is
£25,000, and all of that, and £8262 besides, is
represented by cash and public securities; most of
the balance of the reserves is invested in freehold
premises. In 10 years the co. has made this pro-
greSS:“ 1901. 1911.
Liquid assets .. .. £lO,OOO £33,262
Other assets .. .. 23,070 22,762
Liabilities . . . . 1,201 Nil
Thus it has practically the same total of non-liquid
assets as it had 10 years ago, while it has added no
less than £24,463 to its surplus of liquid assets.
That is to say, after paying a dividend of 10 p.c.
p.a., it has in 10 years accumulated an amount equal
practically to the paid capital—a very fine perform-
ance indeed. This accumulation of reserves, invested
in safe securities, is just about as good a guarantee
as one can have that divs. will be maintained at
somewhere about their present level. A 4 p.c. return
on the funds would, in fact, provide of itself an 8 p.c.
div. No doubt there are some risks; the co., for
instance, might misread a will or other instructions,
or misconstrue its powers, and have to part up, like
one or two other trustee cos. did a few years agfl.
But, given careful management, the risk isn’t a very
big one; and, as this co. always makes a feature of
having three or four front-rank lawyers on the
Board, it presumably is in even less than the average
danger of falling into an awkward hole and hurting
either itself or its clients.

In two years ship property has shrunk from £85,350
to £66,750, and that looks like strenuous writing
down; but it has probably been helped by the sale
of some of the co.’s more ancient arks. Two years
ago liquid assets exceeded the liabilities by £19,820;
to-daj' the surplus is £42,711. But, again, that
rapid increase is probably due in part to the con-
version of arks into coin. Anyway, the financial
position is strong, and the co. could increase its
navy without leaning much on its banker. The
least satisfactory feature is that there isn’t much
inducement to increase the navy, for the co. is up
against keen railway competition all the time.

The Foster is one of the combine breweries ; it holds
72,500 shares in the co. that was formed to do the
business that was previously done by half a dozen.
The combine, of course, doesn’t use all the breweries;
it had tlie right to rent or acquire all those that it
wanted, and to disregard the others. For a while
it wanted the Foster brewery, and paid £2OOO a
year for it, but the lease was presently commuted
for £BOOO, and the co. during the past 12 months
has got only £177 as rent of premises, etc., which
cost £88,719, and still stand in the books at
£45,494. However, the co. gains in another way,
for, through the cutting down of exes., the combine
now pays a pretty regular 8 p.c. Its record for the
past three years has been: —
Jan., 1909 .. 3 p.c. p.a. I July, 1910 .. 16 p.c. p.a.
July, 1909 . . Nil Jan., 1911 . . 8 p.c. p.a.
Jan., 1910 .. 6 p.c. p.a. | July, 1911 .. 8 p.c. p.a.
That is a shade under 7 p.c. p.a. for the three
years; but in 1909 the combine spent a great deal
of profit in putting in new plant, etc. How close
it has to sail to the wind to pay the 8 p.c., which
now seems to be a regular thing, can’t be even hinted
at, because it issues no accounts. Eight p.c. means
to the Foster Co. £ 2900 a half-year ; and that pays
all management exes., and leaves enough for an 8
p.c. div. on this co.’s capital ( £57,600), with two
or three hundred over. Besides the share capital the
co. has £BOOO of debenture capital; but, as men-
tioned above, the brewery lease was commuted for
£BOOO, so that the debenture debt is squared. A
sinking fund is, moreover, being accumulated to wipe
out the debentures; a shower of £lOl2 fell into this
fund from some invisible cloud during the past half-
year. (This amount, by the way, is not included
with the profits mentioned in the table; nor is the
sinking fund included with the reserves. Its total
is now £1521.) The co.’s future depends chiefly
upon the maintenance of the combine—-which is prac-
tically assured —and the absence of any tremendous
opposition. The prospects are certainly good. Con-
sidering the size of the reserves, the div. is very
moderate; but then it has to be remembered that a
sum almost equal to the reserves is represented by
the brewery asset which is at least dormant.

These accounts are, as usual, badly presented, and
the whole position is unsatisfactory. The co. started
in leased premises with a capital of £17,500, of
which no less than £15,000 was represented by
goodwill. That goodwill item should by every rule
of sound —not to say honest —finance have been
written off during the currency of the lease, unless
there was an agreement to renew it. As a matter of
fact, not one penny was written off. Subsequently a
new lease was secured; and still nothing was written
off. About three years ago, still another lease was
got; but this time the co. had to pay £5063 for it.
Now, therefore, goodwill (that is to say, the capital
represented b} r nothing visible —merely the right of
the co. to occupy at £1450 a year premises which the
law may or may not license) stands at £20,063.
Some sort of pretence is now being made at writing
down; that is to say, last half-year £337 was written
off “lease, furniture and improvements account,’ and
this term £22s—a most unsatisfactory way of doing
tilings, because the amount written off for the wear
and tear of furniture should be kept separate from
the sinking fund for the extinction of the goodwill
asset. The present capital is £21,000 in 35,000
shares of 155., paid to 125., and £20,063 plus some-
thing spent on “improvements” is paper. After
meeting liabilities, the co. has not got £2OOO of
visible assets. No doubt it may make a fair amount
of profit out of the pub business; but —where is the
capital in the event of anything going wrong? In
fact, where is it even if nothing goes wrong ?
Ivyloe (N.S.W.) sings its third annual
song in a minor key; for things at the No. 5 level
are not what they were at No. 4. The argument
used to run this way. No. 2 was good, and No. 3
was better, and No. 4 was better still, therefore at
No. 5 the lode must be bigger and richer again, and
at No. 6 it must regard the body at No. 5 as a com-
paratively poor relation. It looked all right; but
The Bulletin invited the sanguine people to spend
l|d. on a pear, and stand it stalk up, and consider
how it widened and widened —and then came to a
sudden end. So far as the rich ore is concerned the
end came about 50ft. below No. 4 level, and No. 5 is
a dreadful blank ; the ore channel is there, certainly,
but the values are not. It is possible, no doubt,
that they resume at a greater depth. But the san-
guine people had now better apply their own old
argument. As No. 3 was better than No. 2, and No.
4 better than No. 3, therefore No. 5 was going to be
better than No. 4. On that principle, as No. 5 is
worse than No. 4, No. 6 is liable, to be still worse
than No. 5. There is certainly no evidence that it
won’t be. During the last few days.the lode has, in
fact, been pricked, and, unfortunately, it consists
mainly of the same broken country as No. 5. Driv-
ing upon it may develop something better; but pros-
pects are not brilliant. Fortunately the co. still has
(or had on June 30) about £5OOO of liquid assets to
the good, after providing for liabilities; and it has
(say) 12 month’s’ ore in reserve, and a shaft equip-
ment capable of reaching 1000 ft. The main shaft
will therefore no doubt be continued another lift.

Theoretically reserves are assets held in excess of
the capital; but in practice it doesn’t often pan out
that way with a mining co., because, as a rule, the
mining property is not written down, though it is
mostly a rapidly-wasting asset. In this case, unless
the bottom improves wonderfully, the mine is worth
little, so (barring that wonderful improvement) not
only are there no reserves, but most of the capital is
not there, either. The true movement can best be
shown this way:—
1909. 1910. 1911.
Liquid assets .. .. £10,329 £6318 £7543
Liabilities . . . . 266 1882 1478
Surplus .. .. £10,063 £4436 £6065
Between 1909 and the present, £17,000 has been
paid in divs.; so that the two years’ work has re-
sulted in cash profit, after paying for development
and some plant, of £20,998. Compared with whac
the market hoped for and paid for, it is a woefully
poor performance.
Though A. J. Chappie, late of the Occi-
dental, introduced the vacuum slime plant to the Cobar
district (N.S.W.), he is not connected with the instal-
lation of the process at the Boppy. For the design,
construction and future working of the plant there
the co.’s own metallurgist Williamson is solely re-
sponsible. Also The Bulletin is assured that the
plant isn’t “similar to the one at the Occidental.”
Local expert opinion expects great things of it, and
describes it as “a scheme for which the co.’s officers
have worked hard and successfully, and which will
undoubtedly prove one of the best, as it is also one
of the most unique in the State.”
Heavy London selling of Broken Hill in-
terests, and especially of shares in the zinc concerns,
is beginning to make some Australian operators
wonder whether the zinc-smelters’ “convention” is
the solid thing it is cracked up to be.
The patents litigation over the zinc-flota-
tion processes is to go on. The Elmore people (Ore
Concentration Co.) have told their English share-
holders, by circular, that they are appealing direct
to the Privy Council against the adverse N.S.W.
judgment in' the suit they brought against the Sul-
phide Corporation.

Kangiara (N.S.W.), though it is still
miles away from success, has more hopeful prospects
in the crosscut at the 300 ft. level. The lode, which
is made up of streaks of ore, interspersed with coun-
try rock, has been sampled for 18ft. The best assays
were obtained between 14ft. and 18ft., over a width
of 4ft. ; copner 8.82 p.c., lead 17.21 p.c., silver
140 z., and gold ldwt. Probably, when the formation
is driven on north and south, some solid makes of
ore will be met with. That, anyhow, was the case
in the shallow levels, and only recently the south
face of No. 2 level was showing 2ft. of high-grade
lead ore (36 p.c. lead). For the week, 24 tons of
lead concentrates and 16 tons of copper ore were
sent away. The stuff is rich ; but the shipments
are miserably small in proportion to the number of
ordinary and preference shares.
Some more about the Oroya Exploration
and its world-wide ventures. Yuanmi (W.A.) : The
estimated treatment capacity of the plant is 4500
tons per month. Transvaal (W.A.) : A safe estimate
of ore reserves to date is 48,500 tons, worth 445. per
ton. About 275. per ton should cover exes, and loss
in residues. Queen of the Hills (W.A.) : Work is at
a standstill pending the erection of a mill. Ore in
sight is now computed at 53,510 tons (actual), assay-
ing 575. per ton, and 17,280 tons prospective,
averaging 445. per ton. Babilonia (Nicaragua) :
Development is retarded pending sinking, and ore
treatment is “off” until the undersized mill on the
property has been patched up, when it is hoped
returns will be sufficient to cover exes, until further
equipment can be obtained. The co. holds its next
annual meeting in October, and in the meantime
every nerve is being strained to make its numerous
mines and side-lines look as presentable as possible.

In talking over things at the Associated
meeting chairman Hooper said that the co. now had
an overdraft of £16,000, but against this he wished
it to be remembered that £25,000 had been expended
on new plant during the year. At a later stage of
the proceedings Herman Landau said that, in order
to pacify nervous shareholders, he wished to point
out that the co. had sufficient securities to meet the
overdraft, and still leave a lot in hand. Besides the
£41,000 invested in Consols, and which the market
had since depreciated to £32,000, the co. had
£lOO,OOO sunk in Transvaal 3 per cents., which at
their depreciated price were still worth £94,250.
On tiMnivr tMq fLrw nresent tried to feel comfort-
able, which feeling would no doubt have been con-
siderably intensified had the Board there and then
decided to take no more risks, and distribute the in-
vested capital, minus the amount of overdraft. As
ii is, tile money will probably be dropped in some
foreign spec., and in the end scrip owners will get
nothing. As an earner of dividends the mine seems
to be more hopeless than ever. The discovery at
depth of something better than it has got at present
is its only chance of pulling through.
A case arising out of the sale of the Bull-
finch Main Lode (W.A.) was disposed of in a
London court the other day, when someone named
Thompson was successful in a claim for commission
against the Occident and Orient, Ltd., and others.
Thompson’s case was that the defendants agreed to
give him £lOOO cash and 1000 £1 shares in any
co. formed to work the property, conditionally on
his securing an option. He secured the option, which
was afterwards acquired by the Westralian United
Goldfields, Ltd. —a visionary concern promoted by
the defendants. The defendants pleaded that the
commission was only to be paid to Thompson if he
underwrote £lOOO worth of shares, and as he failed
to do so, they reckoned that they did not owe him
anything. Finally, a verdict by consent was given
plaintiff for £2265, to be reduced to £265 upon the
transfer of 2000 Westralia United Goldfields pre-
ference shares, the market value of which may, on
account of a scotched flotation, be safely estimated
at approximately nil.
Bullfinch West is stated to have purchased
the King of the Hills at Leonora (W.A.), and already
the local papers have begun to profess a belief
that “the co. has got hold of something that will
pay dividends for years.” The show has been
worked off and on for a long while by its former
owner, and his published reason for selling is that
“he wishes to retire from the fields” —good old stock
phrase!
In all probability, ITavilah (W.A.) will
have to give its Black Range venture best, and get
hold of something else. Minus the main shoot, which
has pitched into an adjoining show, the property
has become practically worthless; and although a
notion preyails in official quarters that other payable
gold deposits will be found out east, there is nobody
in the district, from the oldest inhabitant to the
latest arrival, who can give any logical reason why
the mine authorities should cling to that belief. A
little driving would settle the point, however, and
bring matters to a head.
Whim Well Copper Co. (W.A.) unearthed
about 650 tons of high-grade export ore last month,
and now reckons that it has 2300 tons, averaging
14 p.c., awaiting shipment. A howl has been raised
on the co.’s behalf against the proposed Federal ex-
port duty on crude ore. Being near the seaside, this
concern is able to ship stuff to Europe and get the
copper extracted by the foreigner, a trifle cheaper,
probably, than it could be done just yet in the Com-
monwealth. This, coupled with some supplementary
whine about the danger of such a tax becoming too
burdensome to be borne, is said to be the thing that
is distressing the co.’s friends. The real reason for
the outburst of grief, however, is that the imposi-
tion of the duty would tend to put a stop to the
co.’s mania for gouging out the best ore for export,
and would thus compel the co. to carry on mining
operations along more sensible and legitimate* lines.
Financial help has been given the Oroya
Leonesa by the obliging Oroya Exploration, Ltd.,
to enable it to bear up more successfully against the
troubles which beset it in revolutionary Nicaragua.
Nearly a year’s delay in development and equipment
has been entailed by a sanguinary encounter between
those who are running the country and some of
those who aren’t; but as the latter appear to have
had enough for the time being, the co. is hopeful of
getting a move on towards the producing stage with-
out any further hindrance. The profit in sight is
computed at £150,000 ; but this appears to be more
of a guess than an estimate.
* * * *
It is difficult to shake the truth out of a
number of unofficial and conflicting statements.
Nevertheless, Mount Elliott shares have revived on
the strength of a wire from North Queensland that
the Limestone mine has been purchased. A co. that
neglects to give prompt information to its Australian
shareholders opens the way to the very worst kind
of market-manipulation. The Limestone mine is
situated on a creek of the same name, and was
prospected by George Hart. It has been more
thoroughly opened up by a co-operative party, which
has sent away a fairly large quantity of high-grade
oxidised ore to Cloncurry. It is probable that the
owners of the show refused to extend the option,
and thus forced the co. to come to terms.
Mount Morgan gives the speculator a very
indefinite idea of its monthly profit, because nothing
is said about costs. Including middle products that
have yet to be converted into blister, the run for
August gave £74,805. The mundic works treated
9309 tons for 34210 z. fine, which, at £4 4s. lOd.
per oz., runs to £14,510, leaving £60,295 as the
gross return from the furnacing of 23,450 tons of
copper ore, which pans out at the reduced average
of £2 11s. sd. per ton. The annual report to May
31 showed a gross revenue of £953,292 from
334,869 tons, giving an average from all classes of
ore of £2 16s. lid. The mine that can keep that
extraction going is a wonder, and it is owing to
this good return that Mount Morgan was able to
stand up against a bill of £759,316 for the year’s
expenses, and still hand out its usual Is. per quarter.
For two years the expenditure totalled £1,481,038,
but for a variety of reasons the nayments were ab-
normal.
The Bluff colliery (Q.) people have in-
creased their capital from £2OOO to £6OOO. The cry
for the last three years has been “more dollars.” At
one time the Government was asked to help, but the
State wasn’t taking any. All the profits (which have
never been furious) have, except for one little lot,
gone back into the concern. The whole trouble seems
to be the co.’s grabbing propensities. At present,
besides the Bluff Central colliery, with its coke ovens,
it has leases at Blair Athol (130 miles off by rail),
and an interest in the Mammoth Admiralty Co., and
now it has acquired an option over the Columba.
recently deserted by the big Mount Morgan co. “If
they had not gone outside the Bluff,” said the general
manager at a meeting recently, “they would not have
required this £6000.” Still the acquisition of the
Blair Athol leases has some justification, as a mixture .
of Blair Athol and Bluff coal will be a very fine
fuel. Orders thus far run to less than 2000 tons
per month, so there is room for a lot of expansion.
* * * *
At Charters Towers the improvement of
Kelly’s Queen Block reef (1450 ft. formation), par-
allel to the Brilliant and St. George boundary, has
put a little life into the share market. Some time
since Kelly’s crushing of 172 tons realised £894,
and as the reef is showing an improvement in size
and quality, the market is in a hopeful mood. Kelly’s
has also a crosscut at the 657 ft. level to the St.
George reef, which is now being prospected. Mar-
shall’s Queen crushed 594 tons for an average of
£3 4s. per ton, which gives a profit. East Mexican
crushed 577 tons for £2378. Fortnightly returns
like this provide dividends of 6d. per month; the
next one is declared payable on October 6. The
East Mexican’s prospects depend to some extent on
whether the hanging-wall shoot of stone, which is
being worked profitably by Mills’ United in No. 5
level, can be traced into the Mexican. So far this
has not been done.
Great Fitzroy (Q.) estimates its revenue
for August at £11,763. The report states that 152
tons of blister copper were produced, so apparently
Fitzroy expects to get £77 7s. 9d. per ton for its
blister. Working costs amounted to £10,621,
leaving a surplus of £1142. The furnace treated 5038
tons for 152 tons blister, containing copper 151 tons,
gold 8920 z., and silver 28740 z. On the tonnage smelted,
the extraction works out practically 3 p.c. copper
and 3Adwt. gold ; as often happens, the extraction of
gold is above the assays obtained by sampling the
ore sent to the furnace. Apparently, concentration
of the more silicious ore is to be tried again, for
some alterations have been made to the mill.
A Californian prospector has burst out
into a weird motor-caravan, with which he proposes
to prospect the sand deserts of California. The
engine, 33 h.p., besides supplying the power for the
car, will light up the outfit, and drive a small ore-
crushing plant.

should give close attention to the power wasted in

Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturallv for

bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but has some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in' prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap; two large side rods resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws to be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the Commonwealth.* [3

(29/8/1910) : “A young Life Office has many diffi-
culties to contend against, and in its early years the
progress of its business must be comparatively slow;
rapid growth can only be expected when a certain
amount of success has already been attained, and the
business on the books has reached a total of some
magnitude. To this general rule, the Colonial
Mutual is certainly a striking exception. From the
outset the Society has been remarkably successful in
obtaining business, and its rise to a position of
eminence has been exceptionally rapid.”*
