ROBUR
To appear bright and breezy
when you’re attending a bad case
isn’t the easiest thing in the
world, and no matter what
anyone may say, when it’s a
matter of " Life or Death ” a case
will get you down if you let it —
but of course you musn’t let it.
I find when I’m getting the
blues the best thing to do is take
a cup of good, pure tea —sip it
steaming hot, and sip it quickly.
" Robur ” is the purest tea —
its flavour isn’t sickly, it brightens
you up, and does you no harm.
The "No. 1” Grade "Robur”
is really a delightful tea to drink,
and it isn’t expensive tea to
buy. They use it in many of
the Hospitals, and they don’t
generally use anything that’s bad.;
in those places.
The Nurse..
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
BLACK & WHITE.
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 11, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The power of suggestion, or some less
psychic complaint, recently laid low Tas-
manian Governor Barron’s pretty missus,
after she returned from a ball in aid of the
Apple-Garden’s Bush Nurses’ Fund. She is
getting well slowly, after this digression on
a sick bed.
Hobart, according to a confirmed inhabi-
tant, has had a mild epidemic of returned
Ooronator’s junketting, to welcome home the
wanderers. Premier Elliot Lewis and his
dame have unpacked their frills amid a whirl
of toasts, luncheons, and other explosions of
local hospitality. Elliot is alleged to tackle
his food gleefully, with the healthy appetite
of Australia’s “High Conditioner,” George
the Great. One welcome-home feed in Ho-
bart included a private luncheon, at which
the hostess filled Lewis with his best-loved
diet—cream and custard. This was the
moment ear-marked for the putting of an
awkward question to Tassy’s Premier.
While the company waited, Lewis, with
bulging cheeks, stoked cream and custard,
and cleverly dodged the answer behind a
yard or two of table linen.
Hobart’s Dr. Isabel Ormiston has torn
herself away from the eyes and teeth of
State school-children, which she habitually
inspects in the Apple-Island, for a holiday
trip to Sydney’s blue-water front.
Premier Jim McGowen’s wife lost her
heart to the London policeman during the
Crowning uproar. Mrs. Jim chatters joy-
fully about her scamper through overseas’
cities, and when she wasn’t catching taxis,
or counting her luggage, says sne sat clown
and thanked God she was coming back to
Australia. “Mind you, it’s been a fine edu-
cation, and opened my mind to broader
ideas —anyhow to a better knowledge of how
folks live in other parts ; and it’s given me a
greater appreciation of the splendid oppor-
tunities Australia offers to those who are
determined to succeed. In England the
poorer class seemed so down that they didn’t
want to get up. I’m only giving my own
impression, of course, but the most hopeless
part of the social problem in London was
the crushed outlook of these poor souls—•
‘the submerged tenth,’ they call them —who
don’t seem to have a spark of the Austra-
lian spirit to try and help themselves.
They’re submerged—and why should anyone
on God’s earth be allowed to get right down
under like that? I’m glad I didn’t see the
Thames Embankment in winter —they told
me about it. However, there is a bright and
beautiful side to London life, and I try to
remember only the pleasant picture.”
Mrs. Jim stayed on the eighteenth floor of
a New York sky-scraper, tossed three ostrich
tails on her head in the presence of British
royalty, wandered in English lanes, and has
talked Australia right through her trip, at
the rate of about 70 miles an hour. And she
has done it with such success that a rush of
desirable immigrants may be expected here
shortly. She’s easing off steam just now',
and returns to the broom and dust-pan of
domestic life.
Dr. and Mrs. W. Chisholm, of this patch,
when last heard of, were climbing Swiss
tourist resorts.
Hon. Sec. Miss Compton Legge and Hon.
Treas. Mrs. A. Fraser are sitting down to
get their breath after exhaustive labors on
behalf of Stray Dogs in general, and the
Neutral Bay Home for mislaid beasts in
particular. The recent skating Carnival in
North Sydney Coliseum pushed a stout
cheque into the kennels’ cash-box, and Mrs
Fritz Hart, who incidentally slipped down-
stairs in the middle of her sprint for the
Cause, is getting over her bruises.
Mrs. Lister-Lister poured out tea for the
JR.A. Society’s visitors on Saturday after-
noon.
The Medical Congress, which hasn’t fallen
on its own and each other’s necks in this
■city for 18 years, is dribbling into every
well-known tavern in the town. About 800
members of the B.M.A. will (d.v. and a
locum tenens permitting) meet together for
work and play in a few days. Officially, the
spree opens at the Uni. on September 10.
An evening party, which Dr. R. H. Todd is
helping to push into its place, occurs on the
19th, in the Town Hall. The guests will
not be entirely fed on music, though Henri
stael, Mabel Batchelor and Alice Bryant
are going to take a hand. This Pill and
Powder and Porous Plaster function will
also witness the beauty of the high kick in
a dance by clever Minnie Hooper’s pupils.
The Ernest Wunderlichs, now in Europe,
drop in on a Bayreuth post-card to a Svdney
friend. “Supper washed down with Moselle
from the lovely old cellar, after hearing
fourth day of ‘the Ring’ (‘Twilight of the
Gods’), a magnificent performance. It began
at 4 p.m.. and lasted till 9.45 p.m.” Bay-
reuth, by the way, the home of the elder
Wunderlichs, has an unquenchable thirst
for Wagner.
Does .Melbourne claim Miss Sylvia Yarra,
mentioned at the reception at Claridge’s
Hotel (London), in July?
Mrs. John McCormack is at present in
Lewisham Hospital, where a slight opera-
tion keeps her away from this week's menu
of Skylark pie at Her Majesty’s.
Before this reaches you, the State school
child will have decorated the Sydney Cricket
Ground with flags, a living picture of G.R.
and his gold hat, and other holiday sports.
Six or seven thousand youngsters, decked
out in new clothes for the occasion, will thus
give the local rag-trade a fresh impetus.
In the Town Hall, the other day, there
was a great rally of infants who were
heavily branded as “Christian Endeavorers.”
Rev. Varcoe Cock instructed these babes to
consider the ant —an appropriate change
from the busy bee who is usually hurled at
knickerbocker meetings. The ant is a foolish
beast, who brings half his troubles on him-
self by climbing mountainous grass blades
with gigantic loads on his back, instead of
walking round the obstacles. He is the em-
bodiment of Inherent Cussedness. Mark
Twain considered the ant one of Creation’s
fools, and the infant Endeavorer who carries
too heavy a swag of good resolutions on his
back is liable to develop the ant-like pro-
pensity of rolling in the dust.
The Plain and Fancy Corroboree, last
week, in aid of the North Sydney Women’s
and Children’s Hospital, is expected to
scrape together about £l3O when the cash
is counted in catmittee meeting.
The gilded feathers of modern song-birds
are very different from the shabby plumage
of the Italian opera companies that sang
through Europe in the ’6o’s and ’7o’s of last
century. The conduct of a prima donna of
grand opera, who washed her lingerie and
hung it out to dry—without a blush —in the
window of her hotel bedroom, drew the pro-
tests of the proprietor, but was otherwise not
considered eccentric. Provincial Italy, with
a large population of duchesses, countesses
and such, in poor circumstances, was as-
tonished at nothing—in which it resembled
Papua. G. A. Sala, in the early ’7o’s,
stayed at the Hotel Victoria in Venice,
where a troupe of operatic artists and the
proprietor spent their spare moments in
spirited arguments re the drying of operatic
underlinen on front window sills.
MISS ESTA D'ARGO, the Sydney soprano,
who is to tour Australia with Mr. Ben
Davies , the English tenor.
The short and fat, who nurse a grudge
against the present silhouette fashions in
skirts, have to remember with gratitude
that they have dodged the one-button even-
ing glove, to which the “Traviata” period
condemns Violetta so long as grand opera
walks the earth. It is a lamentable article,
and worthy of all condemnation.
The long arm of coincidence pulls three old
student friends together in the Melba Opera
Co. —John McCormack, M. Kaufmann and
Cristiani, who once shared the same cage in
Milan. The long arm has a wonderful drag-
ging power. If it could only be harnessed to
a plough it would dig up all the Western
District in about two hours.
Samson loses his Titian curls to Delilah
(in Saint-Saens’s massive opera), during the
second act, while a large thunderstorm is
hurrying across the stage. Delilah’s home
in the Valley of Soreck is the shearing shed.
Eleanora de Cisneros, as the Philistine lady,
achieves this clip in a glamor of azure blue,
with a silver-hoar-frost glittering on its milk-
white tunic. The same act reveals Eleanora-
Delilah in another device of Satan for the
downfall of the hefty Israelite —a drape of
royal blue shimmer, with a Tyrian purple
sheen in its subtle folds. One of Israel’s
worst business transactions on record is the
result. Eleanora-Delilah, a magnificent pil-
lar of round, white flesh, is a colossal suc-
cess of physical beauty, and Samson-Zeni,
who’s six feet three in his socks, hasn’t a
hundred to one chance of saving his thatch.
Abimelech (Ivaufmann) lost his yellow
pot-hat, as well as his life, at one produc-
tion of “Samson and Delilah,” and died
without a wriggle, in his bare head. Abime-
lech is killed so early in the opera that Mr.
Kaufmann is free to accept engagements for
supper, while Samson-Zeni is trying to keep
his hair on. It’s a wonderful growth, with
a thick forest of long auburn curls.
Eleanora-Delilah gets away from her
classic pose in purple, when the Strong Man
at Her Majesty’s is girding up his loins to
tackle the pillars of the Temple—final
eclipse in the third act. She covers her
finger joints with jewels the size of small
tombstones, or thereabouts, flashes great
golden flowers on her head, and glitters from
neck to ankles in plumage of blue, green and
gold that shatters the peacock’s pretensions
to consider himself a well-dressed singer.
Grand onera has flooded this city at night
with diamonds, purple and fine linen, motors,
and other signs that Capital still haunts
these parts. The first night of “La Bohfeme,”
with Melba as Mimi, filled various streets
near Her Majesty’s with petrol fumes from
goggle-eyed motors, and language from pedes-
trians who had to walk round a block to get
on to the right kerbstone.
Bassich, the Mimi whose acting was the
star turn of “Boheme” in this hamlet some
years ago, had a figure rather stouter than a
wooden match, mostly wore her melancholy
little head tied up in a black shawl, scat-
tered a consumptive cough across the Bo-
hemians’ attic, and, judging by her clothes,
earned about a day as a work-girl, and
found herself. Her harrowing end—an as-
tounding piece of realism —always sent a
sniffle through the theatre. Melba, better-
dressed, gave a lighter reading of the part,
sang divinely, and, on Saturday night, had
her death prolonged by a riotous curtain
that hung up at one end when it ought to
have come down.
Russian Axarine, the slim Musetta of
“BohSme,” showed the 1830 coiffure in fair
corkscrew curls that dangled over her ears.
The trousers of the period were worn with
cheerful resignation by all the Bohemians.
Bagged at the hips, with a wisp of material
at the ankle, Marcello-Scandiani’s 1830 leg
drapery was laid out in a large Highland
gathering pattern of plaid bars. Habitual
first-nighters were tightly wedged into a
crowded house. Dr. Stewart McKay, Mrs.
George Vivers in black velvet heavily
sprinkled with diamonds, and Mrs. Phil
Morton with an agitated green plume firmly
clasped to her head with a cameo, were in
a box. Overhead the tuneful McQuades sat
in their first-night pen. Front stalls—space
being worth something handsome per inch—
were just able to breathe without hustling
the orchestra. Mrs. Steer Bowker and Mrs.
Schute were half a bar behind Mr. Ange-
lini’s baton ; the Arthur Allens—Mrs. Allen’s
white lace mantle being the last remark in
theatre wraps—were further back, with
daughter Joyce clad in a blush pink film of
chiffon. Geranium-red gowns flecked the
stalls with joyous color. Miss Von Arn-
heim’s osprey nodded in front of Mr. Justice
Gordon’s party, and the Margaret Thomas
smile was in its usual place.
Marshall-Hall heard the beating wing of
the Angelini of his dreams in Melbourne,
anu fled to this Mecca of music for the first
night of “Boheme.” His black thatch, with
a rim of eyebrow, rose in agitated crescendo
above the standing-room-only row in Her
Majesty’s dress circle, when the Skylark’s
silvery song poured into the McCormack
gold mine in a duet.
On the first night, the “Faust” chorus
had a slight mishap to its singing. Wayda-
Marguerite averted the Angelini wrath when
on its way to interview the offenders —pre-
sumably hiding at that moment in the cellars
—by dragging him on to the stage. Thunders
of applause smoothed the Angelini forelock
back to normal, and the generous Wayda
tore her bouquets to pluck flowers for the
director’s ruffled feelings—a climax not anti-
cipated by the givers of the vegetables.
According to the gospel of Edmund Burke,
certain districts in France are the hardest
places out of Heaven to sing in. “They’ll
hiss and applaud a singer in the same item,
tear it to pieces in one bar, and clap it in
the next,” says the experienced Burke.
Melba mostly lives in Her Majesty’s
Theatre, supported by a 1 o’clock sandwich
and a lap of milk, and dives into every detail
of the evening’s production, with a physical
energy which makes the chorus mop its agi-
tated brow.
At last Thursday’s production of “Samson
and Delilah,” the chorus seemed to go to the
devil, or some other well-known musical ad-
dress, for about five minutes. This sprint
occurred in the first act, but Angelini
dragged the voices back to the baton before
the casualties were serious.
Artist Philip Streeton has been scratch-
ing Fogland for a nice-looking young John
the Baptist model with his head on his
shoulders —-with apologies to Maud Allan,
who carried the head as a separate institu-
tion. He wanted a blonde youth, with a
vigorous, upstanding presence, and a John
Storm manner, carefully disguised under a
sac suit. Streeton eventually snared young
Steffani MacDonald’s fair phiz as a study
for the Baptist portrait, which is likely to be
hung in the next Paris Salon. Steff., a
good-looking youngster now warbling on the
London stage, is the musical chick of Mr.
and Mrs. Will Mac Donald —she was song-
bird Kate Slattery, of these parts—and cut
his teeth to the trills of grand opera in his
home-nest.
Contralto Ella Caspers has snatched the
Royal Academy of Music (London) silver
medal, from all competitors, for her fine
singing in the July exam.
Esther Kahn has written some joyous
music for a convivial ballad about tankards
that clink in the early morn. “Old English
Tavern Song” is the name of this flowing
bowl melody, and Nicholson and Co., Ltd.,
forward it. The chorus is arranged for four
voices.
Mrs. Slapoffski is going to pack up her
voice for the Cold Land in January, the
Crystal Palace and Albert Hall being in-
cluded in her overseas’ song burst. On
Monday afternoon some citizens will as-
semble together at the Australia, to consider
a farewell concert to the departing Throat,
and thereby hope to convey to the Warble
that will shortly vanish how much she will
be missed.
Manager E. J. Kilpatrick, of the Alham-
bra Theatre (Haymarket), is looking for
feminine scalps for his “All Australia
Crowning Glory Competition,” and intends
to hand out £4O worth of prizes. The best
heads of hair will be biographed, and will
eventually travel over the globe to show
what can be done in the way of thatch when
an Australian woman is underneath. It’s to
be an all-Australian competition, and the
judges will be the public. Twenty pounds
will go to the woman with the best all-
round summit of roof glory—color, texture
and abundance all considered. The girl with
ropes of hair will get away with £lO if her
ropes are longer than those of any other
girl, this being a question of long continu-
ance only. The best-groomed hair will cap-
ture £5. Finally, a special reward of £5 for
the handsomest crop that children up to
fourteen can grow (without neglecting their
lessons) is the last prize which biograph
Kilpatrick hurls into the sweep.
Hugh Ward has taken a house at Potts
Point, known in old days as Percy Lodge.
In 1850 it was occupied by Mrs. Eleanor
Terry. She gave way to her relative, Major
Wingate, who resided there for many years,
and died there. Recent owners renamed the
place Bellevue. It is sandwiched in by the
old homes of James Martin (Clarens) and
Joseph Thompson (Tor Lodge). The latter
is being demolished to make way for C. J.
Roberts’s house (Chatsworth by name).
Woolloomooloo Day Nursery got a fresh
incentive to continue in well-doing out of
the Palace Theatre matinee for its benefit,
the other day. Ethel Irving was the garnish
of the menu in “Dolly’s Little Bills,” and
wept beautifully over the ancient domestic
problem, “How many new hats are neces-
sary to the happiness of a pretty woman?”
“Golden Lilies,” a new excuse to waltz, is
written by Lai Paton in swinging rhythm
and several flats. Beale and Co., Ltd., pub-
lish this melodious trifle.
Airman “Beaumont,” who won the Daily
Mail £lO,OOO prize, intends to remain in the
navy when he isn't careering through the
atmosphere. His chief trouble, at present, is
the daily post, which is over-weighted with
offers of marriage from girls who saw the
“Beaumont” £lO,OOO smile in the ha’penny
papers. Emancipated woman is taking the
bit in her mouth with a vengeance.
New York Evening News recently re-
corded a queer spasm of street-cleaning in
Atlanta. Society women tucked up their
sleeves, pinned up the tails of their frocks,
and, broom in hand, dashed at dirty streets
and unsavory back yards. Six thousand
housewives joined in the scrimmage, and the
battle of the broom was waged against dirt,
with terrific fury, till nightfall. Then a
clean city ate its supper and went to bed.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

“ROCKY SECTION.”
By SYDNEY PARTRIGE.
ILLUSTRATED BY E. H. MURRAY.
Price, 1/-. Post Free, 1/1.
This is an Australian Love Story pure and simple, and as such very refreshing
and very welcome. A young Queenslander falls in love with a fascinating girl from
town, but is jilted in favor of a titled man with money. He becomes a misogynist of the
deepest dye, and buries himself in a veritable wilderness called Rocky Section—miles
from anywhere. One day he discovers a girl near his place, who has evidently lost
her way, and fainted from sheer exhaustion. He takes- her to his hut, and revives
her, only to find that she has lost her memory, and cannot tell where she comes
from. He hates women with a bitter hatred, but is on the horns of a dilemma. What
happens? “The way out” is most interesting, but you must read it for yourself.

What the Pianola Piano will mean in your home: —
It will mean that you can have music—the best of music—whenever you want
it.
It will mean that you will always have an ever-ready means of entertaining
friends.
It will mean a constantly growing familiarity with an abundance of beautiful
music you may not at present even realise is in existence.
It will mean an important educational factor for the children —a method of
making them familiar with the world’s great composers, thus supplying a deficiency
in the educational system of most schools.
It will mean that you can familiarise yourself with the grand operas that are
to be sung this season by Madame Melba’s Grand Opera Co., in advance of their
production, or it will keep you in touch with the successes of the comic-opera stage.
And, above all. it will mean that, instead of just listening to music, you can play it.
No one can tell you the tremendous fascination of producing music. You must
experience it yourself. And, when you do, you will realise for the first time in your
life you are learning what musical enjoyment is. The STECK Pianola Piano,
having a 1 lanola inside its case, may be played by anyone. The presence of the
Pianola, however, does not interfere with the piano’s use for hand playing and
practice.

DELAY IS DANGEROUS.
There is no class of ailment that affects the system
quicker than bowel complaints, and any delay in
treating diarrhoea or colic is dangerous. Chamber-
lain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy is the
only remedy that is absolutely reliable in cases of
this kind, and as some member of the family is
always liable to an attack at any time, it should
always be kept in the house.*
The courage and endurance which helped
Burgess in his great swim across the Eng-
lish Channel are the qualities which, when
aided by wisdom, conquer in every realm of
enterprise and achievement. And the wis-
dom part of the contract —the best to inspire
the best deeds—is that inspired by D.C.L.,
the Whisky of the Wise.*

MELBOURNE.
Opposite Princes Bridge Station.
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbournk, September 11, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Federal Parliament took down the
shutters again on Tuesday, and joked on the
doorstep. The small G.-G. who did the job
is as decorative as any previous figurehead
we have supported. His inches are few but
neat, and what there are of them are as long
as anybody else’s inches, and his feet reach
the ground easily when he is standing up.
He travels to his job with an unusually
large and elegant suite. On Tuesday, he was
accompanied by half a dozen scarlet, and the
same number of black and gold, military cir-
cumstances. He was punctual to the stroke,
and was forced to tvait several minutes for
members, who were engaged elsewhere. The
suite fidgeted impatiently; but his Ex. filled
in the wait easily by pulling off his white
gloves with immense deliberation. If the
Reps, and Senators had delayed much longer
than they did, he might have removed his
coat and hung it on the nearest nail like the
ordinary toiler when he gets down to graft.
However, just about the time he had fin-
ished stroking his gloves flat, the Clerk smote a
bell, an usher approached, and, several lengths
behind, came the dilatory politicians strung-
out like a Cup field. They had been delayed
by a proclamation or a count or something.
His Ex. then read the Speech in a small
voice, and everybody listened attentively. The
Official Black Beetle, or Usher of the Black
Rod, made his debut in ordinary trousers,
the picturesque but out-of-date knee-breeches
having been deducted from the Ushery. The
magnificent McCall, who has been moved up
one, witnessed the performance of his suc-
cessor with critical attention. The Mace,
like the- knee-breeches of Black Rod, was not
visible, having been left by order in the pro-
perty room. Bruce Smith seemed perturbed
about it, but business went on as usual.
Prime Minister Fisher gave a special tea-
party in the Senate Club Room after the
opening ceremony, to welcome Mrs. Fisher,
just home that day from America. She
stepped off the Sydney train at half-past one,
and came straight along to Parliament
House. , A black and white dress, which was
almost covered by a beautiful lace coat, was
the homing costume. Mrs. Pearce helped to
hostess the pleasant breaking of bread, and
the wives of the other Ministers assisted in
a homelike way. Mrs. P.M.G. Thomas was
in blue with a cute punchinello hat, and Mrs.
Hughes in grey. Lady Denman was in
purple, with waving plumes, and Miss Quirk
in sage green.
The dusty gallery seats were crowded to
suffocation, and the costly raiment of the
gallery guests cleaned the place up nicely.
Many inquiries were made by angry women
as to the whereabouts of the Usheress of the
Feather Duster.
A new and rather anxious G.-G. got the
invalid Chamberlain, Victor Hood, out of his
quiet private hospital, and implored his assist-
ance just once before he set out on the pre-
scribed voyage and extended holiday. The
dutiful sick man, looking very shaken, ap-
peared at the elbow of his Ex. at the opening
of the Australian Parliament, and there was
a relieved look on the faces of the brand-new
and nicely-upholstered suite.
The G.-G. and Lady’s racing carnival in-
tentions for Sydney and Melbourne are an-
nounced betimes by a typed programme from
the Chamberlain’s office. The exactitude of
the dates is not vouched for, but they are
given as “probable.” At Sydney Govern-
ment House there is to be a ball on Friday
the 29th inst., another ball on Monday, Octo-
ber 2, and a garden party on the Thursday
following. The Cup week events for Mel-
bourne are a dinner party at Government
House on Monday. November 6, a ball on
the Wednesday (Bth), and a garden party
on Friday (10th) —the three evenings of the
days when Flemington racecourse is closed
for cleaning and repairs.
| Governor Fuller opened the Daffodil Show
since I last wrote. He resolutely refused to
speechify until he had been shown the blos-
soms. When, at last, he uttered the longed-
for incantations, he accompanied them with
the statement that he was no gardener, and
could not speak with authority. “But,”
added he, “ my wife is a gardener.” The
Fuller lady had, however, made that fact
plain to all during the preliminary inspec-
tion. When his Ex. would merely remark,
“Now that’s a pretty flower,” her Ex. would
criticise or admire like an expert.
“Isn’t that name spelt wrongly?” she asked,
pointing at one polysyllabic label. A hurried
examination showed that it was.
Governor Denman must have “cottoned”
to Arthur Levey—of the Theatre Royal—
when they fellow-voyaged from England. His
Ex. lately presented Arthur with a silver
box, containing myriads of cigarettes, and
inscribed, souvenirly, on the lid.
Melba’s brother Frank secured his trea-
sure of a lady-love in holy bonds last week,
and bore her off to Sydney for the new
moon. On their return to Victoria the happy
pair will domicile with Dad Mitchell at
Doonside, where a suite of rooms has been
beautifully furnished for them.
About the beginning of September each
year the aldermen shuffle themselves, and
new mayors are chained up. Malvern, the
sacred city that houses the State Governor,
lias just started Worshipping solicitor Rupert
Wilks —a boyishffooking young man who is,
figuratively speaking, much too slight to
wear the rabbit skin. He has only been a
councillor for 15 months. Mrs. Rupert—a
fine type of the young Australian matron —
makes a handsome mayoress. Mrs. McKin-
ley, wife of the retiring mayor, was pre-
sented with a twinkling string of diamonds
by the Malvernites, who also placed a dia-
mond gaud upon his ex-Worship’s finger.
The other day, the woolly town of Hamil-
ton flocked to the wedding of Maud Fenton
and William Goodwin Beggs. Auctioneer
John Fenton gave his daughter away, and
Mrs. Fenton, in a wonderful black gown,
shed a few quiet tears over the farewell.
Nell and Molly Fenton, Miss Beggs and Miss
Philip made four pretty bridesmaids in lav-
ender and old lace. The newly-weds motored
away for the honeymoon, and, in the even-
ing, Mrs. Fenton and the bridesmaids
hostessed a dance in the Town Hall.
Ballarat’s wealthiest bachelor, Thomas
Loughlin, has allowed the Voice to breathe
o’er him in its richest brogue. The event
took place in Kilkenny, Ireland, and Thomas
and his bride are how inhaling the air of
freedom in Swiss cantons and other lofty
situations. The groom, who is 42, was long
regarded as incurably single. He owns a
big place at Warrenheip, and is reputed to
be worth a quarter of a million. He has
at different times handed about £15,000 to
various sporting and charitable institutions.
Also, he is known to have embarrassed the
R.G. Church with £60,000 in prodigal gifts.
For this he has naturally incurred the dis-
like of the Pope, who made Mr. Loughlin a
Knight of St. Gregory, to show his holy dis-
approval of such extravagance.
George Porteous, of the cherubic counten-
ance and silver hair, is taking to matri-
mony after a long bachelor career. The
lady with whom he is going into partner-
ship is the pretty widow of actor-manager
Maxwell.
The record-breaking Show is over; 153,350
people have witnessed the prize cow, the
windmills and the pumps; and at least
100,000 of them are suffering from swollen
feet. For some mysterious reason, the vice
of sitting down and resting is discouraged
by the Royal Agricultural Society as much
as possible. It is done by supplying only
a limited number of seats. The result is that
there are plenty of corns in Eygpt, and most
of them are aching. People with loose half-
crowns in their pockets have the grandstand
to sit in—if they get there early enough.
At parade time, that perch is crowded to
suffocation point. In the grounds the only
seats are attached to tea-tables which are
haunted by waiters. You have to eat and
drink, in order to occupy these chairs. If
you don’t, you must retire in favor of some
ravening sorefoot. You have, so to speak,
to rest your feet by subterfuge, and at extra
expense. The Society is knee-deep in cash,
and might, without injuring itself, provide
places where patrons could rest their weary
soles for nothing. Some shady trees, green
lawns and seats would cause such a down-
pour of blessings to descend that the com-
mittee would be forced to put up its
umbrella.
Golf is still raging in this locality. The
players and their friends are now wandering
in clubbed herds on the Metropolitan Links.
The male golfer is, occasionally, a vain crea-
ture. I recently noticed one of him with
cerise rings round the tops of his grey stock-
ings. Cherry-colored rosettes would have
finished the effect; but, apparently, he had
not thought of it. Architect Philip Hudson
has been altering the club house of the Royal
at Sandringham, and making it larger, so
that it will accommodate more residential
members. The rooms being built are lined
with polished hardwood, and perched high
over the terraces. These dens, which are
adorned with camp beds and simple furniture,
command fine views of rolling turf on one
side and surf on the other; also, you climb
to them up a breezy outdoor stairway.
One of the keenest players during the
practice games for the recent tournament was
a young priest, Father Roche.
The extinct pub in Victoria Parade, which
has been purchased by the Marshall-Hall
Conservatorium people needs a lot of altera-
tion before it becomes a fit place for top
notes to soar in. To help the building fund
along, the musical folk put their energies
into a matinee at Her Majesty’s, last Thurs-
day afternoon. The theatre was as full as if
it had been packed by hydraulic pressure.
Fortunately the Agricultural Show had
drawn many trainloads of people out of the
city, otherwise the place might have got over-
loaded and burst. The fat of the land was
present in large quantities, and the numerous
boxes were full of the very best social butter.
The Denman lady, wrapped in white ermine,
led the applause vigorously; she does not
pat her gloves delicately in the manner of
most Sassiety leaders, but really concusses
her hands, Pretty Lady Barttelot peeped
over the ledge of her pen in a black hat with
a delicate and beautiful veil. The rest of
her costume was black and tailor-made. Miss
Quirk, who was also present, is a droopy
young woman who affects pastel colors and
“art” cuts to her gowns. On Saturday, I
thought of the dressmaker in “Lady Fred-
erick,” who, doubtful of the compliment of
being called a “real artist,” remarks: “Well,
I would be ashamed of making some of the
dresses hung in the Royal Academy.” Sev-
eral aides filled in the odd spaces in the two
vice-regal boxes. A Sydney man occupied
another of the perches, and chatted with
three pretty girls in smart spring frocks.
Dolores, who sang a sweet song or two,
afterwards dimpled in a box. She was
wrapped in a scarlet-streaked grey kimono.
She did her warbling in white, with pearls
and diamonds sparkling and glistening on
her shoulders, while the pink health tint of
her real live complexion came and went
and came again in the full glare of the
footlights. Mrs. Pinschof, who waved butter-
colored feathers, entertained the artists in
her coop after they had made their stage
appearances.
The programme was on a high plane
throughout. Marshall-Hall, with the help
of his orchestra and chorus, rendered two
slices of “Faust.” Another weighty item
was a scene from Lytton’s “Richelieu,” with
Harry Hill as the Cardinal, and ’Lisbeth
Pinschof as Julie. An act from “Traviata”
and a playlet, “The Littlest Girl,” done by
Desmond and Winter Hall, also helped to
stretch the proceedings from 2 o’clock till a
quarter past 6, when the house rose stiffly
and tottered out in search of its evening re-
freshments. Meanwhile, however, the Gee-
Gees, being pledged to a complete patronage
and presence, wandered from one to other of
the two boxes like caged lions. They changed
seats, and varied their tete-d-tetes until every
chair and bit of standing room in the pens
must have had a whole series of occupants.
Four hours at a matinee, with the pangs of
hunger increasing as the time goes on, is an
ordeal indeed.
The Past Students (not such very Old
Girls) of the Loretto Convent foregathered
at Albert Park, last Monday night, and Pro-
fessor Tucker lectured to them on the abuse
of the King’s English in the home-made brand
of literature. The King’s English is mauled
by pen and tongue in sixty-nine different
ways in the land where it grows, and it has
to emigrate to Australia to get reasonably
fair treatment. The Australian is forced by
law to acquire a decent working knowledge
of the language, and, although he is occa-
sionally accused by tourists and others of
possessing a twang, a Caucasian from North
Bananaland has no difficulty in understand-
ing a white native of Westralia. It is not
so in England—at least I am told it isn’t.
Almost every county has its own brand of
dialect, and the native of one town spills
much beer in explaining to the native of
another hamlet what it was he really meant
to say before the disturbance took place.
After the lecture nice girls wearing blue and
silver badges handed round consoling refresh-
ments —and they were needed.
The ticket design for the forthcoming
Artists’ Ball, to be held in the Town Hall,
on the 15th, is signed by Laurie Tayler. It
shows a hornet-like dancer in black and scar-
let waving a mask before a fat, laughing
Pierrot, whilst a sad-looking Jester looks
on. The proceeds of the frivol go to help
the Marshall-Hall Conservatorium people
convert their extinct pub into a suitable
place for sonatas and other softgoods. The
students of the music factory who, for years
past, have been the tenantry of the Artists,
are selling the tickets as rubber shares sold
during the late, lamented boom. The Art
Students are reported to be laying in stocks
of feathers, with the intention of disguising
themselves as a tribe of Red Indians. The
unfurnished condition of several roosters I
have seen lately supports the rumor.
“The Arcadians,” as the Clarke and Mey-
nell comic opera crowd is always dubbed,
wandered through Melbourne the other day
on their way west. They overran town in
cheerful groups for a few hours; then, with
a waving of handkerchiefs, they resumed
their pursuit of the setting sun.
Henry Lingham is about to reappear on
the mat at the Austral Salon, and wrestle
with old dramatic subjects in Graeco-Roman
style. The event is fixed for Tuesday next.
Arthur Coppin and the Misses Burns and
Lazarus will assist the dauntless Lingham
in a series of scenes from classic story, “cor-
rectly costumed and staged.” Just now they
are all busy in the preparation of early
Roman and Greek “atmospheres,” which will
be bottled, as it were, and uncorked on the
eventful night. The native atmosphere of
the Salon, being charged with modern gases,
is unsuitable to blank verse dialogues be-
tween Hero and Leander, or Marc Antony
and Cleopatra.
During this tour, Dolores gathered
material for a unique Australian portiere
which is to hang in her home in the south of
France. It consists wholly of eucalyptus
buds, of which she gathered a few barrels
whilst in Maoriland. All the members of
the co. helped with the collecting. They
took long walks into the bush, and came, back
laden. The best haul was made when they
came upon a lonely splitter who was felling
trees. They acquired a few bushels there,
and the hospitable bush worker spent his
off time in carting them into town ; also, he
provided the party with milk and home-made
cake from his bachelor store. The sweet
singer had been ill before she started her
bush walks, and the doctor, with that fine
disregard for other people’s arrangements
which is characteristic of the profession, had
prescribed a long holiday and rest —in the
middle of a tour! Dolores shook her head
resolutely, and continued on her musical way.
However, much to her delight, the open-air
hunt for eucalyptus buds restored her health.
A patient jeweller bored the holes in the
buds, and the singer strung them in her
spare time. The color effect —dull green and
bronze —is very artistic.
Dolores has rural tastes. She keeps pedi-
gree cows, pigs, dogs and other animals at
her place, which is also the old home of
Madame Vandour, her inseparable companion
on all her journeys. Whenever they get
breathing time, they make for the peaceful
retreat. Antonia is almost continually
travelling, and never rents a house
since her experience as a full-blown
householder in London. During a pil-
grimage to the Show, last week, she sur-
prised her party by lingering critically before
each cow and pig. The lower chest notes
of many pigs are very fine. Also, if skilfully
irritated or excited with offerings of food,
they get off some delicious squeals in the
upper register.
The Catholic Ladies’ College has a Kin-
dergarten, and the Kinder had a blow-out
on Friday night. The very small pupils
dressed as grown-ups, and danced and
supped in adult manner. There was a Jose-
phine in full regalia on a miniature mea-
sure of about an inch to the foot, and a
Queen of Hearts had it much her own way.
Molly Holden, in pink roses and a torchon
frock and a mutch bonnet, was so well
dressed as to take a prize, and so begins her
feminine craze for finery and frippery. The
youngest Brenan sister, who is a big
C.L.C. girl, coached the youngsters in their
dance, which delighted mommers gazed upon
from a dais. The C.L.C. full-grown ball
was an affair of great moment about a
month ago, so this frivol was for kiddies
onlv.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The tote continues to shatter the last frail de-
fences of the bookmaking cause. In Tattersall’s
Handicap, on Saturday, Radlunga paid £6l. Peal
gave his backers over £l9 in the Spring Handicap,
and Netherton returned £lO 6s. for second place in
the Hurdles. How, in view of evidence like that,
backers in N. S. Wales and Victoria go on enduring
the old Moses is a mystery to all rational Adelaideans.
There weren’t many women at this meeting, the vice-
regal pen being empty. A spill in front of the stand
resulted in a broken leg for Sir Grenville, who’ had
to be destroyed.
The Hunt Club went to Salisbury on race dry.
Rain had made the roads sloppy, but the going for
the horses was excellent. About 500 road followers
saw most of a splendid run. A good many people
motored up from the city, 12 miles distant, and
others came by special train. Mr. and Mrs. Frank
Russell fed all hands at Paralowie at the end of
the day’s sport.
The Simpson Newlands (he of Murray Waters
fame) have been trying to sell their Burnside home
for some time. They have at last found a purchaser
in H. T. Ward.
Undelcarra has been the scene of much hospitality,
and at one time a hunt tea could be counted on
there as a natural part of the season’s programme.
The writer of “Paving the Way” has recently suffered
from a sharp attack of measles, but he's on the
mend now.
The Spring Show is due next week, as is duly
foreshadowed by fearsome bargains in the windows
of the cheaper shops; strange faces (and stranger
clothes) in the streets, and a cheerful disregard of
schedule time on the part of up-country trains. If
some of the cockies aren’t slain during the pending
festival it will be an everlasting mystery, for the
condition of our streets beggars description. Half of
the busiest corners are neatly railed off, while some
orgy of rail-setting or road-mending takes place
within, and as Show horses are usually led casually
down the most crowded thoroughfares, on the jour-
ney to and from the Show grounds, there should be
some excitement when they catch their first affrighted
glimpse of the electric trams.
There’s trouble in the Education Department. A
Mount Gambier school-teacher was dismissed, and
demanded a Ministerial inquiry. She got the in-
quiry, but apparently no satisfaction, so she has now
appealed to the Law, and Director Williams is cited
as defendant in a case to be heard next week.
When the W.O.T.U. gets hold of a notion, it clings
to it like a demented limpet. At present it is agi-
tating furiously re wines being allowed at the P.A.C.
Old Collegians dinner. The ladies have nothing par-
ticular to do with the college, and their acquaintance
with the merits of the case is indicated by the fact
that they urge pathetically that the accursed grape-
juice shall be kept from “college boys” —though all
the old collegians are grown up, and many are
hoary-headed. But trifling things like that don’t
weigh with your true-blue teetotal enthusiast.
Dr. Basedow has resigned his job of protecting
the N. Territory aborigines. “Geology” Brown is
also resigning, and it may be that the doctor hopes
to get back to his old loves—fossils and stones.
J. R. Beck, who up to now has worked for the
A.M.P., and in the intervals secretaried the Adelaide
Hunt Club and played lawn tennis, has gone on the
land. , .
From the West comes news of a new son for the
J. H. Gorses. Mrs. Gorse is a daughter of T. E. Barr
Smith, and went with her husband to the West early
in the year. ■ , .
The local golf tournaments resulted in a win, so
far as the lady’s championship went, for Mrs.
Fowler. The golfist is better known to fame as Miss
Florence Ayers, an ex-tennis champion, and one of a
family with something of a reputation for games.
She married a naval man.
Our recent dash of mid-summer heat was too much
for some of the oldsters. Among others, Mr. C. t.
Folland, of Enfield, passed over last week, aetat. 97.
He was about our frankest agnostic, and his name
often appeared at the foot of articles and letters
on the subject in the dailies.
On Friday officialdom and the Governor went to
Angaston, and “opened” the railway that has been
threatening the district for 30 years.
The T. R. Bowman estate has been finally locigea
for probate—paying some £16,000 on its £190,000
worth of property. A recent mail-boat bore to Eng-
land the Cecil Brays, she being one of the departed
squatter’s daughters. . ,
The Anglican Synod is in session, and our coffee
palaces and streets are full of melancholy black-
garbed men, with worried-looking wives, whose toilets
suggest speedy dressing and elusive hooks. In tne
intervals of tea-battles the clerics have foregathered
and discussed most things on earth, in Heaven and
elsewhere. They’ve voiced their objection to Easter
military camps, seeing that such revels interfere
with church-going, and cast a blight on the plate ,
also, they propose to take “vigorous measures con-
cerning good old “religious instruction in State
schools.” They beguiled the monotony of one_ sitting
by debating what narticular brand of clothing
parson should wear when exercising hisoffical
duties, and wound up by charging the Bishop with
Extreme Ritualism, at w.uch unlooked-for msilt
the holy man is understood to have leaped in tne
air as though stung by wild hornets.
One Rooney went to his reward on( Friday. He
fought in the Indian Mutiny, as a member ot tne
54th Foot.
Sincerely yours,
JOHNNA.

SLEEPLESS
WITH SCIATICA.
♦
Feverish Nights of Suffering
With a Bad Attack.
♦
This Man tells how he Drove
it from his System with
Dr. Williams' Pink Pills.
An attack of Sciatica causing him many
sleepless, feverish nights was endured by
Mr. Henry Wilkins, of South Fremantle,
W.A., until he drove the complaint from his
system with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Mr. Wilkins is a wonderful old man of
over 80 years, who now enjoys splendid
health, works about the garden, and has
good sight, hearing and memory. He lives
with his married daughter, Mrs. M. Ma-
lacaki, on the Hamilton Road.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have been found
to be of great benefit in treating Sciatica,
because it is a nervous disorder, and they
have proved of great value in toning the
nerves. When interviewed by a reporter,
Mr. Wilkins said :
“I had a very nasty attack of Sciatica a
few years back. In out-door work I would
very often get wet to the waist, going
through rushes and blackberry bushes, and I
suppose this was the original cause. It took
me slowly at first, and then increased
quite rapidly. I did not lie up with
it, I never would when ill unless
absolutely necessary, and I have gen-
erally enjoyed splendid robust health.
The pain was very great. I could hardly
walk, in fact I could rarely go out, and I
would have to press my hands to my hip;
that was the chief part affected. I dreaded
the night time, for as soon as I got settled
warm in bed the pain would start worse
than ever and bring on retching. I would
just have to lie and bear it. I could not
close my eyes until the attack eased off, and
then I might drop off into a doze. The sweat
just poured out of me. I was so feverish, I
was always longing for -water to drink.
Sharp pains like a knife-thrust would go
through the hip. I became a good bit pulled
down ; I could not eat or relish my food. At
last I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and
gradually the Sciatica was driven out of my
system, and I am thankful to say has never
shown the least sign of coming back.”
The price of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is
2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d., and if
you have trouble in getting them send
a postal note for the amount to the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd..
Sydney, and they will be sent post free by
return mail.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Plain Bill Lyne, addressing the London
suffragettes, referred to the prophecy that
when Australian women got the vote they
would exercise it in favor of good-looking
candidates. Bill alleged that this prediction
had been falsified, as the women had helped
to send some particularly ugly men into
the Australian Parliament. Whereupon
London Punch, in a fairly bright moment:
“This not does prove anything. The unsuc-
cessful candidates may have been uglier
still.”
The Vie. Government, with the fear of
another general election before its eyes,
promises that the railways shall be worked
to a better political purpose than the earn-
ing of large departmental dividends. The
trucks that cheese-paring Tait wouldn't pro-
vide are to come along—l7oo before the end
of this year, and another 1000 by the middle
of next. Business profits are to be further
reduced in the interest of the farmer and
grazier, whose prosperous selves and abun-
dant produce will be carried at lower rates.
Firewood freights are to be cheapened, to
the advantage, possibly, of town consumers.
But the laying of new railway lines, to give
the western settler a short, straight, cheap
run to Portland, and the opening to trade
of that splendid, long-neglected harbor, are
not even dimly projected. The Weary Gov-
ernment is only looking ahead as far as the
imminent election.
The little forlorn-hope sheet run by the
single-taxers in Sydney remarks, in its last
issue, that “the recent utterances of Mr.
Wade on the land question show he is unfit
to hold any responsible position in the public
life of the country.”! Of course it is quite
true, only the Standard might just as well
have said that the Calamitous Person’s re-
marks on any question prove the same thing.
But the blinding sorrow of the business is
that the single-taxers habitually join with
the old Freetrade gang in voting for the great
“Liberal” party which the utterly unfit Wade
commands. In fact, dreary Lonsdale and
others actually fought with that party in the
very campaign in which Wade was guilty of
the “recent utterances.”
EAT AND GET THIN.
This is turning an old phrase face about,
but . modern methods of reducing fat have
made this revision possible.
If you are overfat, and also averse to phy-
sical exertion and likewise fond of the table,
and still want to reduce your excess flesh
several pounds, do this : Go to your chemist
and give him 3s. 6d. For this modest
amount of money the chemist will put you
in the way of satisfying your ambition for a
nice trim slim figure. He will hand you a
large case of Marmola Prescription Tablets
(compounded in accordance with the famous
Marmola Prescription), one of w r hich you
must take after each meal and at bedtime
until you begin to lose your fat at the rate
of 12 to 16 ounces a day. Tnat is all. Just
go on eating what you like; leave exercising
to the athletes, but take your little tablet
faithfully, and without a doubt that flabby
flesh will quickly take unto itself wings,
leaving behind it your natural self, neatly
clothed in firm flesh arid trim muscles. Mar-
mola Prescription Tablets are sold by all
chemists, price 3s. 6d., or sent post free on
receipt of price by The Marmola Company,
7 Barrack Street, Sydney, N.S.W.
NOTE. —For those who prefer the liquid
preparation, chemists are still dispensing
the famous prescription as follow.s: One
ounce of fluid Extract of Glycyrrhisa B.P..
one ounce of pure Glycerine B.P.. one half-
ounce of Marmola , and mix with pepper-
mint water to make six ounces in all. The
dose is two tcaspoonfuls after each meal.*
“M.L.C.’s” appreciation of Willis: —
'the further the present session of the N. S. Wales
Assembly proceeds, the more it is borne in on mem-
bers that Henry Willis is the best Speaker his State
has known since Edmund Barton. The new man’s
decisive attitude has opened people’s eyes to the fact
that McCourt, Cann, and the rest were weak creatures
after all. The chief reason why N. S. Wales has
borne the unsavorv reputation of owning the most
lawless, undisciplined, and generally disreputable
Parliament in Australia was because it has been
afflicted with absurdly-affable Speakers. Willis may
be a “political adventurer,” and all the rest of it,
but, certainly, he has courage, and he knows the
game he is playing from A to Z. His manner and
methods are closely modelled on those of the late
Frederick Holder, the most capable Speaker the
Australian Parliament ever knew. Before Willis’s
advent, the “push' legislators made trouble as a
matter of course, when things didn’t go their way.
Under this bad old system, the Speaker ruled, often
in a state of semi-chaos, with members standing or
half-standing, interjecting, cursing, muttering or
howling as the spirit moved them. With the phleg-
matic calm of the stout man, to whom nerves are
unknown, Willis waves dissentients into a humble
sitting posture and absolute silence, before giving
his ruling. And when the same is given, members
have to abide by it or go out.

“F.C.” writes:—
The democrats made another sensible innovation
at the opening of the Australian Parliament. Last
session Speaker and Senate President discarded the
horsehair kickshaws their predecessors used to wear,
and last week the Clerk and his assistant wore only
their own thatches, the blessed mace disappeared,
and Black Rod disremembered his absurd sword and
clothed himself like an ordinary sane person. Next
session the equally futile Address-in-Reply will pro-
bably forget to happen likewise. The State Par-
liaments still keep up the fripperies of the days
when men wore tin-plates on their chests and put
rat-traps on their countenances ; yet, considering the
associations of the Victorian mace, that brummy
affair should have been the first to be scrapped.

Every day we see more plainly how Capi-
tal is being chivvied out of this country. The
latest Awful Example can be read in the
advts. of tlie Nestle Condensed-milk people,
wherein it is stated that at the recent Show
in Brisbane they entered 28 head of cattle
from their dairy herds, and scooped 34
awards. These cattle were from their farms
at Toogoolawah (Q.), where the co. runs
something like 1000 well-bred milk-producers
and a fair number of human families. The
same co. has another big factory at Benning-
ton (Vic.), which cheerfully gulps down ail
the milk the local farmers can pump from
Spot and Polly, and keeps bellowing for
more. Not so very long ago, it used to im-
port all its tinned milk, and the herds and
herders and factory hands browsed on the
hills of Switzerland and other countries. Of
course the transference of the business to Aus-
tralia is a Dreadful Thing for the country,
and a Shuddersome Sample of the evils of
Protection. Anyhow, no doubt that’s how
the Switzers regard it.
Governor Fuller has been wasting admira-
tion on “the agricultural patriotism of
wealthy gentlemen—whose names will read-
ily occur—who have spent money importing
the best stock from the Old Country.” Fuller
might almost as reasonably have extolled
the civic patriotism of the draper who im-
ports expensive “Paris models” for improv-
ing the breed of women’s cloaks and gowns.
Cattle-raising is a competitive business, like
fashionable clothes-making. The finest stock
fetches the best price in both cases. And a
general difference between the rent-paying
draper and the land-owning cattle-king is
that the one advertises the new season’s
goods, and the other cries out against the
new season’s ills in a Stinking Fish spirit.
The contractors for the
third section of the North
Coast (N.S.W.) railway
have surrendered their con-
tract to the Government on
mutually satisfactory terms.
One reason why the Gov-
ernment was anxious to
acquire the right to fin-
ish the job by day labor
was a clause in the con-
tract which entitled the con-
tractors to claim so much
per cubic yard for remov-
ing any part of the globe
which might fall into a cut-
ting after it had been passed
by the inspectors. At one
point of the line a huge hill-
side cutting had just been
completed, when heavy rains
dissolved the rest of the hill
and spread it over the vicin-
ity, and as the earth to be
removed was required for
filling-in purposes a short
distance further on, the con-
tractors were on a good wicket. However,
they had their own reason's for giving un the
business ; and now the Minister for Works
is gaffer of the gang.
The petrol chariot of Fat. which grunts like
a mad pig, throws up the dirt, and leaves m
offensive odor behind, is highly inflammable,
and it is likely to burn frequently in
future contests between Labor and its
owner. The Automobile Association of Eng-
land, the other day, offered the British Gov-
ernment the use of 50,000 cars and the ser-
vices of 35,000 members for the next indus-
trial argument. So the Fat will throw oil
on the troubled waters, and the Lean will
drop a light on top of the oil, and the
troubled waters will blaze like the very devil.
Adelaide Municipal Tram Trust is making
a big dent in the feelings of the people by
getting its car castings from America and
its rails from Germany. But for an ener-
getic protest it would have obtained the car
bodies also from the Canned Beef Country,
but they are being made in Adelaide.
ST. GREGORY, V.C.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Intrigue has been going on for months past to
such an extent that the public at large must be dis-
gusted.—Charles Gregory Wade.
I’ve read of many Noble Blokes beyond corruption’s
reach,
And Aristides was a theme my tutors used to teach ;
But even Aristides in his glory wasn’t made
Of such peculiar marble as the righteous Mr. Wade.
He finds that all of Belial’s sons against him are in
league;
He’s flabbergasted by their wiles, and shocked by
their intrigue ;
lie looks in pious horror on the things they dare to
do,
And whoever differs from him is a Judas through
and through.
I scarcely like to meddle with these sacred subjects,
yet
When Wade's disciples start to yell, one thing I
can’t forget,
That ordinary reverence in this is sacrificed,
For if Willis is a Judas, then Wade must be a Christ.
With all respect to Mr. Wade, I’ll boldly bet my hat
That, though he is a plaster saint, he’s not so high as
that;
And when he puts his halo on, I’d like him to devote
One moment to recalling how he snared the Syrian
vote.
There was a Compact (was there not?): while Jimmy
was away
The Saint would make no trouble ; and just look at
lrim to-day !
And there are other mysteries which make the sinner
doubt
The Wade who played the Hero till the papers bowled
him out.
He is a rather speckled Saint, this Wade whom we
behold,
And patches linger on him yet, from scandals not so
old; '
It makes a very spotty and a very piebald sight
When men with dirty fingers don a robe of dazzling
white.
VICTORIA.
T.
THE STRIKER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A strike of doctors for higher pay is at present
seriously embarrassing the Defence department. They
decline to examine the junior cadets unless they re-
ceive 2s. 6d. a head, and as this is regarded by the
department as beyond its means, the enrolment and
inspection of lads of 12 and 13 years of age is at
a deadlock.- —Melbourne Age.
Lo ! how the grasping Unionist
Demands what pay he likes,
And, failing to secure the same,
Incontinently strikes.
Time-honored economic laws,
Revered in ev’ry land,
Demand. .. .Supply. .. .their interplay,
He cannot understand.
The warnings of Bruce Smith, M.T.,
On his dull brain are lost;
He clamors for a higher wage
Regardless of the cost.
He does not grasp that by such acts
The value of his pound
Is shrunk by 25 per cent. ;
The Socialistic hound !
The people who provide his fees
Supply him, too, with food
And liquor and habiliments
And coal and boots and wood.
The more he charges then for health,
The more they sock it in
To him and his for meat and drink—
The thing’s as clear as Sin.
And yet—insensate Anarch'st
And dullard that he is!
He will not take his sweated pay
In thankfulness and bliss.
The Philosophic Liberal
Stands stupent at such deeds:
The Thoughtful Citizen is shocked,
His heart with anguish bleeds.
But Doctor Brown, M. 8., M.D.,
Heeds not their grief and race,
As cheek-by-jowl with Navvy Smith
He seeks a living wage.
N.S.W.

SOME STRIKING SYMPTOMS.
A child who is afflicted with worms will
often be in an indifferent state of health.
It becomes peevish and irritable, and will
have a strong tendency to pick the nose, and
there will be severe itching of
gh
the lower bowel. Grinding the
teeth and suddenly starting up
in the sleep will also be
noticed. The bowels are irre-
gular, sometimes constipated,
and at other times relaxed,
with bloody discharges as in
dysentery, and not infrequently
the discharges will be in flakes
and shreds, which might be
taken for partly-digested food.
The best proof of Worms is
Worms, and if their presence
is suspected, immediate steps
should be taken to positively establish the
fact, and once established, Comstock’s “Dead

according to directions. Worms require a

Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
