SPORTING
NOTIONS
The decease of J. J. Miller’s Racing
Chronicle leaves the Argus publication—the Rac-
ing Calendar —in sole possession of the Victorian
field, as of yore. J. J. Miller’s Chronicle was a
plucky effort against an old monopoly. The Calen-
dar was a dilatory and incomplete annual record of
gee-gee events, published at 30s. J.J.M. brought
out his handy little Chronicle in half-yearly vol-
umes, at ss. a time. A large circulation would have
kept it going even at 55., but seemingly the principal
purchasers of books of Turf reference are not in a
hurry. The annual book, considerably belated in the
publication, was good enough for them. Besides, the
Argus shook up the Calendar— brought it out earlier
in the season, and reduced the price to 20s. On the
other hand, the Chronicle’s price rose to 7s. 6d.,
and afterwards to 10s, but even at the advanced figure
its two issues a year should have given it the pull
over the rival publication. All the same, the loss
continued, so its 31st appearance was its last. J. J.
Miller’s cheerful notice of the Chronicle’s decease
reminds one that his Sporting Pamphlet (which
summarises the performances of all the entrants for
the big Spring handicaps) is still going strong. The
veteran’s photo, is published on the forefront of the
latest Pamphlet, with the announcement that this
is its 35th year, and his 80th. J. J. Miller, if
not the only survivor of the Early Fathers of the
Australian betting ring, is certainly the only one
who retains a business connection with horse-racing.
The Pamphlet links him with the time of the first
Melbourne Cup (1861), when he was a spry young
bookmaker, and an all-round sport. In after years
his Sweeps made his name a household word, but a
crowd of cronk imitators rushed into the “consulta-
tion” game, and then the Law got on to it, and Miller’s
sweeps were swept away with the rest. After which
Adams arose, with a new method of gaining public
confidence, and Tattersall’s became a national institu-
tion.
Dead in Adelaide, Charlie McEnhill, for-
merly one of S.A.’s best jockeys. He won the Grand
National Hurdles on Ebb, a-nd before that had a
lot of winning mounts as a light-weight for Quinn’s
stable.
Tattersall’s race-meeting at Randwick
(Sydney), on Saturday, enticed over 20,000 to battle
with Dermot Mac Moses in the foolish desire to wax
fat —-and how miserably they failed. Of the six
events, only two fell to favorites, Moses heroically
offering 6 to 4 on one of them. Three of the others
started at anything between 12 and 20 to 1. The
chief event (Chelmsford Stakes, weight-for-age) was
won by the Maorilander Los Angelos, with Flavinius
and Bright Laddie in the other places, and the pick
of other N.S.W. prads creeping furiously behind. As
Los Angelos started at 12 to 1, the handful among
the multitude who defeated Shamrock Moses re-
ceived a fair return. The “double” of the day—
Tramway and Spring Handicaps—were gathered in
by outsiders Grist and Flava, with the heavily-backed
gee-gees trailing behind, accompanied by the smile
of the Man with the Bag.
The meeting was a pipe-opener for the
A.J.C. (Derby) meeting, which begins at Randwick
at the end of the month, and nearly all the big guns
were saddled up. The downfall of candidates, which
held favorite places for the Derby, Epsom and
Metrop., gave punters the usual opportunity to keep
their heads in bandages; but, as many of the candi-
dates were obviously only “out for a run,” the
downfall and bobbing-up of the gee-gees may perhaps
be taken with a bagful of salt.
* * * *
Blairgour—which the bookmakers have
kept at the head of the Epsom betting market since
the weights were loosened —finished second last in a
field of 25 in the Tramway Handicap at Randwick,
on Saturday, and was thereupon pushed to the other
end of the quotations. The prad was knocked about
greatly during the race, and, if it was a scheme to
settle his chances in future events, the attempt was
very nearly successful.
Prince Foote (alleged to have been backed
in the past two weeks for the Melbourne Cup) ran
like a commoner in the Chelmsford Stakes on Satur-
day, and finished near the tail-end. Which isn’t very
reassuring to punters. However, there wasn’t very
much reason why he should have performed any
better (it being his first run for a long while) ; and
he has plenty of time to improve before the Cup
meeting happens along.
* * * *
The horse contingent from Maoriland went
down badly at Randwick on Saturday. Los Angelos’s
success in the big event probably lightened the
horse-feed account of one stable; but other runners
froni the Ricketty Isles didn’t accomplish much.
Vice-Admiral was pounced upon as a “good thing”
for one of the events, and other prads were be-
friended, so the meeting wasn’t very prolific from a
M.L. point of view. However, there is plenty of
boodle left, and plenty of time to reap it; and the
M.L. clans will probably have a fist into it before it
is all over.
» * * *■
Australia’s Bill Lang, recently alleged by
the Mclnstoush to be the hope of the white race for
the overthrow of the sons of Ham (and chicken),
who have captured the front seats in pugdom, was
made a holy show of last Saturday night at the
Sydney Stadium. A party named Jack Lester, who
dropped in from some old where in Canada, went
20 rounds with him, and got a points’ decision.
This is the same party who was disqualified a few
months ago, in a match against Lang, for going down
before receiving a blow. Although his action at
that time was apparently the result of a misappre-
hension, it did Tommy Burns’s protSgS no good, so
that there was little business doing at 3 to 1 or 4
to 1 in favor of Lang on Saturday night, when the
two faced each other for the second encounter. The
battle was the hardest yet seen in the Stadium, and
although Lang was in as good fighting trim as ever
he was, the 20-year-old Canadian took everything
that Bill could send his way without showing any signs
of wear and tear to speak of, piled up points in the
last few rounds, and wound up as fresh as Bill was
otherwise. All of which is bad enough, but when
the said Bill reflects that his conqueror is raw and
utterly inexperienced, what consolation shall he
find? There never looked a more down-hearted man
as he sat with bowed head after “Snowy” Baker had
given the decision, and listened to the uproarious
cheers that heralded his downfall. Never —unless it
was the fistically-deceased Bill Squires who was in
Lang’s corner.
Except for its strenuousness the battle had little
to commend it. Lester displayed about 3in. of
science to the square yard of undefined biff. Lang,
with long experience, could do nothing to stop his
whirling opponent, and if it had been a “finish”
fight, the Australian would probably have been
knocked out, despite the fact that he was in good
trim. Billiam advances the excuse that his hand
went back on him again, and that he was not pro-
perly well. No good! Billiam is a second-rater
that’s all.
The unknown Lester, by defeating Lang, gets a
pass into the upper circles of the game, and as
things stand he should represent the white man
against McVea, Langford, or even Johnson at the
Stadium this season. He is an unassuming lad, and
although his chance of walloping the big coons
seems hardly worth hoping for, Australians will hope
on just the same. He has the qualities from which
champions are built, and can take punishment like
a wooden man, but he is woefully inexperienced, and
he hasn’t time to make up the deficiency before the
murky brigade will be hurling upper-cuts and other
discourtesies at him. Still he is lucky to get the
opportunity that the Mclnstoush will probably give
him within the next few months, and even if he
gets all the kicks he’ll certainly get a fair cut of
the ha’pence. And, anyhow, it is better to have
bashed and failed than had no chance to bash at all.
* * * *
Lester’s next fight will be on September
30, against Sam McVea, the colored man, who is
expected to wear Johnson’s ring-shoes when that gold-
mounted hunk of ebony steps out, or is biffed out, of
them. #
Old-time stousher Monte Andrews met
Joe Wallis in a 20-round argument at the Gaiety
Athletic Club (Sydney) last week, and although hog-
fat and minus that agility which is, alas, the mono-
poly of youth, got the decision over Joseph on points.
The fight went the full 20 rounds.
* * * *
The defeat of Hackenschmidt by Gotch
might be taken as the text for a sermon to young
athletes. Failing health, rather than advancing
years, explains why the really phenomenal Hacky
could be downed twice within 12 minutes or so by
a man 171 b. lighter than himself. The Russian is
still on the right side of 33, hardly so old as to be
past his wrastling prime—yet he was unable to make
a decent show against the man whom he kept busy
for two hours in their questionable bout three years
ago. As a matter of fact, Hackenschmidt has never
“taken care of himself” since the time when, as a
verv young man, he beat the Terrible Turk in London.
When he first came to Australia, in 1905, he told
his friends confidentially he had lost some of his
strength, and this was the 26-year-old “lion” _ who
could do as he liked with wonderful Buttan Singh,
after he had casually disposed of Buttan’s colored
mate on the same mat. The original Hacky was
probably the strongest, quickest, and most versatile
wrastler of all time. Now he is a back-number,
damaged in constitution.
By a strange coincidence the cable news of Hacky’s
easy defeat appeared on the same day in Australia as
the report or Clarence Weber’s match with Alex-
ander Bain. Hacky was something of a mentor to
Weber five years ago, and he always declared that the
statuesque young strong man lacked only craft and
“devil” to "make him a world’s champion on the
mat. Weber acquired the necessary vim (and he
needed all of it) to win the Australian champion-
ship from Buttan Singh, who was just half a century
old at the time. Meanwhile, he has been gaining
experience in his own wrestling school. Bain, the
Scotchman (15st. 81b.), seemed a mere novice in his
hands. Within three minutes of actual wrastling the
challenger had lost two falls, and the crowd in Mel-
bourne, like the 30,000 crowd in Chicago, noted the
fact that a genuine championship “go” is usually a
much more rapid affair than the ordinary match,
which is intended to pile up the agony for another
one. # #
Footballers and barrackers in the eastern
half of Sydney held high revelry last week-end,
owing to successes in the various competitions. In
the Rugby League premiership, Eastern Suburbs ac-
counted for both grades; while East Sydney demol-
ished Y.M.C.A. under the Australian Rules code.
The rest of the city had to be content with a halo
of bandages.
* * * *
Eastern Suburbs and Glebe provided a
strenuous struggle for the N.S.W. Rugby League
premiership, on Saturday. The event was patronised
by about 20,000 barrackers. Throughout the struggle
Eastern Suburbs held the upper hand, and from the
moment Messenger booted a goal 2min. after the
kick-off, Glebe never once raised the hope that it
possessed a winning chance. The first half ended
with East leading (14 points to 5) ; and in the
second section, Glebe dropped further to rearward,
until, at whistle-time, the scores were: Eastern
Suburbs 22, Glebe 9. These figures just about repre-
sented the difference between the teams on the day’s
play. Easts scored four tries to one by Glebe;
Messenger booted five goals (10 points), towards
East’s tally, and Algie shot two goals for Glebe.
The final will be played off between these teams next
Saturday, after which the blind will be finally
pulled down for the season.
The second-grade final provided a spark-
ling curtain-raiser, in which Eastern Suburbs
trampled over Glebe by 12 points to nil, and for the
fourth successive season tacked the second-grade
honors to their jersey.
Two bands of toughs, masquerading under
a sanctimonious title in the N.S.W. Church Rugby
Union, supplied another football riot in Sydney, on
Saturday. When the referee stopped the game, or
pitched battle,- the two, teams and their barrackers
were laying down the law under the Marquis of
Cain’s rules, and, as this is the second occasion on
which a somewhat similar disturbance has taken
place between these two pushes, it is up to the
Synod or Presbytery or Rugby Union, or other re-
sponsible religious body, to make some vigorous re-
marks. * * *
Australian Rules football this season has
been full of surprises, and the premiership final on
Saturday provided another boil-over. Y.M.C.A. and
East Sydney clashed at Erskineville Oval. Except
in the first quarter, when Y.M.C.A. led by 21 points
to 14, the game belonged to East Sydney, and, when
knock-off time arrived, the score was—East Sydney
61, Y.M.C.A. 28, the latter team registering only
seven points in the final three-quarters of the game.
Sydney (minor premiers) will play a challenge match
with East Sydney on Saturday, after which the in-
flation will be allowed to escape from the pig-hide
until next year. .
The semi-final for the Gardiner Cup (Syd-
ney Soccer) was a close thing. Balmain A and
Balmain B met to decide who was to meet Powerful
for the final, and A team got the verdict by one goal
to nil. On Sydney Cricket Ground No. 2, Powerful
will next Saturday argue with Balmain A as to
whether the King’s Navee shall keep the Cup an-
other year, or hand it over.
Something like a walloping was recorded
when Cananore (Southern champions) and North
Launceston (cock-o’-the-North) clashed for the Tas-
manian football premiership (Australian rules) at
Hobart, on Saturday. The Southerners trampled on
the other section of the Flyspeek by 114 points to 10.
Some 43,575 folk crowded into the Metro-
politan Cricket Ground at Yarra bank (Melb.l on
Saturday. The menu for the day comprised an
entree in the shape of a Metropolitan Ass. semi-final
between Beverley and Collegians—won by the latter
and, as joint, the first League semi-final between
South and ’Wood. This was remindful of paper-bag
cookery, inasmuch as all went well till the dish was
half cooked, then the bag broke or something, and
everything went to pieces. Up to half-time ’Wood
was 3 goals 7 behinds to South’s 2 goals 7 behinds.
On resuming, ’Wood staggered everybody, and
smashed South’s hopes by taking entire charge of
the proceedings, and piling up five consecutive goals
while Skinner’s Hirelings could compass but one.
South’s last-quarter task was impossible, but its
barrackers couldn’t see it. The red-and-whites, by
vigorous rushes, forced the ball down thrice, each
time getting a goal; then they got a behind —and
two goals would give them victory. The barrackers
were delirious. But then the Magpies took full
charge, piled up three additional goals in almost as
many minutes, and ran into the final with a tally
of 11 goals 11 behinds to South’s 6 goals 11 behinds.
The “gate” was £1624 6s. 6d., which compares
somewhat favorably with the Association’s first
semi-final “gate” of £250.
That body was simultaneously holding its second
semi-final, between Essendon and P’ran, on the North
Melbourne ground, before a moderate assemblage.
Up to the midway interval the ’Dons had all the best
of the deal —with 48 points to 25. Then P’ran put
its best foot forward, and got within two points, but
the effort was too great, and in the last term the
red-and-blacks managed to get five points further
out of danger, the ultimate tally being: ’Don, 8
goals 9 behinds to P’ran’s 7 goals 8 behinds. But
these ’Don’s are an unreliable lot, and writer’s
opinion is that, if their two chief goal-getters were
better watched, they would be easily beaten. They
now meet Brunswick in the final, but have two
chances to ’Wick’s one.
Sydney athletes are promised a big blow-
out on September 23, under the wing of the N.S.W.
Rugby League. On the professional side there will
be a 130yds. world’s championship struggle between
Jack Donaldson and C. E. Holway, and also a £2O
handicap; while for the amateurs there will be a
£lO handicap (open order) —the tallest amount
allowed under the laws of lily-white amateurism.
The ladies will also be attended to with the 75yds.
championship.
* * * *
The annual Beverley-Perth (W.A.) road
race this year produced a record for the journey. A.
Kaiser (17min.), who finished seventh, did the 116
miles in shr. 58min. 32 l-ssec., whereas the previous
fastest was 6hr. 30sec., done in 1909. The winner,
E. M. Gray (Perth), with 42min. handicap, covered
the distance in 6hr. 2min., with H. Ridley (Kal-
goorlie) second.
* * * *
“All About Cycling.” published by the
Dunlop Co., is as good as its title, and a penny
stamp sent to Dunlop’s will bring anybody a copy.
• * * *
The programme compiled by the Board of
Control in connection with the visit of the English
XI comprises 20 matches. The customary five
“tests” will be played to a finish; four days are
allotted to return matches with N.S.W., Victoria,
and South Australia; and three days to a single
match with Queensland. An “XI of Australia” will
confront the English XI at Brisbane; matches
against Hobart and Launceston will absorb the visit
to Tasmania ; while the remaining fixtures are two-
day picnics against Toowomba (Q.), Newcastle and
Hunter (N.S.W.), Bendigo, Ballarat, and Geelong
(Vic.). So, if the Englishmen have anything to
growl about (which, of course, they never do), it
won’t be that they are overworked.
* * * *
South Australia, Victoria and N.S.W. will
battle for the Sheffield Shield between the English
fixtures; Queensland will get a return State match
or two, though, thusfar, its inclusion in Sheffield
Shield cricket isn’t countenanced; and a Tassy team
may raid the mainland at any time. As for West-
ralia, it is pushed off the cricket map, although an
effort is being made to get the incoming English
XI to drop the kit and play a match at Perth on
the backward journey.
* * * * *
Rockhampton (Q.), which felt sore over
the refusal of the S’Afriean googlies to go up that
far, is now scheming to get a visit from the coming
Bullstein eleven Mount Morgan, alongside, has never
done much at cricket, but when it thinks of its
football it can only wear a very large size in hats.
Sydney baseball premiership fell softly into
the arms of Leichhardt, owing to Paddington being
too weary to play the postponed match. Still, Leich-
hardt’s win wasn’t out of turn, as the team, which
has lent a hand in the premiership turmoil since its
inauguration in 1900, has only gathered in the
bauble once previously (1902).
* * * *
Petersham gathered in the Sydney lacrosse
premiei'ship on Saturday, defeating Sydney by eight
goals to five, after a splendid exhibition. At half-
time the scores were four all; but in the second half
Sydney threw away scoring opportunities, and was
sent home the victim of its own bad judgment. This
is the third season in succession that Petersham has
hung the premiership in its humpy.
The recent ladies’ championship footrace
at Sydney has brought out some more fast women,
and challenges are as thick as hatpins in Johnson’s
tram-cars. Miss Moore, who holds the amateur title,
will make an effort to go the pace faster than any
other amateuress in the championship event on Satur-
day week; and, if that is accomplished, will con-
front Miss Ivy Evans (of Victoria) for the Austra-
lian professional championship. There is quite a
host of other ladies anxious to leg it for gold and
glory, and some of them are likely to be accommo-
dated in the near future.
* * * *
Burgess’s swim across the Channel differed
considerably in its conditions from Matthew Webb’s
famous feat. Webb, who used the breast stroke, ana
pegged away at much the same pace all the time,
was reckoned to have covered 35 miles in the 21}
hours he stayed in the water. The sea was four
degrees colder than Burgess found it, and tabloid
forms of nourishment were not available in 1876.
Webb followed pretty closely the course he had
planned, and prepared himself for the job in the
way his seafaring experience suggested. He had
been the captain of a small merchant sailing vessel.
He made only two attempts. Rough weather stopped
him early in the first try ; the second did the trick.
Burgess made 13 unsuccessful attempts. Appar-
ently he started his fourteenth at a fair racing pace,
with the idea of making a comparatively short jour-
ney across, but was carried far out of his course.
How many miles he was estimated to cover the cable
doesn’t say. Less than 30, perhaps. Anyhow, he
was 23} hours in the water, two hours longer than
Webb’s time. Considering that he had a remark-
ably smooth sea all the way, with a high tempera-
ture, and took two hours longer than the record to
cover a shorter distance than Webb swam with the
old breast stroke, it would seem that Burgess’s stay-
ing power was more passive than active. As a
Channel-swimming hero he doesn’t figure so pictur-
esquely as Matthew Webb, the huge-chested, blue-
eyed blonde, with classical features, and an aspect
that would have been Greek god-like, but for his
trim, flax-colored mo. Burgess, judging from photos,
is a swarthy fellow, with a long, bridgeless nose,
and a thick, unlovely growth of black beard, side-
whiskers and mo. His eye suggests the pertinacity of
the fanatic, and, now that he has bracketed his name
with Webb’s, he may be persuaded to try a swim
across Niagara Falls, where the gallant Captain of
old met his death. At the time it was said that
Webb attempted the impossible, but later student)
of the silly question have argued that he was tin
lucky in striking a snag.
Fogwell will have to come back to Au«-
tralia without the great English prize. On Monday
he rowed Barry for the Fog championship, the
Sportsman Cup, and £2OO a side. The race was
over the Thames championship course (4} miles),
and Barry paddled home four lengths to the good,
after being at one time eight lengths ahead. The
time was 22min. 4sec.
“Crack” billiardists are as much disheart-
ened by a hopeless prospect as the crudest amateurs,
seemingly. They make an up-hill struggle for just
as long as a reasonable offchance of victory remains,
but, when the figures have become appalling, they
take the “knock.” In Melbourne, a few weeks back,
even the veteran Stevenson dropped away beaten when
Lindrum, at a critical point in the' race, started to
increase his handicap lead. And then the young
Australian, with success practically assured, cast
anxiety to the winds, and played masterly, all-round
billiards. In Sydney, the position was reversed.
Stevenson overhauled the receiver of points from
the outset, and, as he drew nearer, he had Lindrum
dead beaten. The “boy” couldn’t play for nuts or
sour apples. In Melbourne, last week, Williams
had nearly succeeded in distancing Lindrum in their
Australian championship race, when the latter made
up his lost ground. The Englishman faltered, and
presently Lindrum had him beaten. It was a case
of Confidence winning easily from Despair.
George Gray, the Australian billiard won-
der, conceded W. Smith, jun., 2250 in a match of
5000, and when Smith had run his points to 4126
and Gray had scored only 1808, the Australian
stepped down rather than be beaten outright. That
he wasn’t off-color seems to be proved by the
fact that the same evening he gave Bedford, the
Cleveland champion, 250 in 750, and ran out while
his opponent added 289 to his handicap. Gray has
played about 40 matches in England, and gets his
first humiliation from a person named Smith. He
has made 23 separate breaks of over 1000. There
will be no new billiards champion of Australia yet.
Williams thought there should be, and he tried to
persuade Lindrum ; but Lindrum won the argument.
The final scores were: Lindrum, 8000; Williams,
6205. Lindrum’s average for the match was 35.8,
and Williams’s 27.8.
GOING OUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Johnson lias informed Mr. Mclntosh that after he
has met McVea, Langford and Lang he intends to
retire from the ring.— Argus.
When he has had a few fights more,
Jack Johnson’s scrappings will be o’er.
He’ll go out with the championship,
None leaving on the earth to whip.
With oof to burn, he’ll take his fling,
And shape no more within the ring.
We’ve heard this little tale before
Of champion fighters half a score.
Oft from the ring in straits most dire
The mighty Johnson will retire.
Two men ’twill need to force retreat,
One at his head, one at his feet.
Till finally his backers stout
Don’t care again to tow him out.

Une day or another the Federal Labor
party will have to resolve that Page, of
Queensland, is a Full Blown Calamity. Thus
spake last week the Huge Disaster from
Maranoa :
The right to strike is the only weapon the work-
ing man has. We are not going to give it up.
So, according to Page, arbitration courts and
Wages Boards are all a sham, and the exist-
ence of a Labor Government and a Labor
majority in Parliament makes no difference,
It would be well if this hulking and ill-
judged member could be sentenced to pre-
serve a dead silence for the next six months,
anyhow.
The Weary Parliament is now being in-
vited to pass a Bill to compel boot manufac-
t who make their boots of brown paper
* f,
instead of leather, to stamp them with the
unpleasant truth. Heaven only knows why
there should be any need for such a measure.
The man who went into a bank to change a
“tenner,” and was handed in return bogus
notes or brummy sovereigns, wouldn’t bother
about a special Bill or having the notes
stamped as bogus; he would merely call a
policeman, and the gaol door would open
quite naturally. And there is no sane reason
why it shouldn’t in the other case.

EMAIL this represents the last of the available
highest-class virgin residential lands on the heights
just above Rose Bay, on the South Head Road. It
is the crowning position of the Vaucluse (William
VrinUo Wentworth) Estate, containing about 12
ACRES and was purchased by Mr. W. B. Dailey in
the 1 0 s, when that distinguished scholar, states-
man and patriot had the fullest scope for the exer-
o' B .*;, °f a classic choice of sites for his own home.
With eye of fine discernment, he realised it was the
key position to the Harbor, for it commanded the
Harbor from the Heads to Manly Cove, and right up
to the reaches of the Parramatta and Lane Cove
Rivers, with the inlets on both sides and the islets,
like gems on a plate of silver. From this noble
elevation, his eyes lighted on the ocean and the Blue
Mountains, North Sydney, and the turreted homes
above Rose, Double and Elizabeth Bays. He
realised it was a site that could never be excelled,
that could never be shut out from lovely, maj'es-
tic and incomparable vistas. On it he built “Clair-
veaux, with its quaint tower. There, in richest
soil, he planted cuttings and seeds of the choicest
trees, shrubs and flowers of the world, and they
flourished exceedingly. There Mr. Dailey entertained
the distinguished in art, politics and the professions
and visiting celebrities, all of whom tributed to the
glory of the site of his home. Subsequently, “Clair-
veaux” was the home of Mr. Alfred Bennett (of the
Evening News and the Town and Country Jour-
nal), and of Lady Martin (widow of the late Chief
Justice Martin). Recently the estate was purchased
tor sub-division and sale. The Dailey Mansion Es-
tate is in direct touch with the electric tramway ser-
vice right into the heart of the city. The tram route
is one of the most pleasant in the world, for there
is no more beautiful and romantic road than that
known as the South Head. It is lined by mansion
and picturesque homes, many of which are embedded
m noble gardens or set off by charming lawns.
There is an alternative ferry service along the in-
dented harbor. Country residents who can afford it
should not be content with rooms at hotels, or be
satisfied with houses rented for the season. These
never give full comfort or satisfaction, whatever th°
cost. They never afford the freedom and joy O'
one’s own home. Families realise this, and the staj
in the metropolis is a qualified pleasure and change
of scene. There is economy, wisdom and satisfaction
in having “a town house” of one’s own. It is always
ready for reception. The opportunity to secure a
superb site for a home of beauty and contentment
is now afforded.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.

• And a band was playing as rich men danced,
In marble halls these men of gout.
And more were coming as e’en they pranced,
In costly gowns, a girl came out.
Too spoilt to know,
Too smug to care
How others go,
To table bare.
And stern bailiffs raided a mother’s home
To pav a debt her all they sold.
Lo! her man’s away ’aeath the raging foam
His babes must starve without his gold
Too old to mend
Too sick to work
Her pride must bend
Though rich men smirk.
We can’t help feeling sorry for the gouty rich,

agonised expression of the tortured victims of Sas-
siety. and has nothing whatever to do with the un-
fortunate widow. .. .Arthur O’H.: A cheerful poetic

NIGHT PADS.
Crouched like treacherous tigers

Watching for a victim Lurk

Difficult for to capture

LONDON, JULY, 1911,
Says :
“ A Hamburg trade journalist, who has just returned from the
“Antipodes, states that the pianos manufactured by the Beale Company
“ in Sydney greatly resemble in touch and in tone those turned out at the
“ best German factories. The Beale is sold at forty-five pounds and
“ upwards, and enjoys a good reputation not only in Australia but in India,
“Java, South Sea Islands and other colonial countries with hot climates.
“ The 'Hamburger’ fears that if these British colonials extend their piano
“manufacturing operations there will be little room left for continental
“makers in the musical instrument line.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Mashtun”: Among the Deserted Villages of the
Commonwealth count in Hamelin, on the sou’-west
coast of Westralia. It took its name from Hamelin
Bay, the whilom principal shipping port for the out-
put of Davies’s Karridale Co., one of the big
timber-concession grabbing concerns in Forrestland.
Nowatimes the co. uses Flinders Bay, and Hamelin
Bay and the township are absolutely without human
inhabitants. Some 20 or 30 buildings—including
three fine structures, the Customs House, Police
Station, and M. C. Davies’s own “Government ’Ouse”
quarters—are all going to rack and ruin ; several of
the dwellings have become buried by the sand, and
one cottage is located only by its protruding chim-
ney-top.
* * * *
G. D. Meudell, from London: Fruit is a luxury
here, and is never good, except when bought by the
rich. All other fruit, imported for the poor and
others, is plucked green and sent frozen. All hot-
house-grown fruit is flavorless. Bananas from West
Indies and Canary Islands are putrid abominations,
weeks old. Apples from Canada are green, hard, and
not succulent. The soft fruits —strawberries and
raspberries—reach Covent Garden mashed outside,
and green and sour inside. Cherries, pulled before
they are quite ripe, are seldom seen outside stews.
Pears are unutterable lumber, and one would just
about as soon buy a pound of apricots as a tin of
toxo, unless he really was a rabbit. But the boast
of every islander here is the hot-house stuff—-which
lacks sun-made saccharin —and the unspeakable
Hinglish Happle. Codim moths, aphides, potato
bugs, gnats, hornets, and Colorado beetles make of
the local apple a habitation and an ephemeral
dwelling-place. Apples are so sour and uneatable,
they mostly squeeze cider out of them, and sell it
from chemists’ shops. The very best apple I’ve ever
eaten came last week from Westralia, which bids
fair to be the apple and wheat supplier of Australia
very soon, if not much earlier. The fruit ring lately
“knocked out” Tasmanian apples, and last week,
when they anointed George V., the dealers got two
shiploads at ss. a case, whereas the normal whole-
sale selling price is 10s. ! The knock-out is a flam-
ing scandal, for the price of apples has not been
changed. In the big shops, like Solomon’s, Brooks’,
Gerard’s or May’s, it is the same old threepence
each. In the smaller city shop, where I usually buy
Tasmanian apples at 2|d. each, the price is also un-
changed, although the fruit ring bought them at half
their value. Such treatment of a great Australian
industry causes one to be tremendously in favor of
an Australian Government depot here for all our pro-
ducts. Australia should buy the useless Royal Ex-
change, opposite the Bank of England, and sell penny
apples there and sixpenny rabbits, and defy the
rings.

“P”: Bulletin quotes the Amurrkan Price Col-
lier : “Everybody agrees to question the honesty of
the Japanese as a trader.” Here is a sample from
a Jap merchant’s letter, printed in London West-
minster Gazette : “As Mr. Brown is most religious
and competent man, also heavily upright and
godly, it fears me that useless apply for his signa-
ture- —please therefore attach same by Y —- office mak-
ing forge, but no cause for fear of prison happen-
ings, as this is often operated by other merchants
of highest integrity.” Looks like a good fake, but
is vowed true.

The wheedling ways of the modern “mission”
crowd seem to have been adopted with some effect-
iveness in London by the hustling Canuck advertise-
ment fiend. Here is a tip for the Commonwealth
Government, when next it writes to Reid: —
HEART TO HEART TALK ON CANADA FOR
WOMEN.
Any r girl or woman wishing to have advice on
Canada from a Canadian who knows that coun-
try from end to end should call on Mrs. Simp-
son Hayes at the offices of the Canadian Pacific
Railway, opposite the Nelson Monument, Trafal-
gar Square. Mrs. Simpson Hayes has had many
years of practical experience in enabling Eng-
lishwomen to find new homes and new oppor-
tunities in the West, and can explain what to
do and what not to do. She will gladly wel-
come any callers on Mondays, Wednesdays and
Fridays from 10 a.m. to 5 p.m.
But let there be no appointing of some unhappy
English-woman Johnny, who has never even seen
the blasted er-colony !
* * * *
“Fisho” : There seems to be some room for official
supervision in the matter of the wholesale market-
ing of de oyst. In the Melbourne Fish Market re-
cently I saw some lots being sold at ss. a bag,
though the ruling price the same day was 60s. to
655. a bag. The difference speaks for itself; at
least the five-bob line should be able to utter some
pretty emphatic language.
* * * *
“Petramos” : Good Mr. Blobbs was a country pub-
lican, owning some city houses. Quaint Mons. Paree
had paid rent to. Blobbs for 17 years. Wicked Mr.
McSquidge was the agent who collected the rents.
Nice Mr. Blobbs one day came to town suddenly,
and casually called on polite’ Mons. Paree, who
opened some wine in the parlor. “Mons. Blobbs,”
he said, “eet is not good of you to raise my rent
five bobs.” “Mossoo,” replied Blobbs, “I wouldn’t
dream of raising the rent on a tenant who has been
in the house since it was a little cottage.” “Mon
Dieu ! but you haf,” cried Monsieur. “Monsieur the
collector say, ‘Up your rent go five bobs.’ ” It was
gradually revealed to trusting Mr. Blobbs that
McSquidge was making ss. per week for himself as
well as his collecting commission. He explained
everything to Monsieur. “Ah, ha ! Do not tell him
of this knowledge of yours,” pleaded the Frenchman.
“Let ’im come again for five bobs. / will deal.”
Blobbs was agreeable. He discovered that other
tenants also had been swindled, and decided to .let
them deal too. All the tenants went into active
training immediately, and, a few days later, when
kind Mr. Blobbs met unhappy Mr. McSquidge - looking
like a football umpire on Sunday morning, he con-
s’derately relieved him of his rent-collecting duties
for ever.
THE PARADOX.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Vice-Admiral King-Hall said that he was struck
with the small amount of money subscribed to the
mission zone fund in Sydney. The Dean of New-
castle, referring to a deficit in the same fund, said:
“It is a shame you cannot raise £2OO in Sydney to
conquer sin,” The Rev. W. L. Langley stated that
the church within was passionless. They wanted all
the business acumen of their men brought to bear
upon Christian work l—Daily Paper.
Oh, this is very sad —-
As sad as sad can be!
We’re going to the Bad
For lack of £ s. d.
Right here, where Sin abounds.
And Satan holds full sway,
Two hundred paltry pounds
Cannot be raised to-day!
The city is defiled!
Come! bring us bags of loot!
Here Evil’s growing wild
Because we lack its root!
The vineyard’s very broad,
And things are in a mess;
Through some unholy fraud
The Church is passionless!
Ye business “Christian” men —
A (paradox, by Gosh !
Rush in and hustle, then
They’ll ante-up some splosh!
Attack the Cursed Root,
And dig it up in blocks;
Then Evil gets the boot —
Another paradox !
“We’ll give old Satan fits—-
Lord, send us heaps of pelf!”
Thus cries the Church —and it’s
A paradox itself!
N.s.w.

SEVEN AND SIX for every photo, used. The
United Commercial Travellers’ Association of
Australasia, proprietors of the National Annual,
“AUSTRALIA TO-DAY,” wish to include in their
coming Christmas issue at least 30 of the most at-
tractive portraits of Australian children (boys and
girls). They want them from EVERY STATE in
the Commonwealth, and will pay 7/6 for every
photo, used, while all others will be promptly re-
turned, post paid. Ages of children to be from
youngest babyhood up to fifteen years. The help of
ALL AUSTRALIAN PARENTS is cordially solicited
to make these pages of Australian Children WORTHY
OF AUSTRALIA. They will be circulated ALL
OVER THE WORLD. No objection to receiving
more than one photo, from same family.
Photo., accompanied by name and address of child,
and name and address of sender, must reach the

Lane, Melbourne, by September 30, 1911.

LONDON.
Among the Weary Government’s recent
visitors was a deputation from the shire of
Seymour (Vic.), bent on securing a grant
of £360. The money, it was said, was badly
wanted, as Seymour happened to be beastly
unfinancial. It was then learnt that the
shire was striking a rate of Is. in the £ on
the usual low valuation, which often makes
the nominal “bob” an actual “tanner” rate,
and out of its annual revenue was providing
£SOO towards the redemption of a loan of
£16,100. The Minister did not think it
proper to read the deputation a lecture on
self-reliance and more honest valuations. On
the contrary, he said he would consider the
demand for a grant, and bowed out the
cadgers with an affability that clearly shows
the elections are approaching.

Can I Obtain a Patent ?
The quickest, wav to ascertain this,
WITHOUT EXPENSE, is to write
to me describing your invention, enclosing a small sketch
where necessary. Nicety of drawing is not essential;
all that is necessary at first is to give me YOUR IDEA.
I will at once inform you whether the idea is patent-
able, and, if so, give the necessary instructions for
further procedure.
Persons desirous of obtaining really reliable advice or
information regarding Patent matters are cordially in-
vited to consult me personally or by letter. In all cases
a careful consideration of their plans, an honest opinion,
and a prompt reply may be expected.

BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
P Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

Children Thrive
on ANGIER’S.
There is nothing better for delicate children than a
course of Angier’s Emulsion. Bland and pleasant,
soothing alike to throat, lungs, stomach and intestines,
an aid to appetite and digestion, and a splendid tonic and
builder, it is the ideal remedy for children 's ailments, and
no mother should be without a bottle in the house. The
medical profession prescribe Angier's Emulsion not only
for coughs, bronchitis, whooping cough and all lung
affections, but also for scrofula, rickets and all wasting
diseases. Of Chemists, 1/3, 2/9 and 4/6.
“THE CHANGE IN HER IS WONDERFUL.”
8 Hampden Street, North Sydney, Aus.
Dear Sirs, —My little girl has been a constant anxiety owing-' to
loss of appetite and a very trying cough. I was so pleased to see
her relish her food after she had had a sample bottle of Angier's
that I got a large '4/6) bottle, which she has just finished.. The
change in her is wonderful; the cough is quite gone, she has put on
firm flesh and has a good colour and appetite. To have accomplished
this with a really palatable mixture is a great cause of thankfulness
to me and you have my full consent to publish this letter in case it
may persuade other mothers to try Angier's Emulsion.
(Signed) Mrs. K. WILKINS.

The Right Way to Buy Clothes.
In buying clothes, some things you can see for your-
self —color, weave, style, fit, price; your judgment for
your clothes is your best guide.
But some things you can't see —quality of fabrics,
quality of “insides,” quality of Tailoring.
Judge for yourself in the things you can see; our
Name on a garment is complete security for you in
the things you can't see. That’s why we say it s a
small thing to look for, and a big thing to find; and
that’s why it pays to look for it; and find it.

BRISBANE.
fgh
IN BANANA LAND
The proud confession of the Brisbane
Courier :
Japan, as a matter of fact, is our protector for the
next 10 years.
Then Heaven help the lamb !
Brisbane’s Water and Sewerage Board —
which doesn’t sewer worth tuppence—pro-
poses to construct another reservoir capable
of holding 5,800,000,000 gallons of water.
It will receive the water from the Cabbage-
tree Creek catchment area, which covers
28 A square miles. The proposed reservoir
will come in handy when a sewerage scheme
is incubated, especially if a dry time strikes
Bananaland. Curing the 1902 drought Bris-
bane’s tongue got very dry at times, and the
city has grown a big bit since then.
Queensland Minister for Lands Macartney
is resigning his seat for Brisbane North to
become a candidate for his old constituency,
Toowong. He lost it at the 1908 election to
It. J. Cottell, who died the other day. Tom
Welsby, who, with Philip Frankel, put up an
unsuccessful fight against Labor-leader Bow-
man and Peter McLachlan for the Forti-
tude Valley seat at the last general election,
is the Denham candidate for the Brisbane
North vacancy. J. T. Gilday, secretary of
the Federated Butchers’ Union, is opposing
Macartney, and Richard Sumner, who used
to represent Nundah, is the Labor candidate
for Brisbane North.
Denham’s party begins to wonder what
sort of a Jonah it has got hold of. In the
few months during which he has been in
charge, he has buried no less than four of
his followers.
August 21 was the fiftieth anniversary of
the first State trial in Australia —Regina v.
Pugh. Pugh was printer and publisher. or
the Brisbane Courier, and the Legislative
Council had instructed Attorney-General
Ratcliffe Pring to prosecute him for sedi-
tious libel in commenting upon certain reso-
lutions passed by the Council with reference
to Judge Lutwyche. The Attorney-General
and John Bramston, who was the next At-
torney-General,* prosecuted, and barristers
Jones and Carey, instructed by Charles
Lilley and J. F. Garrick, both of whom after-
wards became barristers and “took silk,” ap-
peared for the defence. The jury spent only
a few minutes in finding a verdict of not
guilty; and that night fireworks were let off
and bonfires were lighted in the streets of
Brisbane.
THE SUPREME TEST.
PROFESSOR BUNKUM : “I now
introduce to you Braind , the marvel-
lous man-ape. This wonderful creature , gentlemen, can perform all the most intelligent
and important tasks of daily life.'”
GIPPSIAND COCKY: “ Can he milk?”
The Inspector of Bananaland Orphanages
again breaks a lance against that venerable
inst itution, “Mother.” Theoretically (he
XX’, "»«>“ is “ instit “X;
But when the actual, cases that come unde
our notice are considered one doubts
whether the. mother is really the best person
to take charge of children. In a great num-
per 0 f State-assisted cases, I can frankly say
that the children would be better cared for
, £ ~ .
lf removed from their own mother. On t
other hand, the inspector has the highest
opinion of the foster-mothers. Their work
is worthy of great admiration. Ihe love and
affection bestowed on children by the tester
mother is beypnd the comprehension of those
who are not in touch with the work. No
wonder Mother s Day fell flat. Ihe star
of the Foster Mother was just dawning.
The righteous Denham Government has
been raiding two-up schools and betting
shops of late in a virtuous fury. The other
day Premier Denham informed the Assem-
bly that nearly 300 permits had been issued
in 12 months to churches and kindred reli-
gious institutions to hold raffles, lotteries and
art unions. “My House shall be called a
House of Prayer, but ye have made it a
Gambling Den!”
Writes the Q. Inspector of Orphanages on
a matter of grave social import: —
The State requires children, but, I think, healthy
ones. Some mothers, whose husbands were classed
as incurables, have had increases in their families,
and assistance has been paid right from the birth of
the child. One woman, in March, 1909, was re-
ceiving State assistance for four children. She in-
formed me that her husband, who had been suffering
from consumption for several years, had returned
home from the hospital. In October, 1910, she
again applied for help. Her husband was again in
the hospital much worse, but she had then six
children instead of four. Just think of it! State
funds being actually used for the propagation of
consumption !
Some 150 people of the old Pitcairn Is-
land stock feel that they want another
change—preferably to Queensland this time.
So the Banana country is arranging to form
a group settlement. The expected arrivals
are the descendants, more or less, of the
Bounty mutineers of the old whiskered year
1790. Those people went to Tahiti, and
there intermarried with the brown daugh-
ters of the island. Some stayed at Tahiti,
and were captured and more or less hanged;
nine of them, with six niggers and 12 brown
dames and demoiselles, reckoned Pitcairn
safer and moved on. On the new islet they
setlfbd down after a brief civil war, in which
the six niggers were killed, and established
a mixed race of slow, soft-spoken, contented
folk who knew so little of the world that
they scarcely knew there was a world. The
sons and djaughters of the mutiny were after-
wards shifted to Norfolk Island, but the
bustle and: strenuousness of that place was
too much for some of them, and they fled
back to the peaceful, silent fleshpots of Pit-
cairn. Now they propose to face the roar
and tumult of the tempestuous bullock dray
in Queensland. That will be bad enough,
but what a horror it would be if somebody
kidnapped a Pitcairner and deserted him in
the Strand, London, at 9 p.m. on Saturday!
The Call of the Flesh-Pots.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
bo .you hear the Flesh-pots calling in the city,
Brother Mine?
Do you hear a Voice that whispers “There is corn
and oil and wine!”
Do you hear the happy jingle of the glasses in the
bar ?
Do you hear the joy-bells tingle where the city lasses
are ?
Don’t you seem to find the Bush-land is a dreary
kind of beat?-
Don’t you fancy there’s a lush-land where they cool
your weary feet?
Don’t you dream when stars are drooping you’re a
bounder fat and fine?
Don’t you hear the Flesh-pots calling in the city,
Brother Mine?
Oil ! the Flesh is dreadful wicked —and it must be
mortified,
So the goody-goodies tell us (Moral folk who never
lied) ;
But, to judge by their dimensions in the region of the
girth
Where they keep the flesh in order there is mighty
little dearth.
It’s a mortal funny circumstance that folks who own
a lot
Are always telling poor folk: “Be content with what
you’ve got” ;
But how seldom do they clamor when they open up
the wine—
“ Come along and try the Flesh-pots and the Bottle,
Brother Mine?”
Your soul may yearn for better things, and seek the
higher plane,
But flesh and blood are mighty prone to sink to
earth again.
The manna in the wilderness may feed us many a
day,
But manna mush for twenty years is anything but
gay.
For fasting and for praying desert life’s without a
peer,
But Adam Old has yearnings that will culminate in
Beer;
When you’ve passed a quarter-century a-herding with
the swine,
You’re apt to hear the Calling of the Flesh-pots,
Brother Mine.
And the good man in the city, with his cosy feather-
bed,
Who never knew the want of place to lay his weary
head,
Who fills his precious Sabbath with the wind of
empty rant,
And lubricates his week-days with the oil of cunning
cant —
His breast is filled with horror that a Starveling
Fellow Soul
Should riot in a tumult when the bubbles kiss the
bowl.
And yet he lives but half a life who sits beneath his
vine,
In calm content, and never hears the Flesh-pots,
Brother Mine.
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 9, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Macgregor, having expressed at the
Clifton Show his satisfaction with the stock
and agricultural capabilities of the State,
to-day escorted her Leddyship to Ascot, and
listened, with rapt attention, while she de-
clared a Congregational church bazaar open
for traffic and pillage. In return for the ad-
vertisement given the show by the Presence,
a bunch of flowering vegetables was handed
up, and “Gor’save” was dolorously chanted
by Wowsers and other Bohemians.
The week’s disasters have included an
unusual number of marriages. Bertha Mor-
rison and Mr. Harry Gearing led off, and
their example was followed at intervals by
Miss Essie Degn with the aid of Mr. Hayes,
and Miss Ernestine Schureck in league with
Mr. Bennett. Each ceremony was framed
in white satin and decorated with orange
blossoms and qhampagne corks. Talking of
weddings, 1 learn that a contract will be
signed in Melb. shortly, between Dr. E. B.
Heffernan, of Hey field (Vic.), and Dr. Hilda
Greenshields, the resident medical officer of
our Lady Bowen Hospital. Dr. Hilda, a cheery,
pretty little soul whose taste in dress and
hats is beyond that which usually associates
with the letters M. 8., will leave Brisbane
for the Weary capital next month. The
noose will be tightened at the Henry Berry
homestead, Mrs. H. B. being the bride-
groom’s mother.
The Commercial Rowing Club recently
decorated its riverside club house with elec-
tric lights and lanterns, and passed round
the effusive welcome to its girl friends, who
danced and suppered joyously at its expense.
The Ladies’ Rowing Club engineered an-
other frivol an evening later, when the
claret cup, flirtation and other accessories
were given a vote of general appreciation.
Mrs. Mahlon Cowlishaw, of Sydney, and
daughter Stella, are visiting relatives here,
and teas in their honor have become a habit.
Mrs. Tom Woolnough, a niece of the Cow-
lishaw family, took a hand on Thursday,
when several of the Very Tony accepted
bath-buns and other sustenance.
The citron-face which vice-regality turns
to the press representatives at times, re-
minds me of the female half of one Governor
who, while scratching hard in the gold-dust
for the benefit of a long, thin, attenuated
charity, turned to a local reporteress and
almost pleasantly invited her to lend a hand
or its equivalent. The professional lady ex-
plained the reason of her presence, and her
inability to attend to anything but her
work. “Dear, dear,” said the Imported Per-
sonage, gazing scornfully through her eye-
panes ; “what one will do to save a shil-
ling !”
Several of our musicians have decided
that good music shall be heard. Where-
fore private concerts of the Chamber variety
are loud in the land. The latest was given
by Miss Lena Hammond (whose contralto
is not unknown to you), Miss Lucy Baker
and Mr. Percy Brier. The last-named pre-
faced each number with a short description,
which enabled an untutored Plutocracy to
appear wise in its unmusical generation.
The luck of the politician again ! On Thurs-
day evening Mr. Ryan, M.L.A., was con-
suming a quart or so of soup, when the shin-
bone of a dinosaur, or something, wedged
itself in his wind-tube. Fortunately, or un-
fortunately—you can take your choice —an-
other Parliamentarian who was present had
a knowledge of first-aid, and yanked the ob-
struction clear, in time to save Ryan from
suffocation. Life is full of trouble.
Those unfortunate individuals who
chanced to board the Van Linschoten while
it lay at Pinkenba, have been coralled, and
firmly vaccinated, by an anxious Board of
Health, The three passengers who had left
the. vessel before the small-pox scare started
to rampage, were also pounced on after an
arduous search, and now figure forlornly
among the, detainees. Rpmor hath it that
one contact, a man of ink, escaped the wrath
to come by a timely exit to the West, and
his bright home is now in the setting sun.
Arrived back in. Brisbane this week, after
many moons in the Wool Country near Bar-
caldine, “Weary” Hammond, one of Queens-
land’s most strenuous hoofs in the Rugby
field. An injury received at Barcaldine will
prevent him pursuing the jhfseyed enemy in
the future, and brutal assault on the ball
is “off.”
The “Gay Gordons” co. has moved south
again, dragging its alleged funny men in its
train; and Cigarette, Ouida’s tempestuous
little vivandidre, will die nightly in the arms
of the imperturbable and much-beloved
Bertie, till the next stage heroine elbows her
to one side.
After a hurried tour of the Highlands the
Andrew Menzies have departed, bag and
baggage, for America, where they will spend
at least two years before returning to the
Land of the Wattle.
Yours, affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

The Wine of the Barley.
FOR countless generations Ale has been a favorite beverage.
It was drunk in Egypt 3000 years before the Christian
era; it' was known and appreciated by the ancient Gauls;
it was the favorite liquor of the Anglo-Saxons and Danes, and
is still the premier drink of the most alert and active peoples of
the world. It was the drink of the lusty and strong, the “doers”
in the history of the past.
Cascade Ale is akin to the ale of old England, for it is rich
in the qualities that promote health and strength. That its merits
are appreciated by those most competent to judge is proved by
the rapidly-grov/ing sales in every part of the Commonwealth.
Sold at all first-class hotels and stores.
Cascade Ale.

The Chalmers has been
recommended to me.”
Rafter a - v ’ both ' n Sydney and Melbourne, intending purchasers come to our showrooms to
see the Chalmers men from the North of Queensland, men from Tasmania, men from all over
Australia.
Me take this condition to mean that the Chalmers car has not only now made a good reputa-
tion in our- country, but also that the Chalmers Company has succeeded in building cars that
appeal to the careful buyers here.
~ of the 46 cars we have sold since Christmas, 35 of them were Chalmers cars; out of

* SMOKE* IS TILL YOU’VE

aur\.
I'M going to have my Cigars smoked over every
part of Australasia —and the most confirmed
pipe-smokers are going to be the biggest
smokers of my Cigars, too.
Talk about joy! Why, to sit round a camp
fire, over a billy of tea, pitching 1 1 your mates,
each of you smoking one of my Cigars, joy isn’t
in it! It’s just simply bliss!
As the yarns go round, the blue smoke
curling upwards in a fragrant cloud, the
smooth cool Havana flavor lingering on your
lips—l tell you, you'll spend some of the
happiest hours of your life.
fhg
Letters are coining in to
me in shoals from all over
Australasia, praising up my
Cigars. Take one I’ve just
got as I write this, for in-
stance —it's from a chap on a
cattle station up in Northern
Queensland. He writes:—
“Those DIADEM AS Cigars
of yoursare just alright. I’ve
always smoked a pipe—but
no more. Not that I've given
up pipe-smoking altogether;
but when I want areal smoke
—something rich—l light up
one of your DIADEMAS,
and give myself up to its
pure enjoyment.”
To make you acquainted
with my Cigars, and prove
their value, I make you this
FREE OFFER of
5 or 6 CIGARS
Remit the amount, and
I will deliver promptly,
postage or carriage paid,
to any address in Aus-
tralia.
Smoke 5 op 6 of them, and if they’re
not superior to most Cigars at double the
price, send the remainder back to me, and
I’JI return every penny of your money—no
charge made for the Cigars you’ve smoked.
My Cigars are made from the very finest '
mild, rich mellow Tobacco, the filling or inside
heart of the cigar being all straight long
leaf. The Cigars are hand-made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before
it goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed
to burn freely and draw easily.
There sno “catch” about this offer—it’s just
plain and honest. To prove it, you can refer
as to my financial position and way of doing
business to the Manager of the Commercial Bank
of Australia, Collins Street, Melbourne. He’ll
vouch for me.
Send down for a box to-day—once you smoke
them you'll make it a habit, and buy regularly
from me. When ordering, say whether you
like light, medium, or dark colors.
My Only Address is

In Memoriam,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rflO those who worship cammop-sepse
I 1 our paradise is out of bounds;
JL They drudge along their dreary
rounds, and close their eyes in self-
defence ;
They shudder at our laughter shrill, O
dweller on the city wall,
Not knowing how the moonbeams fall upon
your blossomed window sill.
No outlook can their souls devise except the
road they trudge and trust;
But I have lifted from the dust mine eyes
to find your questing eyes;
Let fools seek pleasure in the gloom, and
love the gloom for what it hides—
They cannot see the light that guides my
footsteps to yo» secret room.
Beyond its casement is a star; within its
walls an atmosphere
That makes the starlight’s meaning clear, its
spirit our familiar—
Venus herself, half-jealous lest Adonis for
your lips should sigh,
Made tender by desire when I kneel at your
knee and kiss your breast.
In all the crowded thoroughfare that lies
below our place of love,
None halts to cast his eyes above, for none
suspects a throne-room there,
Where one is crowned who needs no crown,
but that the sacrifice is sweet
To lay it at her lover’s feet, and do him hom-
age kneeling down.
What though our empire may not last?
What though the sands are running out
What Time, the traitor, casts about to break
the net that Love nas cast?
No wave stays ever at its crest, nor planet
at its zenith rides:
Enough to know, whate’er betides, we
plucked the bloom while at its best.
Even as incense was consumed when men
before their goddess fell,
That in their thoughts she yet might dwell
when at strange altars still it fumed,
So shall the fragrance of the rose, since
roses blow about you now,
Recall, some day, each loving vow that struck
to music life’s dull prose.
How should I not remember you, whose soul
is my soul’s counterpart,
Whose bosom beats beneath my heart, whose
kisses thrill me through and through ;
Whose love has taught me life is true, and
not a pinchbeck thing that palls,
While life remains or love enthralls —how
should 1 not remember you?
VICTORIA.
KETTLEDRUM.
The Rescue.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
quiet of an Bdwt. mining township
-—2 O years after its discovery and
12 years after the end of its boom —
brooded over the little Westralian mining
settlement. The long, wide, windswept
stretch of main street held practically
nothing hut golden heat, galvanised iron
roofs and steel-blue distance; a few goats
held the fort on the high backbone of the
buck reef; two gentlemen of leisure squatted
before the Club Hotel and spat at the flies.
The parson’s house-roof behind its pepper
trees crackled in the sun ; three goats sneezed
by the Government dam. In the paddock on
the flats near the railway, a supercilious
camel surveyed saltbush and mulga and the
township, splitting his upper lips as he
chewed ; near the power-house a dog dozed,
rousing himself at intervals to snap at the
battening flies. Everything quiet; no wind
to make a willy-willy; a stillness oppressive.
Suddenly from a house near the dam came
an angular lady with seven clothes pegs
in her mouth. She looked at the road and
the goats and the dam, and then, appar-
ently connecting the dam and the stillness,
dropped the clothes pegs, and rushed, dis-
hevelled by fear, her face grief-distorted.
She ran to the dam, then raised her hands
to tear at her hair, and screamed in horror
and affright.
“Oooooh! . . . Oooooh! . . . Oooooh! Wil-
lie !” The goats heard her, and sneezed
again. The high blue skies to which she
looked were impotent to help. Heaven
but reverberated her prayers.
Suddenly rounding the corner of an iron
shack came the mayor and local baker, coat-
less, and driving the heavy-footed horse
which drew the bread cart, rocking over the
uneven track like a barge in a cross sea. He
drove close to the edge of the dam where the
distraught lady made the welkin ring a com-
plete peal of bells, and the heavy horse
stopped as soon as he felt the backward pull
of the rein. The terrified lady saw him,
and ran to the shafts, shrieking wildly.
“Willie, my little Willie, in the dam!”
The baker slowly descended, and she
caught his sleeve.
“Willie! Little Willie in the dam !”
“All right, mum,’’ said the baker slowly.
“Just a minit. I’m the mayor. Just a
minit while I fasten the ’orse.”
She shrieked again about Willie being in
the dam, but he very slowly and deliberately
(as was his habit) put the strap between
the wheel spokes, and carefully sought for
the customary hole in the strap. That
found, he buckled it.
Then he said: “And now what is it,
mum ?”
“Little Willie in the dam.”
The baker walked to the edge of the em-
bankment and looked over into the thick
water.
“H’m,” he said after a study of the facts.
“In there, is he?”
“Willie, my little Willie, in the dam.”
“H’m. Have you got a clothes prop?”
“In the yard. Oh, Willie! Little Willie
in the dam!”
The baker selected the yard, and
off to get the clothes prop with the gait of a
man studying ft proposition with mftny pos-
sible solutions.
Meftnwhile the welkin rang like a tire-bell,

tracted lady Oft the embapkmept- , „„ ,

asked, suavely. ....

dam ! He’s drowned.”
“My poor lady! But it may be better for
him —"better for us all.”
“Willie, Willie in the dam.”
The baker returned, with due regard to
the reposeful manner that was native to
him, dragging along an eccentrically twisted
prop of gimlet wood.
“Now, marm,” said he, “we’ll have him
out in a jiffy.” .
“Willie, my little Willie in the dam!
“My poor lady, what is his loss is our
gain!” . ~ ~ ~

baker, who had the star part, seeing that he
had the clothes prop, and wanted all the
stage. “Lemme get the prop down and rake
the bottom.”
“Willie, my little Willie in the dam!”
“My dear lady” .

A little girl ran from the yard that had

RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
A King of Kids.
THE CASTLE ROGERS RESTITUTION SYNDICATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was Saturday noon. There was a
high post-and-rail fence of sawn tim-
ber around the deep dam at Waddy.
Danny Addis was hanging most gingerly
from the top rail by his calves. Fod Been-
by, having failed in an attempt to rival this
daring feat, was contorted in a knot on the
ground hard by, chin and knees close toge-
ther, his arms embracing his head, emitting
groans of ineffable agony. Fod sought to
convey an impression that he was mortally
injured, and the end was approaching
rapidly. Stonky Elliott, Dolf Ashworth and
Did Young watched the writhings of the
dying boy with apprehension.
Pepper Elliott arrived on the scene in a
feverish state with information of great pub-
lic interest, and the sad case of the mortally-
injured acrobat was abandoned. Elliott
was breathless. He was pointing across the
flat towards the quarry-holes.
“He’s there. I seen him,” gasped the
newsbearer.
“No fear!”
“He is, I tell yeh. Seen him meself. He’s
gotter tent in the furze. Seen him with me
own eyes —him ’n’ his dog.”
There was a rush for the quarries. Been-
by, forgetting his death agonies, was well in
front. Dan Addis, in his hurry, had fallen
from the rail, and was following lamely.
Further explanation from Elliott was un-
called for, but he was gasping particulars.
The interest of the boys just now was so
centred in one personality that an announce-
ment such as Pepper had made could apply
to nobody but old Bob Rogers.
They found Rogers sitting on the edge ot
the yellow waters under the steep embank-
ments of the big quarry. By his side sat his
astonishing dog. Rogers was fishing tor
yabbies; so was the dog! At first glance
old Bob’s face looked like a bunch ot stiff,
yellowy-grey hair, in which was thrust a
short clay pipe. The dog, Percy, by his
side, by reason of his uncommon hairiness,
presented a quaint family resemblance to
the man. Percy sat stiffly erect on his hind
end, holding the string in his mouth, fishing
with unnatural gravity. He was wearing
Bob’s conical felt hat.
The boys gazed for a few moments at the
delightful spectacle, and then clattered down
the rocky ledges. Old Bob held up his pipe
to them in greeting, and every hair ot turn
crinkled into a grin. ,
“Hello ! hello ! hello !” he chortled. Here s
the boys come t’ call on Ole Bob Rogers, best
friend a boy ever had. Been knockin round
now a couple o’ hundred ’n’ ten year, I have,
’n’ ain t never met a better man than Die

Bob, more sociable, or generouser, or kinder
t’ children. Percival, where’s yer manners V
Speak t’ the boys,”
The dog turned with undiminished grav-
ity, and harked three times.
“He sez lipw’pe yell doin’?” interpreted
Bob. “Come along ’n’ I’ll give yeh lessons
how t’ catch crawfisu he moral suasion.”
The lads clustered about, kneeling on the
spawls. “Does he ever catch any?” asked
Pod, nodding towards Percy with a kind of
respect.
"Why, bless yer ’eart, iv course he does.
Does he look the Kinder dog what ’ud waste
his time pretendin'? Now, watch me. This
is how it’s done. When yeh fool the fish a-
tuggin’, you draw in gently, whistlin’ soft
’u’ low. Don't ferget the whistliu,’ that’s
the art iv it.” Boh Rogers was drawing in
his line, and he commenced to whistle, and
continued whistling seductively until a fat
yabbie was landed and throwp into an old
dish in which about a score were writhing
in holds.
“1 can catch ’em without whistlin’ —some-
times,” said Dan Addis.
“Sometimes maybe, Danny, me lad, hut
the whistlin’s most important. It mesmer-
ises ’em. ’Twas me ole mate Cocker Hobbs
what taught me that. Cocker Hobbs, the
most affectionate pirate what ever sailed
the main. Pirating for 30 year, he was, V
never once hanged a woman to the yardarm
’ceptin’ in self-defence, ’n’ alwuz said his
prayers reg’lar. I told veh ’bout Cocker.
Eight foot high, ’n’ with a beautiful, 18-carat
gold beard, long ez me arm. ’Twas him
saved me life after I was in the mouth iv a
cataleptic alligator seven days ’n’ seven
nights. That was in the Gulf Country,
when me ’n’ Cocker was runnin’ a alligator
hatchery, ’n’ sendin’ droves iv alligators
down t’ the Sydney market every month.
Cocker come back from overlandin’ seven-
undered prime fat crockerdiles, ’n’ discov-
ered me caged behind the teeth iv the big
bull alligator when ufe was near extinck.
There was I, a-breathin’ me last fer arf a
hour, while Cocker raced away fer two
pounds of the best red pepper, which he
flung up the nostrils of the hidyis fish. What
fullered?” Old Bob Rogers looked round
upon the excited and expectant youth of
Waddy. “Why, that bull alligator sneezed
somethin’ awful, V blew me clean head
over tip into Cameron’s ’aystack at Red
Rock. Five miles ez the crow flies. We
measured it with a foot-rule. ’N’ there’s
where the alligator stung me.” Rogers held
up a curiously distorted thumb as a guaran-
tee of good faith, and the horse-liair that
masked his features crinkled into a nest of
grins.
“Whew!” said Dolf. “You was glad yeh
got out, I betcher.”
“Glad, o’ course; but, bless yeh, me lad,
that ain't nothin' to old Bob Rogers, bucca-
neer onst, and” —here he looked about cauti-
ously, pressed a finger on his lip, and whis-
pered—“expert bushranger, late of Morf an’s
gang. See, I trust yeh, boys; but you’ll be
true to Ole Bob Rogers, ’n’ Ole Bob’ll be
true to you. True to death, he is, heart iv
oak, strong ez a horse, ’n’ brave ez a lion,
but lovin ’n’ kind to boys. How's that fer
Ole Bob? God bless ’im! Share his last
crust with yell, he would. Tinder ez a child
alwiz. Now for our fish dinner.” Rogers
took up the yabuies. “Pm a beggar for
fish.”
The boys followed him to his tent, neatly
nitched in the furze scrub. Before the tent
a billy of water was boiling over a burning
log. Bob dropped his yabbies into this,
threw in a handful of salt, and turned on
the boys, beaming happily.
“Now, what I suggest is a collection for
the bread ’n’ butter,” he said. “Bread ’n’
butter ’n’ hot boiled crawfish is a lunch for
a monarch. Who’s on?”
The boys turned out fourpence. Bob
turned it over in his palm with a dubious ex-
pression. “Come t’ think of it,” he said, “I
got plenty bread. S’pose one iv you lads
prigged a bit o’ butter; Ave could spend this
here on ras’berry vinegar. Lovely ras’berr.v
vinegar is with bread ’n’ butter ’n’ hot craw-
fish —nothing lovelier.”
“I can pinch a bit of butter,” said Dolf.
He fled homewards. -
“Good boy!” said Bob gaily. “Percy
brimrs the ras’berry.” Bob brought forth a
second billy, drooped the pennies in, and
the dog took charge, “Now, mind,” said the
little man, shaking an admonitory finger,
“ras’berry—three pen’orth o’ ras’berry vin-
egar. None o’ your tricks.”
Dolf was back before the dog, but Percy
arrived a few minutes later, carrying the
billy gingerly. His master seized the utensil
and lifted the lid. “Well, I’m jiggered,” he
said ; “Ole Bob Rogers is jiggered. Here” —-
he seized Percy by the scruff and pushed his
nose towards the billy. “Call that ras’-
berry? Didn’t I say ras’berry? I appeal
to the company, wasn’t it ras’berry vinegar
I said, straight ’n’ plain?” The boys all
nodded an affirmative. “There, ’ said Bob,
triumphantly, shaking the solemn dog. “It’s
no use you arguin’ ’n’ tryin’ t’ brazin’ it
out. I said ras’berry.” Bob arose with the
billy in his hand, and said with pathetic dis-
gust “The idyit’s bin ’n’ brought beer.” He
made as if to throw the stuff on the
ground. “No,” he said, “I won’t throw it
away. I know what I’ll do with it —I’ll
drink it.” He drank half of it there and
then. “Let this be a lesson to you boys,” he
said, “to bring your dogs up teetotal.”
That was a wonderful dinner. The boys
did not eat much, but Old Bob Rogers never
let the interest flag.
“B’iled crawfish is great eatin’,” he said,
“provided yeh don’t overdo it, ’n’ get craw-
fish poisonin’. I knew a bloke once what
made a hog of hisself eatin’ hot b’iled yab-
bies, ’n’ he got crawfish poisoned, ’n’ went
ereepin’ backwards like a crab fer 5 years,
’n’ nobody ’n’ nothin’ could stop him. His
family had t’ follow him up with his meals.
He was somewhere north o’ Cairns when I
last saw him, ’n’ still goin’.”
Danny Addis remembered a bloke who got
crawfish poisoning, and pinched his wife to
death in her bed.
At this point Bob Rogers arose, looked
carefully about among the furze, as if sus-
pecting* eavesdroppers, resumed his place,
and said in a low, impressive voice: —
“Boys, here’s where Ole Bob confides the
secret'of his life ’n’ the sorrer of his ’eart.
He’s got trust in boys. Boys, he sez, is
faithful ez a dog. The fact is” —lie leaned
forward, and his voice was low —“Ole Bob
Rosers is the rightful heir.” He sat back,
nodding sagely. “The rightful heir he is,
next iv kin, ’n’ lawful -wvner iv the castle
’n’ estates ’n’ all the gold ’n’ precious
stones.”
The boys were not surprised. They real-
ised that Rogers was a man from whom ex-
traordinary things were to be expected. Get
out; y’ain’t!” said Fod Beenby, with ecs-
tatic interest. ■. , .
Bob shifted nearer, bis voice dropped to
little more than a whisper. ’“lts me out
stepmother’s doin’s. A she devil n a sngfie
in the grass she is—a 'orrible woman with
eyebrows like a cat.” With a gesture he
drew the boys’ heads into a circle or awe.
“She don’t believe in llell!” he whispered
with tlie air of a man who has said the
worst. “She's took possession iv Gas tie
Rovers ’n’ all the lands n’ houses. She s
got the documents ’n’ last will ’n’ test ment
hid away in a lioak chest in the haunted
dungeon ’neath Lhe castle moat, n sties
druv ine forth ’ouseless 'u' hi me less, a wan-
derer ’u’ a houtcast, frien’less n alone.
Bob seemed to be on the point of weeping,
but pulled himself together. “Ole Bob
ain’t t’ be so easily beat homsomdever. He s
a sly one, ’n’ fierce, too, when lie’s roused.
It's'the Castle Rogers Restitootian Syndi-
kit what’s goin’ t’ do the trick. Whos the
Castle Rogers Restitootian Syndicate? sex
you. Why, you are. We're all in it, n
this is how. When Ole Bob wants faithful
follerers, ’eroes what’ll stick et nothin’,
bio ires what’s silent ez the grave, he goes fer
toys —specially ginger boys” (here Bob
looked at Addis), “ ’n’ freckled boys” (El-
liott was freckled), “ ’n’ boys with hare lips.’
Beenby had a hare lip.
The boys were too excited to eat, but Bob
had a happy knack of simultaneous masti-
cation and enunciation. The boiled yabbies,
the bread and butter, and the beer were nor
neglected.
“If yer a rigntful heir,” said Hid Young,
“you mus’ be a dook or a liearl.”
“Ssh,” whispered Bob, “walls have ears.
Now, I bet ten thousan’ pounds there ain't a
boy here wouldn’t lay down his life in the
noble ’n’ rightful cause of good Ole Bob
Rogers, libity, equality, ’n’ feternity.”
“I would,” said Dolf fervently. “I bet I
would.”
“Me too.” The boys were unanimous.
Bob nodded approvingly. “ ’Specially agin
a woman like Lady Polly Rogers, what’s a
fiend in human form, ’n’ given to drink.
Drinks like a fish, she does. Often ’n’ often
I’ve heard her say: “I’d far rather have a
glass of porter than a glass of cold water
any day.’ ’N’ she meant it, too. There’s a
she-devil for yeh.” Bob finished the beer.
“But the Castle Rogers Restitootian Syndi-
cate settles her. Three cheers for the right-
ful heir. A boy what has good instincks al-
ways stands up fer the rightful heir wher-
ever met. Him hip, hip” The cheers
were given.
Old Bob Rogers swore- in the members of
the Syndicate. The object of the Syndicate,
it was explained, was to collect funds to
send tne rightful heir to England to claim
Castle Rogers and all its contents. Each
member was expected to introduce a new
member on whose good faith and secrecy
the Syndicate could rely. All members were
to have lucrative billets as faithful retainers
at Castle Rogers when the Syndicate’s object
was gained.
“I propose fer Commander-in-Chief ’n’
honorary treasurer, dear Ole Bob Rogers,”
said the rightful heir; “honest ez the day,
strong ez a horse, ’u’ brave ez a lion. Carried
unanimous!”
Bob next beckoned the boys to follow him
to his tent. lie went cautiously, as if sus-
pecting the observation of an insidious foe.
Before entering, he scanned the horizon
under his palm. “All’s well,” he said.
Within, he pointed to a large block of blue-
stone over which he had built his tent. The
flat, projecting portion of the rock had
served him for a table, apparently. The
boys looked curiously at the rock, Bob’s
manner being mysterious and impressive.
“What is it?” wdiispered Addis.
“Hush,” whispered Bob; “this is a deadly
secret what must never be breathed to mor-
tal man or dog—it’s the sacred treasury of
the Castle Rogers Restitootian Syndicate.
Behold !” He nulled a cork-bung out of the
rock, and exposed a large drill hole. “In
there goes all our money, gold ’n’ precious
stones, w 7 here no ’uman hand can reach ’em.
That’s a fire ’n’ burglar-proof safe what’ll
baffle the hingenuity of any cowardly hemis-
saries what the hauvnty Lady Polly Rogers
might send agin us. The drill hole goes
down inter the bowells iv the earth more or
less, ’n’ with its riches stored in the him-
pregnable rock, the Syndicate can sleep in
peace. Who gives the fund a start, sez you.
Why. Ole Bob Rogers—Ole Bob gives his
last penny.” Bob took a penny from his
pocket, ajid dropped it into the drill hole.
“Ole Bob subscribes the family jools to the
noble cause.” With dramatic ostentation
Bob contributed a bone stud, a brass ring,
and a precious stone of cut glass as large as
a pigeon’s egg, the Castle Rogers diamond,
he said, and worth a matter of ten pounds.
The boys contributed what trifles of money
they had, and those who had no cash under-
tooK to provide something on Sunday, if
only the church nennies dedicated to the
heathen.
So the Syndicate went resolutely to work.
Many pennies and threepenny-bits dribbled
into the rock-bound vault. After school
hours the boys went resolutely to work on
Old Bob’s stone heaps on the Murrumbeet-
road, and cracked more metal in a couple of
hours than the Rightful Heir could break in
two days. The understanding was that these
surplus earnings went into the fund of the
Restitution Syndicate.
But it was on Saturday that the heroic
boys got in their most effective service. Then
tubs and cradles were carried to Pie Creek,
and they toiled all day long puddling,
cradling, and panning off. Bob did no fos-
sicking himself, but he sang roaring sea-
chanties that had a wonderfully-stimulating
effect upon the working partners. He sang
sitting on a tip, whirling a billy of beer.
You could get a little gold in every dish of
dirt along Pie Creek for a mile or more, and
the Saturday’s work was fairly profitable.
The gold, tied in chamois leather, was cere-
moniously dropped into the Syndicate’s
rocky vault, and the boys of Waddy, wet
and weary, but proudly conscious of good
service rendered to a worthy cause, w r ent
home.
A little later, Old Bob Rogers stole to the
back of his tent, and scraped some dirt from
the under edge of the rock, exposing what
looked like a rabbit burrow. Pulling a
string, he drew out of this a small fig box.
The box contained sundry coins and a couple
of weights of void tied in chamois. These
valuables Old Bob pocketed.
The drill hole went right through the
rock. The fig box was the treasury of the
Castle Rogers Restitution Syndicate.
EDWARD DYSON.
